Theological Application and Author's Resolution through Intermediaries
Author resolves a theological counterexample using intermediary analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the chain of entailments from a possible effect to the Necessary Being and explain how a possible can entail an impossible through intermediaries.

Core Idea
A possible entails an impossible by essence when the chain of intermediaries includes an impossible or when a possible intermediary itself entails an impossible.

The author applies the principle to the relationship between a possible effect (like the world) and the Necessary Being (God). If every possible is an effect of the Necessary, then the possible non-existence of the effect seems to entail the impossible non-existence of God. The author rejects inadequate responses and provides a refined analysis involving intermediaries.

Guided Path
1
State the theological dilemma
If every possible effect of the Necessary Being is possible, then its non-existence would entail the impossible non-existence of the Necessary. This seems to violate the principle.
The dilemma is: either the effect is not truly possible, or the principle is false.
2
Analyze the first response and its refutation
One response says the non-existence of the effect only negates God's causality, not His essence. But God's causality is necessary, so its negation is impossible. Moreover, the negation of causality entails negation of the cause, leading to the impossible.
The author calls denying this 'obstinacy'.
3
Examine the second response and its problem
Another response says the non-existence of the effect entails non-existence of the cause only due to the necessity of the Necessary, not in itself. But the author shows that the attribute of effect-hood necessitates causality, which necessitates the existence of the cause; thus the chain is essential.
4
Understand the author's resolution with intermediaries
The author argues that the non-existence of the effect is a 'necessitator' of a chain: effect-hood -> causality -> non-existence of the Necessary. The intermediaries (like causality) are either impossible or possible. In both cases, the original possible entails an impossible by essence.
Intermediaries are only intermediaries in subsistence, not in essential entailment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنه قد اشتهر هذه المقدمة، واستدل عليها بأنه لو استلزم الممكن محالا بالذات لأمكن انفكاك الملزوم عن اللازم؛ لأن الممكن الملزوم غير آب عن قبول الوجود لإمكانه، والمحال اللازم أباه. ويرد عليه ورودا ظاهرا أن الانفكاك إنما يلزم لو كان اللازم محالا بالنظر إلى الملزوم، وهو ممنوع، واستحالة الوجود بالذات لا تنافي الإمكان بالنظر إلى الغير، بل الوجوب بالنظر إليه. وأيضا لو كان الأمر كما هو المشهور، لما جاز كون كل ممكن معلولا للواجب، وإلا فعدمه الممكن يستلزم محالا بالذات، وهو عدم الواجب. وأجيب تارة بأن عدم المعلول إنما يستلزم عدم علية الواجب، لا عدم ذاته إلا بالعرض، ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ فإن جاعليته تعالى ضرورية له، وعدمها محال؛ لأن الواجب واجب مع جميع جهاته، على أن عدم معلوله تعالى يستلزم عدم العلية، وهو يستلزم عدم ذاته تعالى عنه؛ لأن انتفاء اللازم يستلزم انتفاء الملزوم. وأيضا يستلزم عدم العلية عدم العلة فهو مستلزم له، وإنكاره مكابرة. وتارة بأن عدم المعلول إنما يستلزم عدم العلة؛ لامتناعه بالواجب، أما بالنظر إلى ذاته فكلا، وإنما يحكم باستحالة المستلزم للمحال إن كان بالنظر إلى ذاته. ولا يخفى أن عدم المعلول ملزوم لوصف المعلولية، وهي ملزوم جاعلية عدم الواجب، وهي ملزومة لنفس العدم؛ لأن وصف الجاعلية ضروري لما هو جاعل، كما نص عليه الشيخ، فلزم أن يكون عدم المعلول الممكن ملزوما لعدم الواجب المحال. فإن قلت: فعلى هذا لا يكون المحال لازما للممكن بالذات، بل بالعرض، ولا ضير فيه؟ قلت: لا، بل بالذات إنما الوسائط وسائط في الثبوت؛ على ما يشهد به الضرورة، وأيضا شيء من الوسائط محال بالذات، أو كلها ممكنات، وعلى كلا التقديرين استلزام الممكن المحال. أما على الأول: فلأن الممكن يستلزم الوسائط المحالة، وأما على الثاني: فلأن الواسطة الممكنة استلزمت محالا.
Know that this premise has become famous, and it was argued for by [the claim that] if the possible entailed the impossible by essence, then the entailed could be separated from the entailer; because the possible entailer is not averse to receiving existence due to its possibility, while the impossible entailer refuses it. But it is objected to in an apparent way that separation would only be necessary if the impossible were [impossible] with respect to the entailer, which is denied; and the impossibility of existence by essence does not contradict possibility with respect to another, rather it necessitates [existence] with respect to that other. Also, if the matter were as it is famous, then it would not be permissible for every possible to be an effect of the Necessary, otherwise its possible non-existence would entail an impossible by essence, namely the non-existence of the Necessary. And it was answered sometimes that the non-existence of the effect only entails the non-existence of the causality of the Necessary, not His essence except accidentally, and it is not hidden what is in this; for His making [things] is necessary for Him, and its non-existence is impossible; because the Necessary is necessary in all His aspects. Moreover, the non-existence of His effect entails the non-existence of causality, which entails the non-existence of His essence, may He be exalted; because the negation of the necessary entails the negation of the necessitated. Also, the non-existence of causality entails the non-existence of the cause, so it is entailed by it, and denying it is obstinacy. And [it was answered] other times that the non-existence of the effect only entails the non-existence of the cause due to its impossibility by the Necessary; but with respect to itself, not at all; and the judgment of the impossibility of that which entails an impossible is only if it is with respect to itself. And it is not hidden that the non-existence of the effect is necessitator of the attribute of effect-hood, which is necessitator of the making [causality] of the non-existence of the Necessary, which is necessitator of non-existence itself; because the attribute of making is necessary for that which is a maker, as the Shaykh stated. So it follows that the non-existence of the possible effect is necessitator of the non-existence of the Necessary, which is impossible. If you say: then according to this, the impossible is not necessary for the possible by essence, but accidentally, and there is no harm in that? I say: No, rather by essence. The intermediaries are only intermediaries in subsistence, as necessity testifies. Also, some intermediaries are impossible by essence, or all are possible. On both assumptions, the possible entails the impossible. As for the first: because the possible entails the impossible intermediaries. As for the second: because the possible intermediary entailed an impossible.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a defence and refinement of a well-known logical principle: 'If a possible thing entailed an impossible thing by essence, then it would itself be impossible.' The author begins by presenting the famous argument: if a possible (e.g., the world) entailed an impossible (e.g., a self-contradictory state), then it would be possible to separate the entailer from the entailed, because the possible is receptive to existence while the impossible is not. But an objection is raised: separation only follows if the entailed is impossible relative to the entailer, which is denied. Impossibility by essence does not negate possibility with respect to something else; rather it entails necessity with respect to that something else. Then the author adds a theological case: if every possible were an effect of the Necessary, then the possible non-existence of an effect would entail the impossible non-existence of the Necessary. Two responses are given. The first response says the non-existence of the effect only entails the non-existence of God's causality, not His essence, except accidentally. The author criticizes this: God's attribute of causality is necessary for Him, so its non-existence is impossible. Moreover, the absence of causality entails the absence of the cause, which would be the non-existence of God. The second response says the non-existence of the effect only entails the non-existence of the cause due to the impossibility relative to the Necessary; but with respect to itself, it does not. The author then argues that the non-existence of the effect is the necessitator of the attribute of effect-hood, which necessitates the attribute of making (causality) of the non-existence of the Necessary, which itself necessitates non-existence. Thus the non-existence of the possible effect necessitates the impossible non-existence of the Necessary. The objection that the impossible is only entailed accidentally is rebutted: the intermediaries are only intermediaries in subsistence, and they are either impossible or possible. In both cases, the possible entails the impossible.

Key Terms
الممكن
المحال بالذات
الاستلزام
العلية
الملزوم واللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the non-existence of a possible effect entail the impossible non-existence of the Necessary?
A) Because the effect is necessary for the Necessary.
B) Because the chain of entailments includes an attribute of the maker, which is necessary, thus leading to the impossible.
C) Because the Non-existence of the effect is identical to the non-existence of the Necessary.
D) Because the effect is impossible in itself.

Key Takeaway
A possible can entail an impossible by essence when the chain of intermediaries includes an impossible or when a possible intermediary itself entails an impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Trace the chain of entailments from the non-existence of a contingent effect to the non-existence of its necessary cause, using the author's model. Identify each link and state whether it is possible or impossible, and explain why the overall entailment is essential.
?
Construct a similar chain for a different theological example: the possibility of divine forgiveness does not entail the impossibility of divine justice. Show how intermediaries resolve the apparent conflict.

Objection and Response on Division of the Continuous
Objection and refutation on division of continuous bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection against the continuity proof and evaluate the author's refutation based on necessary truths.

Core Idea
Division does not create new natures; what follows must share the original essence.

In this passage, the author defends the proof that a continuous body cannot dissolve into different essences. An objector claims division destroys the original and creates entirely new bodies with different natures. The author counters that this is a necessary truth—what results after division cannot differ in essence from the whole, as absurd as water dividing into fire.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
Read the first part of the Arabic: the objector asserts that division destroys the continuous and generates two new bodies, which could be dissimilar from each other and from the whole.
The objector distinguishes between dissolution and destruction-and-creation.
2
Identify the author's response
The author rejects the possibility by claiming it is a necessary truth that the result after division cannot differ in essence from the original. He gives an example: dividing water cannot produce fire.
The author appeals to 'al-ḍarūriyyāt' (necessary truths).
3
Understand the force of the example
The example of water and fire illustrates that the essence of the resultant parts must be the same as the original. If dividing water could give fire, that would be absurd.
This shows the author's reliance on intuitive impossibility.
4
Evaluate the refutation
Consider whether the author's appeal to necessary truth is sufficient to refute the objector's scenario of destruction-and-creation. Does the example prove that the objector's scenario is impossible?
The author accuses the objector of stubbornness, indicating he sees the refutation as decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض عليه بأنهم اتفقوا على أن القسمة تعدم المتصل ويحدث متصلين آخرين فيجوز حدوث المتصلين المتخالفين في أنفسهما المخالفين للكل، وما ذكر من عدم انحلال المتصل إلى مختلفين مسلم، لكن لا انحلال ههنا ، بل إعدام وإيجاد. والحق أن من الضروريات أن الحاصل بعد الانقسام لا يكون مخالفا للكل في الحقيقة، وإن كان حاصلا من كتم العدم وكيف يجوز عاقل حدوث النارين بعد قسمة الماء المتصل ، وبأنا لا نسلم عدم جواز انحلال المتصل إلى المتخالفين في الحقيقة، وكيف يسلم من يجوز تخالف الأشخاص في الحقيقة ، وأظن أن هذا مكابرة جلية ؛ لما مر .
And it was objected against him that they agreed that division destroys the continuous and generates two other continuous bodies, so it is possible for the two generated continuous bodies to be dissimilar in themselves and different from the whole. And what was mentioned about the continuous not dissolving into different things is accepted, but there is no dissolution here; rather it is destruction and creation. And the truth is that it is among necessary things that what results after division cannot be different from the whole in reality, even if it results from the concealment of non-existence. And how could a rational person allow the occurrence of two fires after the division of a continuous water? And we do not grant the impossibility of the continuous dissolving into things differing in reality. And how can one who allows the difference of individuals in reality concede? And I think this is evident stubbornness; due to what has passed.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the third proof and its refutation. The objector says: scholars agree that division destroys the continuous and creates two new continuous bodies; thus these two new bodies could be different from each other and from the original whole. The original proof claimed that a continuous cannot dissolve into different essences, but the objector says it is not dissolution—it is destruction and new creation. The author responds: It is a necessary truth that what results after division cannot be essentially different from the whole, even if it emerges from non-being. For example, dividing water cannot produce fire. The author further rejects the assumption that the continuous cannot dissolve into different essences, and accuses the objector of stubbornness.

Key Terms
القسمة
إعدام وإيجاد
الضروريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to refute the objection that divided parts can differ in essence?
A) Dividing fire produces water
B) Dividing water produces fire
C) Dividing a human produces a horse
D) Dividing a stone produces air

Key Takeaway
The author defends the continuity proof by asserting that what results from division necessarily shares the essence of the original, making the objection of differing natures impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar objection to another proof in the same book: if a continuous is divided into parts that are created anew, could those parts have different accidents? Use the same pattern of destruction and creation.
?
Identify whether the author's refutation relies on empirical observation (e.g., water never turns into fire) or rational necessity (self-evident truth). Explain your reasoning.

Refuting al-Razi's Doubt on Definition: Identity in Essence, Difference in Consideration
Refutes al-Razi's objection that definition is either tautology or inadequate via 'arad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Razi's objection against definition and explain the refutation using the distinction between identity in essence and difference in consideration.

Core Idea
Definition gives new knowledge because it is the same essence considered differently.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi posed a powerful objection: how can a definition tell us anything new? If it is the same as the defined, it is redundant; if it uses accidents, it cannot convey the essence. This lesson unpacks the refutation: definition is identical in essence but differs in 'i'tibar (mode of consideration).

Guided Path
1
State al-Razi's dilemma
Understand the two horns: either the definition is the same as the defined (tahsil al-hasil) or it uses accidents, which cannot convey the essence. Both lead to the conclusion that definition is impossible.
2
Identify the refutation's key move
The author chooses the first horn but adds that the definition is the same in essence (bi al-dhat) yet different in consideration (bi al-i'tibar). Thus, it is not a mere repetition.
3
Explain the concept of i'tibar
I'tibar means the definition provides a detailed analysis of the parts, while the defined is known summarily. The same reality is considered from different perspectives, yielding new knowledge.
This distinction is common in post-classical logic.
4
Connect to the conclusion
This resolves al-Razi's objection and also shows that his extreme view (all conceptualizations are self-evident) is unnecessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَانْدَفَعَ شَكُ الإمام الهمام الرازي مِنْ أَنَّ تَعْرِيفَ الْمَاهِيَّةِ إِمَّا بِنَفْسِهَا أَوْ بِجَمِيعِ أَجْزَائِهَا، وَهُوَ نَفْسُهَا، فَالتَّعْرِيفُ تَحْصِيلُ الْحَاصِلِ، وَهُوَ بَاطِلٌ، أَوْ بِالْعَوَارِضِ، وَلَا عِلْمَ بِالْحَقِيقَةِ إِلَّا الْعِلْمُ بِالْكُنْهِ، وَالْعَوَارِضُ لَا تُعْطِيهِ، فَقَدْ بَطَلَ التَّعْرِيفُ بِالْعَوَارِضِ، فَالْأَقْسَامُ أَيْ أَقْسَامُ التَّعْرِيفِ الَّتِي هِيَ الْحَدُّ وَالرَّسْمُ بِأَسْرِهَا بَاطِلَةٌ. وَجْهُ الدَّفْعِ: أَنَا نَخْتَارُ أَنَّ التَّعْرِيفَ بِجَمِيعِ أَجْزَائِهَا، وَهُوَ نَفْسُهَا بِالذَّاتِ مُغَايِرٌ بِالِاعْتِبَارِ، فَتَحْصِيلُهَا تَحْصِيلُ غَيْرِ الْحَاصِلِ. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا ذَهَبَ الْإِمَامُ إِلَى بَدَاهَةِ التَّصَوُّرَاتِ كُلِّهَا.
Then the doubt of Imam al-Razi is repelled: that the definition of the quiddity is either by itself or by all its parts, and it is itself, so the definition is the attainment of what is already attained, and that is false; or by accidents, and there is no knowledge of the reality except knowledge of the essence, and accidents do not give it, so definition by accidents is false; thus the divisions, i.e., the divisions of definition which are the definition proper and the description, are all false. The face of the repulsion: We choose that the definition by all its parts, and it is itself in essence, is different in consideration, so attaining it is attaining what is not [already] attained. And from here the Imam went to the position that all conceptualizations are self-evident.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage refutes the famous objection of Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi against the possibility of definition (hadd). Al-Razi argued that either the definition is the same as the defined, making it redundant (tahsil al-hasil), or it uses accidental properties, which cannot convey the essence (kunh). Therefore, all definitions—whether hadd or rasm—are invalid. The author replies that the definition is indeed identical to the defined in essence but differs in consideration (i'tibar): the definition provides a detailed analysis of the parts, whereas the defined is an integrated whole known summarily. Hence, the definition yields new knowledge, not a mere repetition. This leads al-Razi himself to the extreme view that all conceptualizations are self-evident, but the author shows that this is unnecessary once the distinction by consideration is accepted.

Key Terms
الحد
الرسم
تحصيل الحاصل
بالاعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction that resolves al-Razi's objection that definition is tahsil al-hasil?
A) The definition is composed of separate parts while the defined is a simple essence.
B) The definition and the defined are identical in essence but differ in consideration (i'tibar).
C) The definition is a mental construct while the defined is external.
D) The definition uses only accidents and never the essence.

Key Takeaway
A definition gives new knowledge because it presents the same essence in a detailed mode of consideration, avoiding tautology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Apply the distinction of i'tibar to a definition from fiqh, e.g., the definition of 'sawm' (fasting). Explain how the definition is identical in essence to the act of fasting but provides new knowledge by listing its conditions and pillars.
?
Identify another example from kalam (e.g., definition of 'ilm) and show how the i'tibar distinction prevents the charge of tahsil al-hasil.

The Problem of Rasm: Can Description Yield New Knowledge?
Discusses the problem of rasm providing new knowledge and states the nature of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why rasm is problematic for acquiring new knowledge and how the integrated summary image is proposed as a solution.

Core Idea
Without a new image, rasm fails; the composite image saves it but only for distinction.

In this lesson, we examine a key issue in Islamic logic: the difference between hadd (definition) and rasm (description). The author of our passage argues that rasm is problematic because the mind may not generate a new image of the described object distinct from the description itself. If no new image arises, no unknown knowledge is obtained. However, a solution is offered: the integrated summary image composed of accidents provides a distinguishing mark. This lesson will help you understand the epistemological challenge of rasm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contrast between hadd and rasm
The author says all the previous discussion was about hadd; now rasm is problematic. What is the basic difference? Hadd uses essence, rasm uses accidents.
2
Pinpoint the problem
The mind 'shrinks from obtaining an image' different from the description. If no new image, then rasm fails to convey unknown knowledge. Why is that a problem? Because logic aims to move from known to unknown.
3
Find the proposed solution
The author mentions that the majority allow rasm to give new knowledge. Then he provides a possible resolution: the 'integrated summary image' (surah wahdaniyyah mujmalah) is obtained after the description, composed of accidents, and provides distinction.
4
Assess the solution
Does this solution save rasm? It says the composite image gives distinction from others. But is that enough for knowledge? Note that this is not a full essence, but a distinguishing mark.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا كُلُّهُ فِي التَّحْدِيدِ، وَأَمَّا فِي الرَّسْمِ فَالْأَمْرُ مُشْكِلٌ؛ لِأَنَّ صُورَةَ الْمَرْسُومِ الْمُغَايِرَةَ لِصُورَةِ الرَّسْمِ يَنْقَبِضُ عَنْ حُصُولِهَا الْعَقْلُ، وَإِنْ جَوَّزَهُ الْأَكْثَرُونَ؛ إِذْ لَا يَبْقَى إِذَنْ فَرْقٌ بَيْنَ التَّحْدِيدِ وَالتَّرْسِيمِ، وَإِذَا لَمْ يَحْصُلْ صُورَةٌ أُخْرَى لَمْ يَتَحَقَّقْ حُصُولُ مَجْهُولٍ، وَلَا الْحَرَكَةُ الثَّانِيَةُ، إِلَّا أَنْ يُقَالَ: الصُّورَةُ الْوَحْدَانِيَّةُ الْمُجْمَلَةُ الْمُرَكَّبَةُ مِنَ الْعَوَارِضِ تَحْصُلُ بَعْدَ حُصُولِ الرَّسْمِ، وَتُفِيدُ الِامْتِيَازَ عَمَّا عَدَاهُ. ثُمَّ إِنَّ التَّحْقِيقَ أَنَّ حَقِيقَةَ الْعِلْمِ مُغَايِرَةٌ لِلْمَعْلُومِ بِالذَّاتِ، عَلَى مَا يَرَاهُ أَصْحَابُ الشَّبَحِ، وَتَحْتَهُ نَوْعَانِ تَصَوُّرٌ وَتَصْدِيقٌ، وَالتَّصْدِيقُ مِنْهُ تَامٌّ.
All this is concerning definition proper (hadd). As for description (rasm), the matter is problematic: because the mind shrinks from obtaining an image of the described that is different from the image of the description, even though the majority allow it; since then no difference remains between definition proper and description. And if no other image is obtained, then no unknown is attained, nor a second movement [of thought]—unless it is said: the integrated summary image composed of accidents is obtained after the description is obtained, and it provides distinction from what is other than it. Then the reality is that the reality of knowledge is essentially different from the known, according to the view of the adherents of the phantom, and under it are two types: concept and assent, and assent from it is complete.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage addresses the difficulty with rasm (description). Unlike hadd, which yields a new detailed image, rasm relies on accidents; the mind may not produce a new image of the thing described distinct from the description itself. If no new image arises, then rasm fails to convey unknown knowledge and there is no second intellectual movement from principle to conclusion. The author notes a possible resolution: the composite summary image (the whole) is obtained after the description, serving to distinguish the thing from others. The passage then transitions to the nature of knowledge itself, asserting that knowledge is essentially distinct from the known (as held by the 'ashab al-shabah, the philosophers who posit a mental form). Knowledge is divided into concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq), with the latter being complete (full assent).

Key Terms

الصورة الوحدانية المجملة

The Nature of Knowledge: Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Discusses the nature of knowledge as distinct from the known and its division into concept and assent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two components of knowledge (tasawwur and tasdiq) and explain the view that knowledge is distinct from the known.

Core Idea
Knowledge is essentially separate from the known and consists of concept and assent.

In the final part of the passage, the author turns to the nature of knowledge itself. He affirms the view of the 'ashab al-shabah (adherents of the phantom) that knowledge is essentially different from the known object. Then he divides knowledge into two types: tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent). This division is fundamental in Islamic logic and epistemology. This lesson will help you grasp this foundational classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about the nature of knowledge
The author states 'the reality of knowledge is essentially different from the known.' That is the shabah view. What does that mean? The mental representation (shabah) is not the same as the external object.
2
Find the division of knowledge
Under knowledge, there are two types: tasawwur and tasdiq. What are these? Tasawwur is the simple conception of something without judgment; tasdiq is the judgment of truth or falsity.
3
Note the qualification on tasdiq
The author says 'assent from it is complete' (al-tasdiq minhu tamm). That means full or complete assent, which is certainty. Why is this important? Because not all assent is complete; some are probabilistic.
4
Connect to earlier discussion
This classification is the basis for all further logic: definitions deal with tasawwurat, syllogisms with tasdiqat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا كُلُّهُ فِي التَّحْدِيدِ، وَأَمَّا فِي الرَّسْمِ فَالْأَمْرُ مُشْكِلٌ؛ لِأَنَّ صُورَةَ الْمَرْسُومِ الْمُغَايِرَةَ لِصُورَةِ الرَّسْمِ يَنْقَبِضُ عَنْ حُصُولِهَا الْعَقْلُ، وَإِنْ جَوَّزَهُ الْأَكْثَرُونَ؛ إِذْ لَا يَبْقَى إِذَنْ فَرْقٌ بَيْنَ التَّحْدِيدِ وَالتَّرْسِيمِ، وَإِذَا لَمْ يَحْصُلْ صُورَةٌ أُخْرَى لَمْ يَتَحَقَّقْ حُصُولُ مَجْهُولٍ، وَلَا الْحَرَكَةُ الثَّانِيَةُ، إِلَّا أَنْ يُقَالَ: الصُّورَةُ الْوَحْدَانِيَّةُ الْمُجْمَلَةُ الْمُرَكَّبَةُ مِنَ الْعَوَارِضِ تَحْصُلُ بَعْدَ حُصُولِ الرَّسْمِ، وَتُفِيدُ الِامْتِيَازَ عَمَّا عَدَاهُ. ثُمَّ إِنَّ التَّحْقِيقَ أَنَّ حَقِيقَةَ الْعِلْمِ مُغَايِرَةٌ لِلْمَعْلُومِ بِالذَّاتِ، عَلَى مَا يَرَاهُ أَصْحَابُ الشَّبَحِ، وَتَحْتَهُ نَوْعَانِ تَصَوُّرٌ وَتَصْدِيقٌ، وَالتَّصْدِيقُ مِنْهُ تَامٌّ.
All this is concerning definition proper (hadd). As for description (rasm), the matter is problematic: because the mind shrinks from obtaining an image of the described that is different from the image of the description, even though the majority allow it; since then no difference remains between definition proper and description. And if no other image is obtained, then no unknown is attained, nor a second movement [of thought]—unless it is said: the integrated summary image composed of accidents is obtained after the description is obtained, and it provides distinction from what is other than it. Then the reality is that the reality of knowledge is essentially different from the known, according to the view of the adherents of the phantom, and under it are two types: concept and assent, and assent from it is complete.

The Predicate of Conditionals: Relation, Not Consequent
Defense of logicians' predicate view in conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the logicians' position that the predicate of a conditional is the entire conditional relation, using the example of 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray' to illustrate the point.

Core Idea
The truth of a conditional lies in the entailment, not the actuality of the consequent.

In this passage, the author defends the logicians' view that in a conditional proposition, the predicate (khabar) is the entire conditional relation between antecedent and consequent, not just the consequent alone. This matters for how we evaluate the truth of conditionals in theology and law. We will unpack the central arguments and responses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The grammarians treat the consequent as the predicate of a conditional, but logicians argue the predicate is the entire relation. Recognize this as the core disagreement.
The passage begins by stating that the dispute can occur about whether the 'creational relation' is between antecedent and consequent or in the consequent alone.
2
Understand al-Sayyid's proof
Al-Sayyid argues that if the predicate were the consequent, the conditional could not be true when the consequent is false. He gives the example: 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray' is true, yet Zayd never actually brays. Therefore, the predicate must be the relation.
He uses a modus tollens argument: if predicate = consequent, then true conditional requires true consequent; but we have true conditional with false consequent; therefore predicate ≠ consequent.
3
Analyze al-Dawani's refinement
Al-Dawani introduces the concept of 'estimative times' (al-awqat al-taqdiriyya). The consequent is true in all times where the antecedent is hypothetically true, even if false in actual times. Thus, the conditional is true because in all estimated donkeyhood times, Zayd brays.
This preserves the truth of the conditional without requiring the consequent to be actually true.
4
Evaluate the objection and response
An objection says that in a proposition like 'Zayd is standing in my supposition', the predicate is 'standing in my supposition', not 'standing'. But al-Dawani dismisses this as a weak analogy because brayingness is not a mental attribute; it is a real property that would hold if the antecedent were true.
The distinction is between actual and hypothetical conditions, not between mental and real predicates.
5
Synthesize the final position
The later scholar's remark on categorical propositions reinforces the point: the import of a proposition is the affirmation of the predicate for the subject in reality, but 'reality' here includes the hypothetical reality of the conditional. Thus, the absolute absolute (mutlaq) that is negated is not the same as the one in the conditional; the conditional's absolute is broader, covering estimated worlds.
This shows the logical depth: conditionals quantify over possible worlds or estimated states, not just actuality.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

مردود بالصواب.
(قَالَ السَّيِّدُ [الْمُحَقِّقُ ] قُدَّسَ سِرُّهُ) الشريف: (الْأَوَّلُ)، أي قول أهل المنطق هُوَ الْحَقُّ ، لِلْقَطْع بِصِدْقِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ مَعَ كَذِبِ التَّالِي فِي الْوَاقِع ، كَقَوْلِنَا إِنْ كَانَ زَيْدٌ حِمَارًا كَانَ نَاهِقًا ، وَلَوْ كَانَ الْخَبَرُ هُوَ التَّالِي لَمْ يُتَصَوَّرْ صِدْقُهَا)، أي الشرطية (مَعَ كَذِبِهِ)، أي التالي ؛ ضَرُورَةَ اسْتِلْزَامِ انْتِفَاءِ الْمُطْلَقِ انْتِفَاءَ الْمُقَيَّدِ) ؛ فإنه مشتمل على المطلق، وتجويز منافاة القيد للمطلق قول بالجمع بين النقيضين. ويقرب منه أنها تصدق مع كذب المقدم، ولو كان الخبر هو التالي لما أمكن ذلك ؛ ضرورة انتفاء المقيد بانتفاء القيد.
(قَالَ الْعَلَّامَةُ الدَّوَّانِي رحمه الله: كَذِبُ التَّالِي فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَوْقَات [ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْهُ كَذِبُهُ فِي الْأَوْقَاتِ التَّقْدِيرِيَّةِ، فَالنَّاهِقِيَّةُ فِي جَمِيع أَوْقَاتٍ قُدِّرَ فِيهَا حِمَارِيَّةُ زَيْدٍ ثَابِتَةٌ لَهُ ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ بِحَسَبِ الْأَوْقَاتِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ مَسْلُوبَةَ عَنْهُ ، أَلَا تَرَى [أَنَّ] زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ في ظَنّي لَمْ يَكْذِبْ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْقِيَام في الواقع .
وما قيل : المحكوم به في تلك القضية مظنون القيام لا قائم، فإذن إنما يكذب بانتفاء مظنونية القيام بخلاف الناهقية، فنداء من بعيد على ما لا يخفى
وَمَا ذُكِرَ مِنَ الِاسْتِلْزَامِ بين انتفاء المقيد وانتفاء المطلق فَمُسَلَّمْ، لَكِنْ لا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ الْمُطْلَقَ هَهُنَا مُنْتَفٍ ، فَإِنَّهُ الْمَأْخُوذُ عَلَى وَجْهِ أَعَمَّ مِمَّا فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ ، بحيث يشمل عالم التقدير أَيْضًا، غَايَةُ مَا يُقَالُ: إِنَّ الْعِبَارَةَ غَيْرُ مَوْضُوعَةٍ لِتَأْدِيَةِ ذَلِكَ الْمَعْنَى المطلق بانفراده مُطَابَقَةٌ، وَلَا ضَيْرَ فِيهِ. بل يدل في ضمن المقيد.
قال بعض أجلة المتأخرين مفاد القضية الحملية، سواء كانت مطلقة أو مقيدة ، ثبوت المحمول للموضوع في الواقع، لا مطلق الثبوت، وإلا لم تكن كاذبة بانتفائه عن نفس الأمر ؛ ضرورة أن انتفاء المقيد لا يستلزم انتفاء المطلق، فلو فرضنا عدم تحقق المطلق في الواقع يلزم عدم تحقق المقيد
It is possible to dispute also about whether the creational relation is between the condition and the consequent or in the consequent alone. Then the truth is what al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq said—may his secret be sanctified—that there is no dispute at all; for the grammarians also say that the judgment is between them, not in the consequent. How so, when they say that the conditional particle is placed for the causality of the first and the effectuality of the second. And the statement of the author of al-Miftah is either interpretable [or...] and supports it is what he said in Daw' al-Misbah that the terms of the conditional have departed from being a sentence beneficial for silence upon it. And the saying is his, and God knows best. It is refuted by the correct. (Al-Sayyid [al-Muhaqqiq]—may his secret be sanctified—said: The first, meaning the statement of the logicians, is the truth, because of the certainty of the truth of the conditional while the consequent is false in reality, like our saying 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray.' And if the predicate were the consequent, the conditional could not be conceived to be true while the consequent is false, necessarily because the negation of the absolute necessitates the negation of the qualified; for it includes the absolute, and allowing the qualification to contradict the absolute is a statement combining two contradictories. And close to it is that it is true while the antecedent is false, and if the predicate were the consequent, that would not be possible, necessarily because of the negation of the qualified by the negation of the qualification. (Al-'Allama al-Dawani—may God have mercy on him—said: The falsity of the consequent in all actual times does not entail its falsity in estimative times. For 'brayingness' in all times in which Zayd's donkeyhood is estimated is established for him, even if according to actual times it is stripped from him. Do you not see that 'Zayd is standing in my supposition' is not made false by the absence of standing in reality. And what is said: the judged thing in that proposition is 'that which is supposed standing', not 'standing', so then it is only made false by the absence of the supposition of standing, unlike brayingness, so it is a far cry, as is not hidden. And what was mentioned of the entailment between the negation of the qualified and the negation of the absolute is conceded, but we do not concede that the absolute here is negated, for it is taken in a more general way than what is in actuality, such that it includes the world of estimation as well. At most what is said is that the expression is not placed to convey that absolute meaning alone by correspondence, and there is no harm in that. Rather it indicates within the qualified. (Some of the later eminent scholars said: The import of a categorical proposition, whether absolute or qualified, is the affirmation of the predicate for the subject in reality, not absolute affirmation; otherwise it would not be false by its negation relative to actuality, necessarily because the negation of the qualified does not entail the negation of the absolute. For if we suppose the non-existence of the absolute in reality, it would necessitate the non-existence of the qualified)
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the logicians' view regarding the predicate of conditional propositions. The issue is whether the assertive content (khabar) of a conditional is the consequent alone or the entire conditional relation between antecedent and consequent. The author begins by noting that there is a dispute: the grammatical tradition often treats the consequent as the predicate, but logicians argue that the predicate is the entire conditional connection. Al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani is quoted asserting that there is no real dispute because grammarians also effectively judge the relation between the two parts, as they say the conditional particle indicates causality of the antecedent and effectuality of the consequent. The author then presents a proof: a conditional can be certainly true even if the consequent is false in reality, e.g., 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray' is true, though Zayd never actually brays. If the predicate were merely the consequent, truth would require the consequent to be true, which is not the case. Al-Sayyid explains that the negation of the absolute (consequent) would follow from the negation of the qualified (conditional), so allowing the conditional to be true with a false consequent would entail a contradiction. Next, al-Dawani offers a response: the falsity of the consequent in all actual times does not mean it is false in the hypothetical times of the conditional; the conditional quantifies over estimated times. He uses an analogy: 'Zayd is standing in my supposition' is not falsified by Zayd not standing in reality. An objection is raised that the predicate in such a case is 'supposed standing', not 'standing', so the falsity would depend on the supposition. Al-Dawani dismisses this as a weak objection. Then an argument about the entailment between the negation of the qualified and the absolute is addressed: it is conceded that negating the qualified implies negating the absolute, but the absolute here is not the actual absolute; it is a more general absolute that includes the estimated world. The expression may not directly express that absolute by correspondence (mutabaqa), but that is fine; it indicates it within the qualified. Finally, some later scholars are cited: the import of a categorical proposition (hamliyya) is the affirmation of the predicate for the subject in reality, not absolute affirmation; otherwise it could never be false. This distinction preserves the possibility of falsity. The passage overall clarifies the truth conditions of conditionals and defends the logicians' view that the predicate is the conditional relation, not just the consequent.

Key Terms
النسبة الإنشائية
الشرطية
التالي
المطلق
المقيد

Key Takeaway
The logicians' view that the predicate of a conditional is the entire conditional relation is defended by showing that conditionals can be true even when the consequent is false, provided the consequent holds in all hypothetical situations where the antecedent holds.

Truth Conditions of Conditionals: Actual vs. Estimated Times
Al-Dawwani and later scholars critique conditional truth conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between actual and estimated truth conditions for conditional propositions using the critique of al-Dawwani.

Core Idea
A conditional can be true in estimation even if false in reality.

In Islamic logic, the truth of conditional propositions is not always tied to actual reality. Al-Dawwani and later scholars debate whether the consequent must be true in all actual times or only in estimated times. This lesson unpacks their key arguments, focusing on the example of Zayd's neighing to illustrate the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Dawwani's Main Claim
Find where al-Dawwani states that the falsity of the consequent in all actual times does not entail its falsity in estimated times. Notice the example: 'Zayd is neighing' in times when his donkeyness is estimated, even if he never neighs in reality.
This is the core of the critique.
2
Contrast with the Later Scholar's Objection
The later scholar argues that categorical propositions always assert the predicate in actual reality. How does this challenge al-Dawwani's view? Consider the example 'Day exists at sunrise'.
The objection is that truth requires actual existence.
3
Analyze al-Dawwani's Response
Al-Dawwani distinguishes between the absolute (including estimated times) and the restricted. He grants that non-existence of the restricted entails non-existence of the absolute, but argues the absolute here is more general, covering estimation. The expression may not be direct but is implicit.
This shows how he defends his position.
4
Evaluate the Debate's Resolution
The author notes an oversight in the later scholar's objection: narration can be about estimation, but sciences focus on reality. Assess whether the critique is resolved or remains nuanced.
The final stance is that conditional truth must consider estimated contexts.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أَوْقَاتٍ قُدِّرَ فِيهَا حِمَارِيَّةُ زَيْدٍ ثَابِتَةٌ لَهُ ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ بِحَسَبِ الْأَوْقَاتِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ مَسْلُوبَةً عَنْهُ ، أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ في ظَنّي لَمْ يَكْذِبْ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْقِيَام في الواقع. وَمَا قِيلَ : الْمَحْكُومُ بِهِ فِي تِلْكَ الْقَضِيَّةِ مَظْنُونُ الْقِيَامِ لَا قَائِمٌ، فَإِنَّمَا يَكْذِبُ بِانْتِفَاءِ مَظْنُونِيَّةِ الْقِيَامِ بِخِلَافِ النَّاهِقِيَّةِ، فَنِدَاءٌ مِنْ بَعِيدٍ عَلَى مَا لَا يَخْفَى. وَمَا ذُكِرَ مِنَ الِاسْتِلْزَامِ بَيْنَ انْتِفَاءِ الْمُقَيِّدِ وَانْتِفَاءِ الْمُطْلَقِ فَمُسَلَّمْ، لَكِنْ لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ الْمُطْلَقَ هَهُنَا مُنْتَفٍ، فَإِنَّهُ الْمَأْخُوذُ عَلَى وَجْهِ أَعَمَّ مِمَّا فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، بِحَيْثُ يَشْمَلُ عَالَمَ التَّقْدِيرِ أَيْضًا، غَايَةُ مَا يُقَالُ: إِنَّ الْعِبَارَةَ غَيْرُ مَوْضُوعَةٍ لِتَأْدِيَةِ ذَلِكَ الْمَعْنَى الْمُطْلَقِ بِانْفِرَادِهِ مُطَابَقَةً، وَلَا ضَيْرَ فِيهِ. بَلْ يَدُلُّ فِي ضِمْنِ الْمُقَيِّدِ. قَالَ بَعْضُ أَجِلَّةِ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ: مُفَادُ الْقَضِيَّةِ الْحَمْلِيَّةِ، سَوَاءً كَانَتْ مُطْلَقَةً أَوْ مُقَيَّدَةً، ثُبُوتُ الْمَحْمُولِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ، لَا مُطْلَقُ الثُّبُوتِ، وَإِلَّا لَمْ تَكُنْ كَاذِبَةً بِانْتِفَائِهِ عَنْ نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ؛ ضَرُورَةَ أَنَّ انْتِفَاءَ الْمُقَيِّدِ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ انْتِفَاءَ الْمُطْلَقِ، فَلَوْ فَرَضْنَا عَدَمَ تَحَقُّقِ الْمُطْلَقِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ يَلْزَمُ عَدَمُ تَحَقُّقِ الْمُقَيِّدِ ضَرُورَةً، فَمَعْنَى النَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ وَقْتَ طُلُوعِ الشَّمْسِ: النَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ فِي الْوَاقِعِ فِي ذَلِكَ الْوَقْتِ، فَلَا بُدَّ فِي صِدْقِهِ مِنْ وُجُودِ الْمُطْلَقِ مَعَ الْقَيْدِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ، فَلَوْ لَمْ يَتَحَقَّقْ لَمْ يَتَحَقَّقْ مَعَ الْقَيْدِ أَيْضًا. نَعَمْ، الْقَضَايَا الْمُقَيَّدَةُ بِمَا هِيَ حِكَايَةٌ عَنْ نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ تَدُلُّ عَلَى ثُبُوتِ شَيْءٍ لِشَيْءٍ فِي الْوَاقِعِ بِحَسَبِ الْحِكَايَةِ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنِ انْتِفَائِهِ انْتِفَاؤُهُ بِحَسَبِ الْحِكَايَةِ، وَلَكِنْ لَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ هَذَا الْقَيْدَ لَا يَصْلُحُ أَنْ يَقَعَ مُقَدَّمًا لِلشَّرْطِيَّةِ، فَمَا قَالَ: إِنَّ انْتِفَاءَ ثُبُوتِ التَّالِي بِحَسَبِ نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ انْتِفَاءَ ثُبُوتِهِ عَلَى التَّقْدِيرِ، فَهُوَ إِذَا كَانَتِ الْقَضِيَّةُ شَرْطِيَّةً، وَالنَّظِيرُ خَارِجٌ مِنَ الْبَحْثِ. وَفِيهِ نَظَرٌ ظَاهِرٌ؛ فَإِنَّ الْحِكَايَةَ كَمَا تَصِحُّ عَنِ الْوَاقِعِ تَصِحُّ عَنْ عَالَمِ التَّقْدِيرِ، وَقَوْلُ الْحَاكِي عَنِ التَّقْدِيرِ: زَيْدٌ نَاهِقٌ كَلَامٌ تَامٌّ الْبَتَّةَ، وَلَيْسَ إِنْشَاءً، فَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ خَبَرًا غَايَةَ الْأَمْرِ أَنَّ الْعِبَارَةَ غَيْرُ مَوْضُوعَةٍ لَهُ، وَالْمُعْتَبَرُ فِي الْعُلُومِ وَالصَّنَائِعِ مَا هُوَ حِكَايَةٌ عَنِ الْوَاقِعِ، وَلَا مُضَايَقَةَ فِيهِ.
Al-'Allamah al-Dawwani (may God have mercy on him) said: The falsity of the consequent in all actual times does not necessitate its falsity in estimated times. Thus, 'neighing' is affirmed for Zayd in all times in which his 'donkeyness' is estimated, even if it is negated of him in actual times. Do you not see that 'Zayd is standing in my estimation' does not become false by the non-existence of standing in reality? And what was said: 'What is predicated in that proposition is the estimated-standing, not standing itself; so it is falsified only by the non-existence of estimated-standing, unlike neighing' – that is a call from afar, as is not hidden. And what was mentioned of the entailment between the non-existence of the restricted and the non-existence of the absolute is granted, but we do not grant that the absolute here is non-existent; for it is taken in a sense more general than what is in reality, such that it includes the world of estimation as well. The most that can be said is that the expression is not designated to convey that absolute meaning by itself directly, but there is no harm in that; rather, it indicates it within the restricted. Some of the eminent later scholars said: The import of a categorical proposition, whether it is absolute or restricted, is the affirmation of the predicate of the subject in reality, not absolute affirmation; otherwise it would not be false by its non-existence in reality, because the non-existence of the restricted does not entail the non-existence of the absolute. So if we suppose the non-actualization of the absolute in reality, then the non-actualization of the restricted would necessarily follow. Thus the meaning of 'day exists at the time of sunrise' is: day exists in reality at that time. So for its truth, the absolute with the restriction must exist in reality; if it does not, then the restricted does not exist either. Yes, restricted propositions insofar as they are narratives about reality indicate the affirmation of something to something in reality according to the narrative; so its non-existence does not entail its non-existence according to the narrative. But it is not hidden that this restriction is not suitable to serve as the antecedent of a conditional. So what he said – that the non-existence of the affirmation of the consequent according to reality does not entail the non-existence of its affirmation on the hypothesis – applies if the proposition is conditional, and the analogy is outside the discussion. And in this is an obvious oversight; for narration, just as it is valid of reality, is also valid of the world of estimation, and the statement of the narrator about estimation: 'Zayd is neighing' is a complete sentence, and it is not a performative utterance; so it must be a statement. At most, the expression is not designated for that, but the consideration in the sciences and arts is what is a narrative about reality, and there is no narrowness in that.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a position regarding the truth conditions of conditional propositions and the distinction between actual and estimated times. The discussion originates from al-'Allamah al-Dawwani's response to an argument by al-Sayyid al-Sharif. Al-Dawwani argues that the falsity of the consequent in all actual times does not necessitate its falsity in estimated times. He gives the example of 'Zayd is neighing' in estimated times of his being a donkey: even if Zayd is not actually a donkey and never neighs in reality, the proposition 'Zayd is neighing' can be true under the estimation that he is a donkey. Analogously, 'Zayd is standing in my estimation' is true even if he is not standing in reality, because the predicate is 'estimated-standing', not actual standing. Al-Dawwani then addresses an objection that in the 'standing' example the predicate is 'estimated-standing', so the denial of actual standing does not falsify it, but in the 'neighing' example the predicate is just 'neighing', not 'estimated-neighing'. Al-Dawwani dismisses this objection as misguided. Next, he considers the claim that the non-existence of the restricted (the conditional) entails the non-existence of the absolute; he grants this entailment but argues that the absolute in these cases is not the absolute of actual existence but a more general absolute that includes estimated existence. The expression may not directly convey this absolute, but it does so implicitly within the restricted. A later scholar then argues that categorical propositions (hamliyya) always assert the predicate of the subject in actual reality, not absolutely; otherwise they could never be false. For example, 'Day exists at the time of sunrise' must mean that day actually exists at that time; if the absolute (day existence) is not actual, then the restricted (day at sunrise) cannot be actual either. So the truth requires actual existence of the absolute with the restriction. However, al-Dawwani's point is that restricted propositions as narratives about reality indicate affirmation according to the narrative; the non-existence of the reality does not negate the narrative. But this restriction (the narrative aspect) cannot serve as the antecedent in a conditional proposition. Therefore, the claim that the non-existence of the consequent in reality does not entail its non-existence on the hypothesis is valid only if the proposition is conditional, and the analogy (of 'Zayd is standing in my estimation') is outside that discussion. Finally, the author notes that this last point has an obvious oversight: narration can be about estimation as well as about reality; 'Zayd is neighing' on the estimation is a complete declarative sentence, not a performative. The expression may not be conventionally designated for that meaning, but in the sciences and arts, what is considered is what narrates reality, and there is no difficulty in that. Thus the passage carefully refines the truth conditions of conditional and categorical propositions, emphasizing the distinction between actual and estimated contexts.

القضية الحملية
المطلق
المقيد

عالم التقدير

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions can be true under estimation even when false in reality, a distinction crucial for sound logical reasoning in Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism

Implication of Contradictories and Reductio Proof
Defense of reductio principle and teacher's critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logicians' reductio proof using the principle of implication of contradictories and identify the teacher's objection to its validity.

Core Idea
Reductio ad absurdum relies on the permissibility of impossible things implying impossible things.

This passage defends a key principle of qiyas khalfi (reductio ad absurdum): that a statement can imply its own contradictory or both contradictories. The logicians, following al-Dawani, leverage this to answer a famous fallacy. We will trace their argument step by step and consider a teacher's critique that reveals its limitations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational principle
Find the claim: logicians permit a thing to imply its contradictory and both contradictories. This is based on the idea that one impossible thing can imply another impossible thing.
This is radical: normally a true premise implies only true conclusions, but here the premises may be impossible (e.g., the negation of a true claim).
2
Understand the fallacy they address
The fallacy: if a claim is not established, its contradictory is established, and whenever the contradictory is established, some thing is established. Objectors say the contradictory of a thing is its negation, which is not a thing, so nothing is established.
The fallacious reasoning assumes that 'not established' means an affirmation, but it may be a negation.
3
Follow the contrapositive inversion
The logicians reply: if the claim is negative (e.g., 'no thing is established'), then its contradictory is an affirmative. They apply aks al-naqid (contrapositive) to the conditional: 'If the claim is not established, then something is established' becomes 'If nothing is established, then the claim is established.' This yields a contradiction (the claim both established and not), so the claim is proven.
This is the classic reductio: assume the negation, derive a contradiction, therefore the original holds.
4
Examine the teacher's critique
Some teachers object that the invalidity of the contrapositive step only invalidates the conclusion or one of the premises, not the contradictory of the original claim. Hence the reductio does not necessarily prove the claim; the proof is not universally valid.
The passage ends with this critique, leaving the issue unresolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَقُولُ: في نصرة أهل المنطق ممهدا للأصل، وهو: أنهم - ومنهم المحقق الدواني - جوزوا استلزام شيء للنقيض والنقيضين، وعليه مدار القياسات الخلفية؛ بناء على جواز استلزام محال محالا، وتشبثوا بذلك في مواضع عديدة، منها في جواب المغالطة العامة الورود المشهورة، من أن المدعى ثابت، وإلا يكن ثابتا فنقيضه ثابت، وكلما كان نقيضه ثابتا كان شيء من الأشياء ثابتا. وإن شئت قلت: كان قضية ما ثابتة، فلا يرد أن نقيض كل شيء رفعه، وهو ليس بشيء، فعلى تقدير ثبوت النقيض لا يثبت شيء، بل يرتفع شيء، على أنه إن فرض المدعى سالبة لا يفي هذا الدفع؛ فإن نقيضها أو لازمه الثبوت الذي هو الشيء، فكلما لم يكن المدعى ثابتا كان شيء من الأشياء ثابتا، أو قضية ما صادقة، وينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا كلما لم يكن شيء من الأشياء ثابتا، أو كلما لم يكن قضية ما صادقة كان المدعى ثابتا، هذا خلف، فثبت المدعى. قال بعض الأساتذة: إن بطلان هذا العكس إنما يوجب بطلان النتيجة الموجب لبطلان إحدى مقدمتي القياس، أو هيئته لا بطلان نقيض المدعى حتى يثبت المدعى، فهذه المغلطة ليست عامة الورود، بل إنما يتوجه على
I say: In support of the logicians, preparing the ground for the principle, which is: They - among them the investigator al-Dawani - permitted that a thing implies its contradictory and both contradictories. And on this is based the reductio ad absurdum syllogisms (qiyasat khalfiyya), based on the permissibility of an impossible thing implying another impossible thing. And they clung to this in many places, including in answering a commonly occurring famous fallacy, that the claim is established, otherwise if it is not established then its contradictory is established, and whenever its contradictory is established, some thing among things is established. Or if you wish, you may say: some proposition is established. Then it is not objected that the contradictory of every thing is its negation, and that is not a thing, so on the supposition of the establishment of the contradictory, nothing is established, rather a thing is removed. Moreover, if the claim is supposed to be negative, this repulsion does not suffice; for its contradictory or its necessary concomitant is the existence which is the thing. So whenever the claim is not established, some thing among things is established, or some true proposition. And it is reversed by the contrapositive (aks al-naqid) to our statement: Whenever nothing among things is established, or whenever no proposition is true, the claim is established. This is absurd, so the claim is established. Some teachers said: The invalidity of this reversal only necessitates the invalidity of the conclusion, which necessitates the invalidity of one of the premises of the syllogism or its form, not the invalidity of the contradictory of the claim so that the claim is established. So this fallacy is not of common occurrence, but rather it is directed only against
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents the defense of logicians regarding the principle that a thing can imply its own contradictory or both contradictories, a key tenet for qiyas khalfi (reductio ad absurdum). The author begins by stating that logicians, including al-Dawani, permit this, grounding it in the permissibility of one impossible thing implying another. They use this widely, especially in answering a famous fallacy that attempts to prove a claim by showing that its negation leads to a contradiction. The fallacy goes: if the claim is not established, then its contradictory is established, and whenever the contradictory is established, some thing is established. But the objector might say that the contradictory of a thing is its negation, which is not a thing, so no thing is established. The logicians counter that if the claim is negative, its contradictory or concomitant is an affirmative, so something is established. Then they use contrapositive inversion (aks al-naqid) to derive an absurdity: if nothing is established, then the claim is established. This is a contradiction, so the claim is established. Some teachers object that the invalidity of the contrapositive only invalidates the conclusion or premises, not the contradictory of the claim, so the proof fails. The passage ends mid-sentence with the teacher's critique, suggesting that the fallacy is not universally applicable.

نقيض
قياس خلفي
عكس النقيض

Key Takeaway
Reductio ad absurdum is built on the principle that an impossible thing can imply another impossible thing, but its validity depends on the correctness of the contrapositive step and the structure of the proof.

The Syllogism

The Common Reductio Fallacy and Its Limited Applicability
Author presents and restricts the common reductio fallacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the common reductio fallacy and explain why it is not universally valid according to the objection by the masters.

Core Idea
A reductio argument's validity depends on the correctness of the contrapositive step, which may fail when antecedents are impossible.

This passage examines a famous sophism in reductio arguments that seems to prove any claim by showing its negation leads to something true. We will dissect its logical form and then consider an objection that the fallacy is not universal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the reductio structure
Locate the proposition being proved (al-mudda'a) and observe how the argument assumes its falsehood to derive an absurdity.
2
Write the conditional form
Express the reasoning as a conditional: 'If the claim is false, then something is true (or some proposition is true).'
This is the core conditional premise.
3
Apply contrapositive
Convert the conditional to its contrapositive: 'If nothing is true (or no proposition is true), then the claim is true.' This is the step used to force a contradiction.
The contrapositive is supposed to be false, so the claim is established.
4
Recognize the objection
Understand the objection from 'some masters': the falsity of the contrapositive only shows the syllogism is invalid, not that the claim's contradictory is false. Hence the fallacy is not universal.
The objection limits the fallacy's scope to assents (tasdiqat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياسات الخلفية؛ بناء على جواز استلزام محال محالا، وتشبثوا بذلك في مواضع عديدة، منها في جواب المغالطة العامة الورود المشهورة، من أن المدعى ثابت، وإلا يكن ثابتا فنقيضه ثابت، وكلما كان نقيضه ثابتا كان شيء من الأشياء ثابتا. وإن شئت قلت: كان قضية ما ثابتة، فلا يرد أن نقيض كل شيء رفعه، وهو ليس بشيء، فعلى تقدير ثبوت النقيض لا يثبت شيء، بل يرتفع شيء، على أنه إن فرض المدعى سالبة لا يفي هذا الدفع؛ فإن نقيضها أو لازمه الثبوت الذي هو الشيء، (فكلما لم يكن المدعى ثابتا كان شيء من الأشياء ثابتا)، أو قضية ما صادقة، (وينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا كلما لم يكن شيء من الأشياء ثابتا)، أو كلما لم يكن قضية ما صادقة (كان المدعى ثابتا، هذا خلف، فثبت المدعى. قال بعض الأساتذة: إن بطلان هذا العكس إنما يوجب بطلان النتيجة الموجب لبطلان إحدى مقدمتي القياس، أو هيئته لا بطلان نقيض المدعى حتى يثبت المدعى، فهذه المغلطة ليست عامة الورود، بل إنما يتوجه على التصديقات. قاعدة إنتاج اللزوميتين لزومية، أو انعكاس الموجبة الكلية كنفسها. وهذا الكلام جيد على التقرير المشهور، وربما يقرر بأن المدعى ثابت، وأن عدم ثبوته ملزوم لثبوت شيء من الأشياء بذلك القياس، فيكون ملزوما للزوم شيء الأشياء له، فيكون ملزوما لهذه القضية الملزومة للعكس، وهو باطل، فيكون ملزومه الذي هو عدم ثبوت المدعى باطلا، فيكون المدعى ثابتا، وهو المطلوب. وتقرير جواب المصنف: أنا لا نسلم بطلان عكس النقيض؛ فإن المقدم فيه محال، فمن الجائز أن يستلزم ثبوت المدعى الذي هو مناف له، وليس هذا منعا مجردا، بل له سند جيد، هو أن عدم ثبوت شيء من الأشياء ملزوم لارتفاع النقيضين المدعى ونقيضه، وهو مستلزم لاجتماع النقيضين، فيكون عدم ثبوت شيء من الأشياء ملزوما لمجموع ثبوت المدعى ونقيضه، فيكون ملزوما لأحدهما الذي هو ثبوت المدعى، فصدق العكس بلا ريب. فما قال بعض الشراح، من أن عكس قولنا: لو كان المدعى ثابتا كان شيء من الأشياء ثابتا مجزوم الصدق ومع الجزم بصدق عكسه على طريقة القدماء والمتأخرين لا يبقى الجزم بذلك العكس، فالعكس باطل قطعا، والمنع مكابرة ساقط؛ لأن عكسه على طريقة القدماء قولنا: كلما لم يكن شيء ما ثابتا لم يكن المدعى ثابتا وهو لزومية موجبة مثل العكس المتقدم، ولا تنافي بين اللزوميتين الموجبتين، وإن كان تاليهما متنافيين. نعم العكس على طريقة المتأخرين نقيض للعكس المتقدم، لكن لا اعتداد في الشرطيات على ما ستقف إن شاء الله. ثم أجاب بالقدح في الصغرى إن أخذت كلية بأن من تقادير عدم ثبوت
Reductive arguments, based on the permissibility of one impossible thing implying another impossible thing. And they clung to this in numerous places, among them in answering the common well-known fallacy, that the claim is true; if it were not true, then its contradictory is true; and whenever its contradictory is true, something from among things is true. And if you wish, you say: a certain proposition is true. So it is not objected that the contradictory of everything is its negation, which is not a thing; so on the supposition of the truth of the contradictory, nothing is established, rather something is removed. Except that if the claim is assumed to be a negative proposition, this dodge does not suffice; for its contradictory, or its necessary concomitant, is the existence which is the thing; (so whenever the claim is not true, something from among things is true), or a certain proposition is true; (and it is inverted by contraposition to our statement: whenever nothing from among things is true), or whenever no proposition is true, (the claim is true; this is a reductio ad absurdum, so the claim is established). One of the masters said: The falsity of this contrapositive only necessitates the falsity of the conclusion, which necessitates the falsity of one of the premises of the syllogism or its form, not the falsity of the contradictory of the claim so that the claim would be established. So this fallacy is not of universal applicability, rather it only applies to assents. The rule: the production of two conditional propositions is a conditional, or the conversion of the universal affirmative is like itself. And this statement is good according to the well-known formulation. And perhaps it is formulated that the claim is true, and that its non-existence necessitates the existence of something from among things in that syllogism, so it becomes a necessitator of the necessity of something from among things for it, so it becomes a necessitator of this proposition which is the necessitator of the converse, and that is false; so its necessitator, which is the non-existence of the claim, is false; so the claim is true, which is the required. And the formulation of the author's answer: We do not concede the falsity of the contrapositive; for its antecedent is impossible, so it is possible that it necessitates the truth of the claim which is contrary to it; and this is not a mere denial, rather it has a good basis, which is that the non-existence of something from among things necessitates the removal of the two contradictories, the claim and its contradictory, and it necessitates the conjunction of the two contradictories; so the non-existence of something from among things necessitates the aggregate of the truth of the claim and its contradictory, so it necessitates one of them, which is the truth of the claim; so the truth of the converse is without doubt. So what some commentators said, that the converse of our statement: 'If the claim were true, something from among things would be true' is definitely true, and with certainty of the truth of its converse according to the method of the ancients and the later scholars, certainty about that converse does not remain, so the converse is absolutely false, and the denial is a futile obstinacy; because its converse according to the method of the ancients is our statement: 'Whenever nothing is true, the claim is not true' and it is an affirmative conditional like the preceding converse, and there is no contradiction between two affirmative conditionals, even if their consequents are contradictory. Yes, the converse according to the method of the later scholars is the contradictory of the preceding converse, but conditionals are not considered, as you will understand if God wills. Then he answered by attacking the minor premise if taken universally, that from some suppositions of non-existence
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage critically examines a common fallacy in reductio ad absurdum arguments (قياسات خلفية). The fallacy arises when one argues: 'The claim is true; if it were not true, then its contradictory would be true, and that leads to something being true (or some proposition being true), which is impossible, therefore the claim is true.' The author points out that the fallacy lies in the contrapositive (عكس النقيض) step: from 'if the claim is not true then something is true' to 'if nothing is true then the claim is true'. This step is not always valid, especially when the antecedent of the conditional is impossible (محال). The objection from 'some masters' is that the falsity of the contrapositive only shows that the original syllogism is invalid, not that the contradictory of the claim is false. The author then defends the contrapositive by arguing that if the antecedent is impossible, it can still imply the consequent, and gives a metaphysical argument involving the impossibility of both a claim and its contradictory being false (رفع النقيضين). The commentator then responds to a further objection about the certainty of the original conditional and its converse, distinguishing between the methods of the ancients and the later scholars. Finally, the author attacks the minor premise of the fallacy when taken as a universal statement. Throughout, the discussion uses examples from the logical tradition and shows careful attention to the conditions of validity in conditional syllogisms.

Key Terms
قياس خلفي
عكس النقيض
مغالطة عامة الورود
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection by 'some masters', what does the falsity of the contrapositive in the common reductio fallacy indicate?
A) That the claim is false.
B) That the original syllogism is invalid (a premise or its form is false).
C) That the contradictory of the claim is true.
D) That the claim must be true.

Key Takeaway
The common reductio fallacy is not universal because the contrapositive step's failure only invalidates the syllogism, not the claim itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a reductio argument on the topic of 'wine is forbidden' using the fallacy structure, then identify where the contrapositive step fails.
?
Given the conditional 'If the claim is false, then something is true', derive its contrapositive and explain why it may not be valid in this context according to the objection.

Defending the Contrapositive with Impossible Antecedents
Author defends contrapositive validity using impossible antecedents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's defense of the contrapositive using the notion that an impossible antecedent can imply anything, and distinguish between the ancient and later methods of contraposition.

Core Idea
An impossible antecedent can still imply a consequent, so the contrapositive can be true even if the original conditional seems counterintuitive.

After presenting the objection, the author mounts a defense: the contrapositive's antecedent is impossible, so the conditional can be true. He then discusses the methods of the ancients and later scholars regarding contraposition. We will unpack this nuanced argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the author's defense
The author does not concede that the contrapositive is false. Instead, he argues that its antecedent (e.g., 'nothing is true') is impossible (muhal), and an impossible antecedent can imply any consequent, including the original claim.
This is a key logical principle: ex impossibili sequitur quodlibet.
2
Follow the metaphysical argument
The author provides a basis: 'nothing is true' entails the conjunction of the claim and its contradictory (ref' al-naqidayn), which in turn entails the claim itself. Therefore the contrapositive is true.
This shows the contrapositive's truth from the impossibility of the antecedent.
3
Distinguish ancient vs. later methods
The commentator notes that according to the ancients, the contrapositive is an affirmative conditional ('if nothing is true then the claim is not true'), which does not contradict the original conditional. According to later scholars, the contrapositive is the contradictory, but this is not a problem for conditionals.
The distinction affects how we assess the validity of the reductio.
