Condition as Predicate Restriction Leads to Contradiction
Author argues that condition as predicate restriction leads to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradiction that arises when a condition is interpreted as a restriction on the predicate in a conditional proposition, and distinguish this interpretation from the connection interpretation.

Core Idea
Treating a condition as a predicate restriction leads to contradictories; the connection interpretation avoids this.

This lesson examines a critical distinction in the logic of conditional propositions: whether the condition (shart) restricts the predicate (musnad) in the consequent, or whether it expresses a connection (ittisal) between two relations. The author demonstrates that the restriction interpretation leads to a contradiction when the antecedent is a concomitant of both an affirmative and negative consequent. Through concrete examples, you will learn why the connection interpretation is logically sound.

Guided Path
1
State the hypothesis
The author hypothesizes: if the condition restricts the predicate in the consequent (as grammarians claim), then when the antecedent is a concomitant of both an affirmative and a negative consequent, two contradictory propositions become true.
The hypothesis is a conditional: if restriction interpretation, then contradiction.
2
Understand the example
Consider the antecedent 'If nothing of things is established.' Then two propositions: 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not standing.' Under the restriction interpretation, both conditionals are true, so Zayd is both standing and not standing at that time.
The example shows the absurd consequence.
3
Analyze the two cases
The grammarians say the condition restricts affirmation in the affirmative consequent. For the negative consequent, either the condition restricts the negation (making it a restricted negation) or restricts the negation as a whole. Both lead to contradiction: either affirmation and negation of the same restricted predicate, or the restricted negation implies the negation of the restricted, again a contradiction.
The key move: the contradictory of a restricted predicate is its negation, not the restricted negation.
4
Contrast with the connection interpretation
If the conditional judgment is about the connection (ittisal) between the two relations (antecedent and consequent), then the contradictory of the connection is its removal, not another connection. Thus, no contradiction arises even if the antecedents are the same.
The connection interpretation treats the conditional as a single proposition about the entailment, not as a restriction on the predicate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبَعْدَ تَمْهِيدِ ذَلِكَ نَقُولُ : لَوْ كَانَ الشَّرْطُ قَيْدًا لِلْمُسْنَدِ فِي الْجَزَاءِ، كما نسب إلى أهل العربية لَزِمَ اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ فِيمَا إِذَا كَانَ الْمُقَدَّمُ مَلْزُومًا لَهُمَا ؛ إذ حينئذ يصدق قضيتان تالي أحدهما موجبة، وتالي أخرى سالبة، كقولنا: إذا لم يثبت شيء من الأشياء فزيد قائم، وإذا لم يثبت شيء منها فزيد ليس بقائم، فنقول : أهل العربية قد قالوا بأن الشرط في موجبة التالي قيد للثبوت، ففي سالبة التالي إن كان قيدا له أيضا فقد ورد السلب على المقيد، وثبوته وسلبه نقيضان قطعا، وإن كان قيدا للسلب فهو سلب مقيد، ولكونه أخص من سلب المقيد صدق سلب المقيد أيضا وهما نقيضان، وإلى هذا أشار بقوله: فَإِنَّ قَوْلَنَا زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ فِي وَقْتِ عَدَمِ ثُبُوتِ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْأَشْيَاءِ يُنَاقِضُ قَوْلَنَا زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِقَائِمٍ فِي ذَلِكَ الْوَقْتِ ، وَهُوَ بَدِيهِيٌّ، وبتقريرنا اندفع ما قال بعض الشراح من أنهم أرادوا بكون الشرط قيدا في المسند أنه قيد للثبوت في موجبة التالي، وقيد للسلب في سالبة التالي، ونقيض المقيد سلبه، لا السلب المقيد، كما أن نقيض الاتصال رفعه ، لا اتصال الرفع. وَأَمَّا إِذَا كَانَ الْحُكْمُ فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ بِالاِتِّصَالِ بَيْنَ النِّسْبَتَيْنِ فَلَا يَلْزَمُ ذَلِكَ ؛ فَإِنَّ نَقِيض الاِتِّصَالِ رَفْعُهُ ، لَا وُجُودُ اتِّصَالِ آخَرَ أَيَّ اتِّصَالٍ كَانَ، تاليه مناف لتالي الأول ، أم لا . واعترض بأن اجتماع النقيضين إنما يلزم إذا كان المراد بالتالي الثبوت في نفس الأمر، وهو ممنوع ، وإنما المراد الثبوت على ذلك التقدير، فلا تناقض ، كما لا تناقض بين المتصلتين المذكورتين . وأجاب عنه بعض الأساتذة في مكاتباته أن فيه خلطا بين الشرطية والحملية ؛ فإنه إن أراد بالثبوت على التقدير ثبوت نسبة على تقدير أخرى، بأن
And after that preface we say: If the condition were a restriction for the predicate in the consequent, as is attributed to the grammarians, then the conjunction of the two contradictories would be necessary in the case where the antecedent is a concomitant of both of them; for then two propositions would be true: the consequent of one is affirmative, and the consequent of the other is negative, like our saying: If nothing of things is established, then Zayd is standing, and If nothing of them is established, then Zayd is not standing. So we say: The grammarians have said that the condition in the affirmative consequent is a restriction for the affirmation, so in the negative consequent, if it is also a restriction for it, then the negation applies to the restricted, and its affirmation and negation are definitely contradictories. And if it is a restriction for the negation, then it is a restricted negation, and because it is more specific than the negation of the restricted, the negation of the restricted is also true, and they are two contradictories. And to this he alluded by his saying: 'Indeed our saying Zayd is standing at the time of non-establishment of anything contradicts our saying Zayd is not standing at that time, and it is obvious.' And by our explanation, what some commentators said is refuted: that they meant by the condition being a restriction in the predicate that it is a restriction for affirmation in the affirmative consequent, and a restriction for negation in the negative consequent, and the contradictory of the restricted is its negation, not the restricted negation, just as the contradictory of connection is its removal, not the connection of removal. And if the judgment in the conditional is by connection between the two relations, then that does not follow; for the contradictory of connection is its removal, not the existence of another connection, whatever connection it may be, whether its consequent is incompatible with the first consequent or not. And an objection was raised: The conjunction of the two contradictories is necessary only if by 'consequent' is meant actual existence, which is denied; but what is meant is existence on that assumption, so there is no contradiction, just as there is no contradiction between the two conditionals mentioned. And some masters answered in his correspondence that there is confusion between the conditional and the categorical; for if he means by 'existence on the assumption' the existence of a relation on another assumption, that...
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the logical consequences of treating the condition (shart) as a restriction (qayd) on the predicate (musnad) in the consequent (jaza') of a conditional proposition. The author begins with a hypothesis: if the condition restricts the predicate, as grammarians (ahl al-'arabiyya) are said to hold, then when the antecedent is a concomitant (malzum) of both an affirmative and a negative consequent, two contradictory propositions become true simultaneously, which is impossible. He gives an example: 'If nothing of things is established, then Zayd is standing' and 'If nothing of things is established, then Zayd is not standing.' According to the grammarians, the condition restricts the affirmation in the affirmative consequent and the negation in the negative consequent. The author then analyzes two cases: either the condition restricts the negation as well (first case), leading to a contradiction because the affirmation and negation of the same restricted predicate are contradictories; or the condition restricts the negation itself, making it a restricted negation, which is more specific than the negation of the restricted, so the negation of the restricted also holds, again leading to contradiction. He cites a statement from the text that 'Zayd is standing at the time of non-establishment of anything' contradicts 'Zayd is not standing at that time,' which is obvious. The author then refutes some commentators who claim that the condition restricts affirmation in one case and negation in the other, arguing that the contradictory of the restricted is its negation, not the restricted negation, analogous to the contradictory of connection (ittisal) being its removal, not the connection of removal. He then states that if the judgment in the conditional is by connection between the two relations (rather than by restriction), then the contradiction does not follow; the contradictory of connection is its removal, not the existence of another connection, regardless of compatibility. An objection is raised: the contradiction only arises if 'consequent' means actual existence, but it means existence on that assumption, so no contradiction. The author says some masters answer that this confuses conditional with categorical, and if by 'existence on the assumption' is meant the existence of a relation on another assumption... (text cuts off).

Key Terms
الشرط
المسند
الاتصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what follows if the condition is a restriction for the predicate in the consequent and the antecedent is a concomitant of both an affirmative and a negative consequent?
A) The conditional is false
B) Two contradictories become true simultaneously
C) The conversion is valid
D) The antecedent is impossible

Key Takeaway
Treating a condition as a predicate restriction leads to a logical contradiction; the connection interpretation avoids this by making the conditional about the entailment between the two relations.

The Syllogism

6 min
The Logicians' View on Conditional Syllogisms
Author declares logicians' view correct on conditional syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logicians' understanding of conditional syllogisms from the grammarians' and explain why the exceptional syllogism is valid only under the logicians' view.

Core Idea
For a conditional syllogism to be valid, the condition must qualify the judgment, not just be an assumption.

This passage settles a key debate between logicians and grammarians: does an exceptional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) produce a certain conclusion? The author declares the logicians' view correct and explains why the grammarians' understanding fails to yield valid inference. You will see how the logical relationship between a conditional's antecedent and consequent determines whether denying the consequent can negate the assumption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing doctrines
In this passage, the author declares the logicians' doctrine (مذهب المنطقيين) correct, contrasting it with the grammarians' view (أهل العربية). Read the passage and locate where each is mentioned.
Look for the phrases 'مذهب أهل المنطق' and 'أهل العربية'.
2
Understand the logicians' view on conditionals
For logicians, a conditional proposition expresses a necessary connection: the antecedent (تقدير) qualifies the judgment. This means that if the antecedent is true, the consequent must be true; and if the consequent is false, the antecedent must be false.
This allows both modus ponens and modus tollens.
3
Understand the grammarians' view
For grammarians, the condition is a mere assumption without necessary connection. Affirming the antecedent allows the consequent, but denying the consequent does not necessarily deny the assumption.
The text says: 'ثبوت شيء لشيء على تقدير لا يلزم من انتفائه انتفاء التقدير'.
4
Analyse the teacher's refinement
The teacher (بعض الأساتذة) explains that on the grammarians' view, modus tollens only works if we consider that the consequent is necessary for the assumption (لزومه للتقدير). This means the assumption implies the consequent, so its negation negates the assumption.
This effectively shows that the grammarians' view is insufficient unless supplemented with the logical necessity that the logicians assume.
5
Apply the rule to a fiqh example
Consider the conditional: 'If this is wine, then it is forbidden' (إن كان خمراً فهو حرام). On the logicians' view, if it is not forbidden, it is not wine. On the grammarians' view, we cannot conclude that it is not wine from it not being forbidden, unless we know that being forbidden is necessarily tied to being wine.
This example illustrates the practical difference between the two doctrines in legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَمَذْهَبُ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ هُوَ الْحَقُّ). قال في «الحاشية»: إنتاج اللزوميتين لزومية ضروري، وإنما يتسر على نظم طبعي على مذهب أهل المنطق، بخلاف ما ذهب إليه أهل العربية. ومثله ما قال بعض الأساتذة رحمه الله: القياس الاستثنائي إنما ينتج إذا كان الشرطية على فهم أهل المنطق، دون أهل العربية ؛ فإن ثبوت شيء لشيء على تقدير لا يلزم من انتفائه انتفاء التقدير، اللهم إلا بملاحظة لزومه للتقدير، وهذا كلام متين غاية المتانة، والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال .
So the doctrine of the logicians is the truth. He said in 'al-Hashiya': The productivity of two necessary premises to a necessary conclusion is necessary, and it is only achieved by a natural order according to the doctrine of the logicians, contrary to what the Arabic grammarians went to. Similar is what some teachers, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The exceptional syllogism only produces a conclusion when the conditional is according to the understanding of the logicians, not the Arabic grammarians; for the affirmation of something for something upon an assumption does not necessitate from its negation the negation of the assumption, unless by considering its necessity for the assumption. And this is a statement of utmost solidity, and Allah knows best the reality of the matter.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion by asserting the correctness of the logicians' view. The author cites 'al-Hashiya' stating that the production of a necessary conclusion from two necessary premises (luzumiyyat) is necessary, and this is achieved only by a natural arrangement (nazm tab'i) according to the logicians' doctrine, unlike the grammarians' view. Then he quotes a teacher who clarifies the crucial point about the exceptional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). For example, if we have a conditional 'If this is wine, then it is forbidden' (inna khamran fahuwa haram), and we affirm the antecedent (this is wine), we conclude the consequent (it is forbidden). But if we deny the consequent (it is not forbidden), we can conclude the denial of the antecedent (it is not wine). This works on the logicians' understanding: the condition is a qualification of the judgment, so denying the consequent negates the condition. On the grammarians' view, the condition is a mere assumption; denying the consequent does not necessarily deny the assumption, unless we consider that the consequent is necessary for the assumption (i.e., the assumption implies the consequent, so its negation negates the assumption). The teacher emphasizes this subtlety, calling the argument very solid. The passage ends with 'Allah knows best.'

Key Terms
مذهب المنطقيين (madhhab al-mantiqiyyīn): The doctrine of logicians regarding conditional propositions: the condition qualifies the judgment, not just the subject.
القياس الاستثنائي (al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī): The exceptional syllogism (modus ponens/tollens) that infers from a conditional and a premise about one part.
اللزومية (al-luzūmiyya): A necessary proposition; in context, a conditional that expresses necessary connection.
التقدير (al-taqdīr): The assumption or condition upon which the consequent is affirmed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why does the exceptional syllogism not produce a conclusion on the grammarians' understanding of the conditional?
A) Because grammarians deny the law of non-contradiction.
B) Because on their view, denying the consequent does not necessarily deny the assumption.
C) Because grammarians only use categorical syllogisms.
D) Because the conditional is always false.

Key Takeaway
The exceptional syllogism is valid only when the conditional expresses a necessary connection between antecedent and consequent, as the logicians hold.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct modus ponens and modus tollens using the following conditional from fiqh: 'If the amount of water is two qullas or more, then it remains pure (i.e., does not become ritually impure).' Write both valid inferences.
?

Why Conditional Syllogisms Do Not Require Actual Contradiction
Author defends logicians' conditional syllogism against grammarians' objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between actual and conditional judgments and explain why the logicians' view on conditional syllogisms avoids the contradiction alleged by Arabic grammarians.

Core Idea
In a conditional, the consequent is not asserted; only the connection is asserted.

Arabic grammarians objected that conditional syllogisms lead to contradiction when the antecedent is impossible. But the logicians responded by clarifying that the consequent in a conditional is not asserted as an actual fact—it is only hypothetically connected to the antecedent. This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows why the logicians' position is logically sound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the grammarians' argument: if the antecedent is impossible, then both antecedent and its negation could coexist without contradiction. Why would they think that?
They treat the conditional as asserting the truth of the consequent.
2
Distinguish actual vs conditional judgment
Note that the logicians hold that in a conditional, the consequent is not affirmed as an actual fact (thubut) but only on a supposition (taqdir). There is no report (hikaya) that could be contradicted.
This is the key difference that resolves the objection.
3
Recognize the absence of report
The author says 'the judgment is not in the consequent'—meaning the consequent does not make a claim about reality, so its negation does not create a contradiction with the conditional statement.
Contradiction requires two assertions about the same reality.
4
Affirm the logicians' position
Conclude that the logicians' understanding of conditional syllogisms is correct. The grammarians' objection is based on a misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون الحكم بين الثبوت والتقدير فمسلم أنه لا تناقض، لكن ذلك بعينه مذهب الحكماء وإن غيرت العبارة، وإن أراد ثبوت محمول التالي لموضوعه، بأن يكون مصححا للسكوت عليه لكن على تقدير فممنوع أنه لا تناقض، كيف وقد ورد السلب على ذلك الثبوت، والشيء وسلبه نقيضان ضرورة، وإنكاره مكابرة، هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب. واعترض عليه بأن اجتماع النقيضين إنما يستحيل إذا لم يكن القيد محالا، والقيد ههنا محال ألا ترى أنه في الشرطية الميزانية قد يجتمعان على تقدير المقدم المحال، ولا يخفى سخافته؛ فإن اجتماع النقيضين بمعنى مطابقتهما لما حكيا عنه محال بالضرورة، وأما في الشرطية فليس الحكم في التالي:، فلا حكاية، فلا مطابقة لما حكيا عنه، والله أعلم بالصواب. (فَمَذْهَبُ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ هُوَ الْحَقُّ). قال في «الحاشية»: إنتاج اللزوميتين لزومية ضروري، وإنما يتسر على نظم طبعي على مذهب أهل المنطق، بخلاف ما ذهب إليه أهل العربية. ومثله ما قال بعض الأساتذة رحمه الله: القياس الاستثنائي إنما ينتج إذا كان الشرطية على فهم أهل المنطق، دون أهل العربية؛ فإن ثبوت شيء لشيء على تقدير لا يلزم من انتفائه انتفاء التقدير، اللهم إلا بملاحظة لزومه للتقدير، وهذا كلام متين غاية المتانة، والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال.
The judgment is between affirmation and supposition, so it is conceded that there is no contradiction. But that is exactly the opinion of the philosophers, even if the expression is changed. And if he means the affirmation of the consequent's predicate for its subject, such that it qualifies for being predicated, but on a supposition, then it is denied that there is no contradiction. How can that be when the negation has been applied to that affirmation, and a thing and its negation are necessarily contradictory, and to deny that is obstinacy. This, and Allah knows best what is correct. And objection was raised against him that the conjunction of two contradictories is only impossible if the condition is not impossible, and the condition here is impossible. Do you not see that in the balanced conditional [syllogism] they may both come together on the supposition of an impossible antecedent? And its absurdity is not hidden. For the conjunction of two contradictories in the sense of their corresponding to what they report about is impossible of necessity. However, in the conditional proposition, the judgment is not in the consequent, so there is no reporting, and thus no correspondence to what they report about. And Allah knows best what is correct. (So the doctrine of the logicians is the truth.) He said in al-Hashiyah: The production of a necessary consequent from two implicative [premises] is necessary, and it is only realized according to a natural order according to the doctrine of the people of logic, contrary to what the people of Arabic [grammar] held. And similar to that is what some of the teachers, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The exceptional syllogism only produces a conclusion if the conditional proposition is according to the understanding of the people of logic, not the people of Arabic. For the affirmation of a thing for a thing on a condition does not entail from its negation the negation of the condition, unless by noting its necessary connection to the condition. And this is a very solid statement, and Allah knows best the reality of the situation.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of an objection raised against the logicians' view on conditional syllogisms. The author begins by conceding that if the judgment in a conditional proposition is merely suppositional (taqdiri) rather than actual (thubuti), then there is no contradiction when the antecedent is false. However, he points out that this is exactly the logicians' position, just expressed differently. The objection claims that the conjunction of two contradictories (i.e., affirming both the antecedent and its negation) is only impossible if the condition is possible, but here the condition (the antecedent) is impossible, so they can coexist. The author refutes this by distinguishing between actual judgment and hypothetical judgment: in a conditional, the consequent is not asserted as actual, so there is no report (hikaya) that could be contradicted. Thus, no contradiction arises. The key point is that the logicians' understanding of the conditional (as expressing a necessary connection between antecedent and consequent) is correct, while the Arabic grammarians' view (which treats the conditional as a factual conjunction) leads to confusion. The passage ends by affirming that the logicians' position is the truth, and that the exceptional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) only yields a valid conclusion when the conditional is understood in the logicians' sense, not the grammarians'.

Key Terms
الشرطية الميزانية
النقيضان
القياس الاستثنائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the consequent is not asserted as an actual fact, so no contradiction arises
C) Because Arabic grammarians have a different definition of contradiction
D) Because the antecedent and consequent are always both false

Key Takeaway
The logicians' view is correct: conditional propositions do not assert the truth of the consequent; they only assert a necessary connection, so no contradiction arises from negating the consequent.

The Exceptional Syllogism Requires the Logicians' Conditional
Author defends logicians' conditional syllogism against grammarians' objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the exceptional syllogism (modus ponens/tollens) is valid only under the logicians' interpretation of the conditional, not the grammarians'.

Core Idea
The exceptional syllogism works because the conditional asserts a necessary connection, not a factual conjunction.

As the passage concludes, the exceptional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) only yields a valid conclusion when the conditional is understood as the logicians do: as expressing a necessary connection between antecedent and consequent, not as a mere factual conjunction. This lesson applies the previous theoretical distinction to this practical syllogistic form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exceptional syllogism structure
Recall that an exceptional syllogism has a conditional major premise and a minor premise that affirms or denies either the antecedent (modus ponens) or consequent (modus tollens).
E.g., 'If A then B; A is true; therefore B is true'.
2
Understand the logicians' conditional
The logicians view the conditional as asserting a necessary connection: the consequent necessarily follows from the antecedent, regardless of the actual truth of either.
The conditional expresses a logical entailment, not a material implication.
3
Contrast with grammarians' understanding
The grammarians treat the conditional as a factual conjunction: they think it asserts the truth of the consequent under the condition, but not a necessary connection. Thus, denying the consequent does not necessarily deny the antecedent.
This undermines the validity of modus tollens.
4
See why validity depends on logicians' view
For the exceptional syllogism to be valid, the connection must be necessary. The grammarians' view does not guarantee that, so the syllogism fails under their interpretation.
The author quotes a teacher: 'The affirmation of a thing for a thing on a condition does not entail from its negation the negation of the condition, unless by noting its necessary connection.'

Source Text

5 min
Interpreting the Predicate Variable 'ج' in Quantified Propositions
Author clarifies what the variable 'ج' stands for in the quantified statement 'كل ج ب'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly interpret the predicate variable 'ج' in quantified propositions and distinguish it from incorrect interpretations.

Core Idea
The variable 'ج' refers to what is truly predicated of individuals, not the essence of the predicate or subject.

In quantified propositions like 'كل ج ب', the predicate variable 'ج' might be misinterpreted. This lesson clarifies its correct referent, which is essential for constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic logic. Understanding this distinction prevents errors in subsumption and ensures the first figure produces conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two incorrect interpretations rejected
The passage rejects that 'ج' means the essence of the predicate (haqiqa of J) or the essence of the subject (dhat al-mawdu'). If either were the case, examples like 'every writer is human' or 'every human is animal' would fail, and the first figure would not produce a conclusion due to lack of subsumption.
2
Understand the correct interpretation
The author explains that 'ج' means what is true of (yusdaq 'alayhi) the individuals of the subject, whether essentially (dhatiyyan) or accidentally (aradiyyan). This allows the predicate to apply to the actual instances of the subject.
3
Examine the concept of real individuals
The individuals considered are 'real individuals' (afrad haqiqiyya), whose particularity comes from the considerer. They can be personal (shakhsiyya) or specific (naw'iyya). The author notes that the latter is more precise.
This relates to how quantifiers range over individuals in logical analysis.
4
Apply to syllogistic reasoning
If 'ج' is a genus, general accident, or remote differentia, the truth applies to both personal and specific individuals. This ensures the middle term subsumes the minor term in first-figure syllogisms.
Example: 'All animals are living beings' – 'animal' as predicate applies to individual animals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن (ج) لا نعني بها ما حقيقته (ج)، وإلا لما يتناول كل كاتب إنسان، ولما ينتج الشكل الأول؛ لعدم الاندراج، ولا نعني ذات الموضوع، أي جزء له أو عرضيا، وإلا لما يتناول كل إنسان حيوان، ولم ينتج الشكل الأول، بل ما نعني ما يصدق عليه (ج) من الأفراد، صدقا ذاتيا أو عرضيا. وتلك الأفراد قد تكون حقيقية، وهي ما يكون خصوصيته من اعتبار المعتبر، سواء كان فردا أو حصة، وهي أيضا قد تسمى اعتبارية أو الأفراد الحقيقية شخصا أو صنفا أو نوعا، كالأفراد الشخصية أو النوعية وهذا أجود من الاعتبارية. قالوا: إن كان (ج) جنسا أو عرضا عاما أو فصلا بعيدا فالحكم عليها...
The second: that (ج) we do not mean by it its essence (of ج), otherwise 'every writer is human' would not apply, and the first figure would not produce a conclusion due to lack of subsumption. And we do not mean the essence of the subject, i.e., a part of it or an accident, otherwise 'every human is animal' would not apply, and the first figure would not produce a conclusion. But we mean what is true of (ج) from among the individuals, essentially or accidentally. And those individuals may be real (haqiqiyya), which are those whose particularity is from the consideration of the considerer, whether it is an individual or a share, and they are also sometimes called i'tibariyya, or the real individuals as individuals, classes, or types, like personal or specific individuals. And this is more precise than the i'tibariyya. They said: if (ج) is a genus, a general accident, or a remote differentia, then the judgment applies to...
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage defines the intended referent of the variable 'ج' in quantified propositions like 'كل ج ب'. The author rejects two incorrect interpretations. First, 'ج' does not mean the essence (haqiqa) of the predicate itself; if it did, then 'every writer is human' would be problematic because 'writer' (katib) is not the essence of 'human', and the first figure syllogism would fail due to lack of subsumption. Second, 'ج' does not mean the essence of the subject (dhat al-mawdu') or any part/accident thereof; if it did, 'every human is animal' would be problematic because 'animal' is not part of the subject's essence in that sense. Instead, 'ج' refers to what is truly predicated (yusdaq) of the individuals of the subject, whether essentially (dhatiyyan) or accidentally (aradiyyan). Then the author discusses that these individuals may be 'real' (haqiqiyya), meaning their particularity comes from the one considering them, whether they are individuals or shares. They may also be called 'i'tibariyya' (considered). The real individuals can be personal (shakhsiyya) or specific (naw'iyya). The author notes that the latter is more precise. Finally, he cites that if 'ج' is a genus, general accident, or remote differentia, the judgment applies to both personal and specific individuals.

Key Terms
ج
الأفراد الحقيقية
الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the variable 'ج' correctly refer to in 'كل ج ب'?
A) The essence of the predicate (haqiqat J)
B) The essence of the subject (dhat al-mawdu')
C) What is true of the individuals of the subject (ma yusdaq 'alayhi min al-afrad)
D) The collective whole of the subject

Key Takeaway
The predicate variable 'ج' in quantified propositions must be interpreted as applying to the individuals of the subject, not to the essence of the predicate or subject, to ensure valid syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'كل مسكر حرام' (Every intoxicant is forbidden), explain what the predicate variable 'ج' (here 'حرام') correctly refers to according to the passage.
?
Consider the syllogism: 'كل كاتب إنسان (Every writer is human), وكل إنسان ناطق (Every human is rational), إذن كل كاتب ناطق (Therefore every writer is rational).' Show how the correct interpretation of 'كاتب' as predicate in the first premise ensures subsumption and a valid conclusion in the first figure.

The Correct Meaning of the Middle Term (ج)
Establishes the correct interpretation of (ج) as individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct interpretation of the middle term (ج) in a quantified proposition and distinguish it from erroneous interpretations.

Core Idea
The middle term is not the essence; it is the set of individuals the subject falls under.

In this passage, the author clarifies what exactly the middle term (ج) means in a syllogistic proposition. Many beginners mistakenly take it to mean the essence of the subject or a part of it. The author shows that both errors break the first figure. Instead, (ج) should be understood as the individuals that the term (ج) is true of, whether essentially or accidentally. This correct interpretation ensures the validity of first-figure syllogisms used in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Reject the essence interpretation
Note the first error: interpreting (ج) as the essence of the subject. The author says this would exclude 'every writer' if (ج) meant 'essence of man', because 'writer' is accidental. This would break subsumption. Understand why this fails.
2
Reject the part/accident interpretation
The second error: interpreting (ج) as a part or accident of the subject. This would break 'every man is an animal' because 'animal' is not a part of 'man'. Again, subsumption fails.
3
Adopt the individuals interpretation
The correct view: (ج) means the individuals that (ج) is true of, whether essentially (like 'animal' of 'man') or accidentally (like 'writer' of 'man'). This preserves subsumption and first-figure validity.
This aligns with the concept of predication (haml) in Arabic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن (ج) لا نعني بها ما حقيقته (ج)، وإلا لما يتناول «كل كاتب» المراد بـ(ج) هو ذات إنسان، ولما ينتج الشكل الأول؛ لعدم الاندراج. ولا نعني بـ(د) هو عنوان الموضوع، أي ولا به، أي بـ(ج)، بأن يكون جزءا له أو عرضيا، وإلا لما يتناول كل إنسان حيوان، ولم ينتج الشكل الأول. بل ما نعني يصدق عليه (ج) من الأفراد، صدقا ذاتيا أو عرضيا. وتلك الأفراد قد تكون حقيقية، وهي ما يكون خصوصيته من اعتبار المعتبر، سواء كان فردا أو حصة، وهي أيضا قد تسمى اعتبارية أو الحقيقية شخصا أو صنفا أو نوعا، كالأفراد الشخصية أو النوعية. وهذا أجود مما قالوا: إن كان (ج) جنسا أو عرضا عاما أو فصلا بعيدا فالحكم عليها جميعا، وإن كان نوعا أو خاصة أو فصلا قريبا فالحكم على الأشخاص فقط؛ ليدخل فيه نحو كل نوع كذا، ويكون مفهوم القضية منطبقا على سائر القضايا. وحينئذ لا حاجة إلى ما أجاب به شارح «المطالع»، من أن الكلام في القضايا المستعملة في الحكمة، ومورد النقض خارج عما نحن فيه. وقد تكون اعتبارية، وهي ما خصوصيته بمجرد الاعتبار، كالحيوان الجنس؛ فإنه أخص من مطلق الحيوان، وبهذا دفع الشيخ النقض عن الكلية القائلة بأن شيئا إذا حمل على شيء، وحمل هذا الشيء على ثالث فيجب حمل الأول على الثالث، بأن الجنس محمول على الحيوان، وهو على زيد، أن الجنس لا يحمل على زيد بأن الحيوان المحمول عليه الجنس أخص من مطلق الحيوان، والمحمول على زيد نفس الحيوان، فلم يتكرر الواسطة.
Second: That (ج) we do not mean by it what its essence is (ج), otherwise it would not include 'every writer' when what is meant by (ج) is the essence of man, and the first figure would not produce a conclusion due to lack of subsumption. And we do not mean by (D) it is the title of the subject, i.e., (nor) by it, i.e., by (ج), that it is a part of it or an accident, otherwise it would not include 'every man is an animal', and the first figure would not produce a conclusion. Rather, we mean by it what (ج) is true of from among the individuals, true essentially or accidentally. And those individuals may be real, which have their particularity from the consideration of the one who considers, whether they are an individual or a portion, and they are also sometimes called conceptual or real as person, class, or type, such as personal or specific individuals. And this is better than what they said: If (ج) is a genus or a universal accident or a remote differentia, then the judgment applies to all; if it is a species or a property or a proximate differentia, then the judgment applies only to persons; so that it includes things like 'every type is such', and the concept of the proposition becomes applicable to all propositions. Then there is no need for the response of the commentator of al-Matali' that the discussion is only about propositions used in philosophy, and the point of refutation is outside our scope. And they may be conceptual, which have particularity merely by consideration, such as 'animal as a genus'; for it is more specific than absolute animal, and by this the author refuted the objection against the universal proposition that if something is predicated of something, and that something is predicated of a third, then the first must be predicated of the third, because the genus is predicated of animal, and it is predicated of Zayd, that the genus is not predicated of Zayd, because the animal of which the genus is predicated is more specific than absolute animal, and what is predicated of Zayd is animal itself, so the middle term is not repeated.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a detailed clarification of the meaning of the middle term (ج) in quantified propositions. The author first rejects two extreme interpretations: that (ج) means the essence of the subject, and that (ج) means a part or accident of the subject. Both would prevent the first figure from producing a conclusion. The correct interpretation is that (ج) denotes the individuals of which (ج) is true, whether essentially or accidentally. The passage then distinguishes between real individuals (which have particularity from the perspective of the observer) and conceptual individuals (which are purely mental considerations). The author endorses the view that the type of (ج) determines the scope of judgment: if (ج) is a genus or universal, judgment applies to all individuals including types; if it is a species or property, judgment applies only to particular persons. This avoids the need for an artificial restriction to 'philosophical propositions' as some commentators suggested. Finally, the passage gives an example involving 'animal as a genus' to show how conceptual individuals resolve an apparent violation of the transitivity of predication in categorical syllogisms.

Key Terms
(ج)
حمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the middle term (ج) correctly refer to?
A) The essence of the subject
B) A part or accident of the subject
C) The individuals of which (ج) is true
D) The predicate of the major premise

Key Takeaway
The middle term denotes the set of individuals that satisfy it, not the essence of the subject nor a part of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the proposition 'Every intoxicant is forbidden', what does the middle term 'intoxicant' refer to? Explain using the individuals interpretation.
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' Identify why the essence interpretation would break this syllogism.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Real and Conceptual Individuals
Classifies individuals and solves the transitivity puzzle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish real from conceptual individuals and apply the distinction to resolve apparent violations of syllogistic rules.

Core Idea
Conceptual individuals preserve middle term identity across predication shifts.

The passage next divides individuals into real (haqiqiyya) and conceptual (i'tibariyya). Real individuals have their particularity from external reality; conceptual individuals are mentally posited, like 'animal as a genus'. This distinction is crucial for a puzzle: if the genus is predicated of animal, and animal is predicated of Zayd, does the genus then apply to Zayd? The answer depends on whether 'animal' in the two premises is the same conceptual individual. Understanding this saves the transitivity of predication.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification
The author says individuals may be real (حقيقية) or conceptual (اعتبارية). Real individuals have particularity from the observer's consideration and include persons, classes, or types. Conceptual individuals are purely mental, like 'the genus animal' as a universal considered in itself.
Real individuals are further subdivided into personal (shakhsiyya), class (sinfiyya), or type (naw'iyya).
2
Apply to the transitivity puzzle
Consider the claim: if A is predicated of B, and B of C, then A must be predicated of C. A counterexample: 'animal as genus' is predicated of 'animal' (the absolute), and 'animal' is predicated of Zayd, but 'animal as genus' is not predicated of Zayd. The resolution: 'animal' in the first premise is a conceptual individual (the genus), while in the second it is a real individual (the species). They are different individuals, so the middle term is not repeated.
This shows the importance of distinguishing real vs conceptual individuals.
3
Contrast with the commentator's response
The author notes that some commentators restrict the discussion to philosophical propositions to avoid this puzzle. But the conceptual individual approach is better because it applies to all propositions without artificial restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن (ج) لا نعني بها ما حقيقته (ج)، وإلا لما يتناول «كل كاتب» المراد بـ(ج) هو ذات إنسان، ولما ينتج الشكل الأول؛ لعدم الاندراج. ولا نعني بـ(د) هو عنوان الموضوع، أي ولا به، أي بـ(ج)، بأن يكون جزءا له أو عرضيا، وإلا لما يتناول كل إنسان حيوان، ولم ينتج الشكل الأول. بل ما نعني يصدق عليه (ج) من الأفراد، صدقا ذاتيا أو عرضيا. وتلك الأفراد قد تكون حقيقية، وهي ما يكون خصوصيته من اعتبار المعتبر، سواء كان فردا أو حصة، وهي أيضا قد تسمى اعتبارية أو الحقيقية شخصا أو صنفا أو نوعا، كالأفراد الشخصية أو النوعية. وهذا أجود مما قالوا: إن كان (ج) جنسا أو عرضا عاما أو فصلا بعيدا فالحكم عليها جميعا، وإن كان نوعا أو خاصة أو فصلا قريبا فالحكم على الأشخاص فقط؛ ليدخل فيه نحو كل نوع كذا، ويكون مفهوم القضية منطبقا على سائر القضايا. وحينئذ لا حاجة إلى ما أجاب به شارح «المطالع»، من أن الكلام في القضايا المستعملة في الحكمة، ومورد النقض خارج عما نحن فيه. وقد تكون اعتبارية، وهي ما خصوصيته بمجرد الاعتبار، كالحيوان الجنس؛ فإنه أخص من مطلق الحيوان، وبهذا دفع الشيخ النقض عن الكلية القائلة بأن شيئا إذا حمل على شيء، وحمل هذا الشيء على ثالث فيجب حمل الأول على الثالث، بأن الجنس محمول على الحيوان، وهو على زيد، أن الجنس لا يحمل على زيد بأن الحيوان المحمول عليه الجنس أخص من مطلق الحيوان، والمحمول على زيد نفس الحيوان، فلم يتكرر الواسطة.
Second: That (ج) we do not mean by it what its essence is (ج), otherwise it would not include 'every writer' when what is meant by (ج) is the essence of man, and the first figure would not produce a conclusion due to lack of subsumption. And we do not mean by (D) it is the title of the subject, i.e., (nor) by it, i.e., by (ج), that it is a part of it or an accident, otherwise it would not include 'every man is an animal', and the first figure would not produce a conclusion. Rather, we mean by it what (ج) is true of from among the individuals, true essentially or accidentally. And those individuals may be real, which have their particularity from the consideration of the one who considers, whether they are an individual or a portion, and they are also sometimes called conceptual or real as person, class, or type, such as personal or specific individuals. And this is better than what they said: If (ج) is a genus or a universal accident or a remote differentia, then the judgment applies to all; if it is a species or a property or a proximate differentia, then the judgment applies only to persons; so that it includes things like 'every type is such', and the concept of the proposition becomes applicable to all propositions. Then there is no need for the response of the commentator of al-Matali' that the discussion is only about propositions used in philosophy, and the point of refutation is outside our scope. The Famous Doubt on Predication and Its Self-Refutation
Author presents doubt and self-refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical structure of the doubt against predication and explain why it is self-refuting.

Core Idea
The doubt that predication is impossible is itself a predication, making it self-defeating.

This passage presents a classic skeptical challenge: how can a predicate be united with a subject if they are either identical or different? The doubt leads to a paradox, but the author shows that the very statement of the doubt undermines itself. The exchange also includes a conditional syllogism used by the doubter to support his claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt
The doubter argues that predication is impossible because the predicate (ج) is either identical to the subject (ب) or different from it. If identical, the predication is uninformative (tautology); if different, the union required for predication is impossible. Thus, predication cannot occur.
This is a dilemma: both alternatives lead to a problem.
2
Recognize the first refutation
The author immediately points out that the statement 'predication is impossible' itself involves a predication (the predicate 'impossible' is ascribed to 'predication'). Therefore, the objection is self-refuting: it denies predication while using it.
This is a classic ad hominem argument, but logically sound.
3
Examine the doubter's retreat
The doubter responds that his claim is a negative proposition (saliba), not an affirmative predication. He says 'predication does not exist' instead of 'predication is impossible', hoping to avoid the charge.
However, even a negative proposition is a predication (the nonexistence is predicated).
4
Analyze the conditional syllogism
The doubter then formalizes his argument as a conditional syllogism (modus tollens): If predication were possible, then the predicate is either identical or different; in either case, the result is either uninformative or impossible; but we know that informative predications exist (the consequent is false), so the antecedent (that predication is possible) must be false. This is an attempt to prove the impossibility of predication.
This argument also implicitly uses predication in its formulation, so the self-refutation charge may still apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْ هَهُنَا اعْتُبِرَ فِي التَنَاقُضِ اتَّحَادُ نَحْوِ الْحَمْلِ فَوْقَ الْوَحْدَاتِ الثَمَانِ الذَائِعَاتِ ؛ الصدق الموجبة الأولية والسالبة الشائعة، نحو «الجزئي جزئي» و «الجزئي ليس بجزئي» . وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ مَشْهُورٌ ، وَهُوَ : أَنَّ الْحَمْلَ مُحَالٌ ؛ لِأَنَّ مَنْهُومَ ج عَيْنْ مَنْهُوم ب أَوْ غَيْرُهُ، وَالْعَيْنِيَّةُ تُنَافِي الْمُغَايَرَةَ، وَالْمُغَايَرَةُ تُنَافِي الاتحاد . ولا بد للحمل منهما . وأجيب بأن هذا القول مبطل لنفسه؛ لأن فيه أيضا حملا، فعاد السائل بأن الدعوى سالبة، أي الحمل ليس موجودا، بدليل قياس استثنائي . ويقال: لو كان ممكنا فإما أن يكون ج عين ب أو غيره، وكلما كان كذلك فإما غير مفيد أو ممتنع، والتالي باطل، فالمقدم مثله.
From here, in contradiction, unity of the mode of predication is considered in addition to the eight well-known unities: the truth of the primary affirmative and the common negative, such as 'the particular is particular' and 'the particular is not particular'. And here is a famous doubt, which is that predication is impossible, because concept J is either identical to concept B or other than it; identity precludes difference, and difference precludes union. And predication necessarily requires both. And it was answered: This statement refutes itself because it too contains a predication. Then the questioner returned: The claim is a negative proposition, i.e., predication does not exist, by means of a conditional syllogism. And it is said: If it were possible, then either J is identical to B or other, and whenever that is the case, it is either not informative or impossible, and the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents a classic dialectical exchange: a doubt (shakk) against the very possibility of predication (al-haml) and its refutation. The doubt argues that in any predication, the predicate (J) is either identical to the subject (B) or different. If identical, the predication is not informative (for it is mere tautology); if different, union is impossible because difference precludes identity. Thus predication is impossible. The answer (jawab) first shows that the doubt is self-refuting, because the statement 'predication is impossible' is itself a predication. The doubter then retreats, claiming the statement is a negative proposition (saliba) that does not affirm existence, thus avoiding the charge. The refutation then uses a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i): if predication were possible, then either identity or difference would obtain, leading to either uninformativeness or impossibility; both are false (since we do have informative predications), so the antecedent (that predication is possible) is false by modus tollens. The example used in the context of contradiction involves 'the particular is particular' (primary affirmative) and 'the particular is not particular' (common negative), which are contradictory because they share the same subject and predicate but differ in quantity and mode. The discussion of contradiction conditions (eight unities) is background: here the unity of mode of predication (i.e., primary vs. common) is added.

Key Terms
شك مشهور
الحمل
قياس استثنائي
ج
ب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The doubt incorrectly assumes that identity is required for predication
B) The doubt itself uses predication, so it is self-refuting
C) The doubt confuses primary and common predication
D) The doubt relies on an invalid conditional syllogism

Key Takeaway
The doubt against predication is self-refuting because it must use predication to deny predication.

Refutation of al-Tusi's Condition for Talazum
Author critiques al-Tusi's condition for talazum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the author's three objections to al-Tusi's requirement of a third locus for necessary concomitance between two effects.

Core Idea
A single efficient cause suffices for talazum; al-Tusi's third locus is unnecessary.

In his Sharh al-Isharat, Nasir al-Din al-Tusi stipulated that for two effects of a common cause to be necessarily concomitant, there must be a third locus (mafqud) that is the site of mutual need (iftiqar) between them. The author of this passage subjects that condition to a rigorous three-pronged critique, arguing that a single efficient cause (al-illa al-mujiba) is sufficient to guarantee concomitance, thereby refuting the need for an external locus. Let us unpack the logical structure of that critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Tusi's condition
First, locate al-Tusi's requirement. He said that for two effects (like two correlatives or two leaning bricks) to be necessarily concomitant, there must be a third thing that is the locus of need between them from both sides, in a non-circular manner. This is the claim being critiqued.
Al-Tusi's examples: true correlatives need each other's substrate; well-known correlatives have parts that need each other; leaning bricks have positional dependence.
2
Extract the author's first objection
The author argues that a single efficient cause for both effects is sufficient. He uses a syllogism: whenever one effect exists, its cause exists; whenever that cause exists, the other effect exists; therefore the effects are concomitant. This shows that no third locus is needed.
The author dismisses the counterargument about multiple aspects of the cause, saying it applies only to the complete cause, not to the efficient cause proper (the final part).
3
Extract the second objection
The author says that if the need-relation between the two effects is like an effect's need for its efficient cause, that relation itself suffices for concomitance. Then the third cause is irrelevant; otherwise, the need does not produce concomitance.
This is a conditional argument: either the need is sufficient (so third unnecessary) or it is not (so it doesn't help).
4
Extract the third objection
Even if we grant the need-relation, the third locus is supposed to cause that need. But the author notes that if the need already exists by itself, it's enough; the third is redundant.
This is a point about causal overdetermination: you don't need an extra cause for something already present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واشترط النصير الطوسي في شرح الإشارات» في تلازم معلولي ثالث أن يكون الثالث موقعا للافتقار بينهما من الجانبين على وجه غير دائر ، فقال في المتضايفين الحقيقيتين إن كل واحد منهما مفتقر إلى معروض الآخر، وفي المشهوريين : إن جزء كل واحد منهما - وهو الوصف - مفتقر إلى جزء ـ الآخر وهو الذات - وفي اللبنتين المنخبيتين) لا تلازم بين ذاتيهما، وإنما التلازم بين حفظ وضع كل منهما، وهما معلولان للالتقاء، ومفتقر كل منهما إلى معروض الآخر. وفيه نظر، أما أولا : فلأن استناد كل إلى علة واحدة كاف بالشكل : كلما تحقق أحد المعلولين تحقق علته، وكلما تحقق علته تحقق الآخر، ينتج: : كلما تحقق أحد المعلولين تحقق الآخر. وما قيل: إنه لا يمكن استناد أمرين إلى علة واحدة إلا عند تعدد الجهات فالجهة التي صدر عنها أحد المعلولين غير الجهة التي صدر عنها المعلول الآخر، فحينئذ لم يتكرر الأوسط غير سديد ؛ فإن ذلك إنما هو في العلة التامة لا الموجبة مطلقا، فمتى كانت العلة الموجبة التي هي الجزء الأخير حير من التامة واحدة تحقق التلازم قطعا من غير حاجة إلى الافتقار . وأما ثانيا: فلأن الارتباط الافتقاري الذي لأحد المعلولين إلى الآخر إن كان كافتقار المعلول إلى العلة الموجبة فهو كاف في التلازم، ولا دخل لعلية الثالث لهما قطعا ، وإلا فلا يفيد ذلك الافتقار التلازم. وأما ثالثا: فلأنه لو سلم ذلك فلا دخل لإيقاع الثالث ذلك الافتقار قطعا ؛ إذ لو حصل ذلك الافتقار بنفسه لكفي. هذه الأسئلة أوردها الجامع بين المعارف الإلهية والعلوم الفلسفية أبي، قدس سره، والله أعلم.
Al-Tusi in his Sharh al-Isharat stipulated for the concomitance of two effects of a third that the third be a locus of need between them from both sides in a non-circular manner. He said regarding true correlatives that each one is in need of the substrate of the other, and regarding well-known correlatives that a part of each one—namely the attribute—is in need of a part of the other—namely the essence. And in the two intertwined bricks, there is no concomitance between their essences, rather the concomitance is between the preservation of the position of each, and they are two effects of the meeting, and each is in need of the substrate of the other. But there is a problem with this. First: because the dependence of each on a single cause is sufficient in the form: whenever one effect is realized, its cause is realized, and whenever its cause is realized, the other is realized, resulting: whenever one effect is realized, the other is realized. And what was said—that it is not possible for two things to depend on a single cause except when there are multiple aspects, so the aspect from which one effect emanates is different from the aspect from which the other emanates, and thus the middle term is not repeated—is not sound. For that is only in the complete cause, not in the efficient cause absolutely. So when the efficient cause, which is the final part of the complete cause, is one, concomitance is realized necessarily without need for need. Second: because the need-related connection of one effect to the other, if it is like the need of an effect for its efficient cause, is sufficient for concomitance, and the causality of the third for them plays no role absolutely; otherwise that need does not produce concomitance. Third: because even if that is granted, the third's causing that need plays no role absolutely; for if that need were realized by itself, it would suffice. These questions were posed by the compiler of divine gnoses and philosophical sciences, Abi, may his secret be sanctified, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of Nasir al-Din al-Tusi's condition for necessary concomitance (talazum) between two effects of a common cause. Al-Tusi, in his commentary on Ibn Sina's al-Isharat, had required that a third locus (mafqud) be the site of mutual need between the two effects to ensure non-circular concomitance. He illustrated this with examples: true correlatives (like fatherhood and sonship) each need the other's substrate; well-known correlatives (like blackness and whiteness) each have a part (the attribute) needing a part of the other (the substance); and two bricks leaning against each other have no inherent concomitance but rely on their positions. The author then rebuts with three objections. First, he argues that a single efficient cause suffices to produce concomitance by a simple syllogism: whenever one effect exists, its cause exists, and whenever that cause exists, the other effect exists—thus the two effects are necessarily concomitant. The counterargument about multiple aspects of the cause is dismissed because it applies only to the complete cause, not the efficient cause proper; an efficient cause that is the final part of the complete cause is unitary and directly entails both effects. Second, the need-relation between the two effects, if it resembles the effect's dependence on its cause, is itself sufficient for concomitance, making a third cause irrelevant. Third, even if the need-relation exists, a third thing is unnecessary because the need itself would be enough. The passage ends by attributing these objections to the compiler (al-jami' bayn al-ma'arif) and invoking divine knowledge.

Key Terms
التلازم (al-talāzum): Necessary concomitance between two things, here between two effects of a common cause. [Note: In earlier logic, talazum often referred to logical implication; here it is the mutual necessity of existence.]
الافتقار (al-iftiqār): Need or dependence, here the relation between two effects that ensures their concomitance. [Note: Not a standard term in earlier logic; introduced by al-Tusi in this context.]
العلة الموجبة (al-ʿilla al-mūjiba): The efficient cause that necessitates its effect; the final part of the complete cause. [Note: Distinguished from the complete cause (al-ʿilla al-tāmma); this distinction is specific to this critique.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is sufficient to establish necessary concomitance between two effects?
A) A mutual need-relation between them.
B) A single efficient cause for both.
C) A third locus of need.
D) A complete cause with multiple aspects.

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that necessary concomitance between two effects is sufficiently established by a single efficient cause, rendering al-Tusi's third locus condition superfluous.

Using the author's first objection as a model, construct a syllogism that shows how a single attribute of Allah (e.g., omnipotence) can be the efficient cause for both the creation of the world and its sustenance, thereby establishing their necessary concomitance without requiring a third locus.

Condition of causal relation for entailment
Author states the requirement of a causal relation for entailment between co-necessitants with different origins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a causal relation is required for entailment between two things and explain the rationale using abstractive attributes.

Core Idea
Entailment between co-necessitants requires a causal relation only when they do not share a single origin.

In logic, we often encounter pairs of concepts that necessarily accompany each other. But not all entailments are the same. This passage from Bahr al-Ulum clarifies a subtle condition: the causal relation is only required when the two things have different origins. This is especially relevant for abstractive attributes derived from distinct acts or events.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two co-necessitants (mutalāzimān)
Look for a pair of things that are said to entail each other. In the passage, these are abstractive attributes derived from different origins.
2
Determine if they share a single origin (manšaʾ)
Check whether both abstractive attributes come from the same source or event. If they do, no causal relation is needed; if they don't, a causal relation is required.
The origin of an abstractive attribute is the event or act from which it is abstracted, e.g., 'created' from creation.
3
Apply the condition
If the origins differ, you must establish a causal link between the two things for entailment to hold. The passage says investigation necessitates this.
This is not a universal rule for all entailments, but specifically for co-necessitants with different origins.
4
Understand why: origin acts as cause
In abstractive attributes, the origin functions like a cause. If two attributes have different origins, a causal connection between the origins is needed for one attribute to entail the other.
Example: 'temporally originated' abstracted from temporal origination; 'contingent' abstracted from contingency. They have different origins, so entailment requires a causal relation.
5
Test with an example from Kalam
Consider 'temporally originated' (muhdath) and 'contingent' (mumkin). Both are abstractive attributes. Do they share a single origin? No: one from temporal origination, the other from possibility. So according to the passage, entailment between them requires a causal relation.
This shows the practical application of the rule in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة لمبحث الشرطيات ونحن إنما نشترط علاقة العلية بين المتلازمين الذين لا يكون منشأهما واحدا، وليس هذا تخصيصا للقواعد، بل الفحص يوجب ذلك والسر فيه أن المنشأ في الانتزاعيات قائم مقام العلة هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Continuation of the discussion on conditionals. And we only require a causal relationship between two mutually necessitating things that do not have a single source. This is not a specialization of the rules, but investigation necessitates that. And the secret in it is that the source in abstractive [attributes] stands in the place of the cause. This, and God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is a methodological clarification about the condition for entailment between two things that are co-necessitants (mutalāzimān). The author states that he requires a causal relation (ʿalāqat al-ʿilliyya) for their entailment only when the two things do not share a single origin (manšaʾ). This is not a blanket rule but is discovered through investigation. The reason is that in abstractive attributes (al-intizāʿiyyāt), the origin from which the attribute is abstracted functions as the cause. For example, the attribute 'created' is abstracted from the act of creation; the creation itself is the cause of the attribute. Thus, when two attributes are abstracted from different origins, a causal link is needed for one to entail the other. This passage guards against assuming that all logical entailments require a causal relation, clarifying that it applies only when the origins differ.

Key Takeaway
Entailment between co-necessitants requires a causal relation only when they do not share a single origin, because the origin functions as the cause for abstractive attributes.

Critique of Implication with Impossible Antecedents
Author critiques two views on when an impossible antecedent implies a consequent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the two views on implication with an impossible antecedent and identify the author's refined definition of istilzam.

Core Idea
Implication is defined by impossibility of separation, not by part-whole or causal relations.

In this passage, the author critiques two common views on what makes an impossible antecedent imply a consequent. You will learn why requiring a part-whole or causal relation is insufficient, and how the author defends a broader definition based on the impossibility of separation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first view
Find the claim that implication holds when the consequent is a part of the antecedent, e.g., 'If Zaid were a donkey, he would bray.'
This is the 'part-whole' view.
2
Understand the author's objection to the first view
The author calls it arbitrary because the essence of implication is the impossibility of separation (imtina' al-infikak) between two things, regardless of part-whole relationship.
The condition is extra and not inherent.
3
Identify the second view and its two interpretations
The second, more famous view requires a relation ('alaqa) between antecedent and consequent. The author distinguishes causal relation vs. a relation that prevents separation.
4
Analyze why causal relation fails
If 'alaqa means causal relation, it cannot apply to impossible antecedents, so nothing would follow from impossibilities. This is absurd, so the view is false.
5
Understand the acceptable sense of 'alaqa
If 'alaqa means the antecedent cannot be conceived without the consequent in existence, then it is true, but it does not require the consequent to be non-contradictory. The author argues that an impossible antecedent can relate to its contradictory in this way.
Thus, the claim that implication prevents contradiction is not straightforward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ زَعَمَ أَنَّ الإِسْتِلْرَامَ ثَابِتُ إِذَا كَانَ التَّالِي جُزْء لِلْمُقَدَّمِ، كَقَوْلِنَا: إِنْ كَانَ زَيْدٌ حِمَارًا كَانَ نَاهِقًا، وَذَلِكَ تَحَكُّمٌ؛ فَإِنَّ حَقِيقَةَ اللُّزُومِ امْتِنَاعُ بَيْنَ الشَّيْئَيْنِ، سَوَاءٌ كَانَ أَحَدُهُمَا جُزْءًا لِلْآخَرِ أَمْ لَا. الانْفِكَاكُ وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ زَعَمَ أَنَّهُ ثَابِتٌ إِذَا كَانَ بَيْنَهُمَا عَلَاقَةٌ، وَهُوَ الْأَشْهَرُ. إِنْ أَرَادَ هَذَا الزَّاعِمُ بِالْعَلَاقَةِ عَلَاقَةَ الْعِلِّيَّةِ فَذَلِكَ بَاطِلٌ قَطْعًا، كَيْفَ وَهَذِهِ الْعَلَاقَةُ لَا يُمْكِنُ تَحَقُّقُهَا فِي الْمُحَالِ، فَيَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمَ الْمُحَالُ شَيْئًا. وَإِنْ أَرَادَ بِهَا الْعَلَاقَةَ الَّتِي بِهَا يَأْبَى الْمُقَدَّمُ عَنِ الانْفِرَادِ عَنِ التَّالِي فِي الْوُجُودِ فَذَلِكَ حَقٌّ؛ لِأَنَّ الْمُحَالَ وَالْمُمْكِنَ مُتَسَاوِي الْقَدَمَيْنِ فِي ذَلِكَ، لَكِنْ لَا يَصِحُّ حِينَئِذٍ التَّفْرِيعُ الْمُشَارُ إِلَيْهِ بِقَوْلِهِ: وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالَ هَذَا الزَّاعِمُ: إِنَّ الْمُقَدَّمَ الْمُحَالَ يَجِبُ أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ مُنَافِيًا لِلتَّالِي؛ فَإِنَّ مِنَ الْجَائِزِ أَنْ يَكُونَ بَيْنَ الْمُحَالِ وَنَقِيضِهِ عَلَاقَةٌ، بِهَا يَأْبَى عَنْ فَرْضِ الْوُجُودِ إِلَّا مُسْتَصْحِبًا مَعَهُ بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى ذَاتِهِ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمُنَافَاةَ تُصَحِّحُ الانْفِكَاكَ، وَالْمُلَازَمَةَ تَمْنَعُهُ.
And among them is one who claimed that implication is established when the consequent is a part of the antecedent, such as our saying: 'If Zaid were a donkey, he would bray.' And that is arbitrary; for the reality of entailment is the impossibility of separation between two things, whether one is a part of the other or not. The separation. And among them is one who claimed that it is established when there is a relation between them, and this is the more famous. If this claimant means by 'relation' a causal relation, then that is absolutely false—how can this relation be realized in the impossible? So it would follow that the impossible does not imply anything. And if he means by it the relation by which the antecedent refrains from being alone without the consequent in existence, then that is true; because the impossible and the possible are equal in that regard. But then the division indicated by his saying: 'Hence this claimant said: The impossible antecedent must not be contradictory to the consequent' is not valid; for it is possible that there be a relation between the impossible and its contradictory, by which it refrains from being assumed to exist except accompanied by it with respect to its essence. For contradiction validates separation, and implication prevents it.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage critiques two opinions on the nature of implication (istilzam) in conditional propositions, specifically when the antecedent is impossible (muhal). The first opinion claims that implication holds if the consequent is part of the antecedent, e.g., 'If Zaid were a donkey, he would bray.' The author dismisses this as arbitrary (tahakkum), because the real meaning of entailment is the impossibility of separation (imtina' al-infikak) between two things, regardless of any part-whole relation. The second, more famous opinion holds that implication requires a relation ('alaqa) between antecedent and consequent. The author breaks this down: if 'relation' means causal relation ('illiyya), it is false because impossible things cannot have real relations, so an impossible antecedent would imply nothing—which is absurd. If 'relation' means the relation that prevents the antecedent from being conceived without the consequent in existence, then it is true, but the consequent cannot then be contradictory to the antecedent. The author argues that an impossible antecedent can relate to its contradictory in a way that prevents it from being assumed without it, so contradiction (which permits separation) is not automatically excluded. Thus, the claim that implication prevents contradiction is not straightforward.

استلزام
مقدم
تالي
علاقة
منافاة
انفكاك

A) Because it ignores the impossibility of the antecedent
B) Because the real nature of implication is the impossibility of separation between two things, regardless of part-whole relationship
C) Because it requires a causal relation which cannot exist for impossible things
D) Because it leads to an infinite regress

Key Takeaway
The true nature of implication is the impossibility of separation, not any specific relation like part-whole or causality.

The Reductio Argument and Its Validity in Mental vs. Real Implication
Author examines the reductio argument and argues it only proves impossibility in mental conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mental and real implication in reductio arguments and evaluate their validity using the example from kalam.

Core Idea
Reductio proves impossibility in mental conception, not necessarily in external reality.

Reductio arguments (qiyas khalfi) are common in Islamic logic and kalam, but their validity depends on where the implication holds. The author of this passage critically examines a reductio and argues that it only shows impossibility in the mind, not necessarily in the external world. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying reductio in theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reductio structure
Find the conditional statement: 'If a substance with position were indivisible, it would be divisible.' Note the antecedent and consequent.
2
Recognize the logical operation
The consequent is false (the substance is not divisible), so by modus tollens the antecedent must be false. This is a valid reductio ad absurdum.
The form is: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
3
Analyze the author's critique
The author argues that the implication (luzum) in this reductio is only mental (dhihni), not real (khariji). The impossibility derived is that the supposition leads to a contradiction in the mind, but this does not prove that the supposition cannot exist externally.
The key distinction is between tahaqquq dhihni and tahaqquq khariji.
4
Apply the distinction to evaluate validity
Determine whether a reductio argument you encounter relies on mental or real necessity. If the necessity is mental, the conclusion only applies to our conception, not to external existence.
This affects how reductio is used in kalam proofs for God's existence or the world's temporality.

Source Text

وَفِيهِ: أَنَّ حَاصِلَ ذَلِكَ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى قَضِيَّتَيْنِ رُومِيَّتَيْنِ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ، تَالِي أَحَدِهِمَا نَقِيضُ تَالِي الْأُخْرَى، وَالْخَصْمُ لَا يُسَلِّمُ الْمُنَافَاةَ بَيْنَهُمَا. وَكَيْفَ يُسَلِّمُ؟ فَإِنَّ غَايَةَ مَا لَزِمَ مِنْهُ اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ مُقَدَّمٍ مُحَالٍ، وَلَا اسْتِحَالَةَ فِيهِ. ثُمَّ إِنَّ أَدَلَّ الدَّلِيلِ عَلَى بُطْلَانِ هَذَا التَّفْرِيعِ أُرِيدَ هُنَاكَ تَبْيِينُ أَنَّ الْمُسْتَحِيلَ لَوْ كَانَ وَاقِعًا كَانَ عَدَمُهُ وَاقِعًا، وَلَوْ كَانَ الْمُتَحَقِّقُ هُوَ كَانَ نَقِيضُهُ مُتَحَقِّقًا، فَذَلِكَ مِنَ الْأَبَاطِيلِ الْفَاسِدَةِ، لَا يُبْتَنَى عَلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ، بَلْ لَوْ وَقَعَتْ تِلْكَ الْمَفْرُوضَاتُ لَوَقَعَتْ هِيَ بَتَّةً دُونَ نَقِيضِهَا. وَإِنْ أُرِيدَ أَنَّهُ يُبَيِّنُ بِالْبَيَانَاتِ أَنَّهُ لَوْ فُرِضَتِ الْمَوَادُّ كَانَ هُنَاكَ مَا يُسَاقُ إِلَى أَنَّهُ غَيْرُ مُطَابِقٍ، مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ فَرْضُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ، فَذَلِكَ مَا يَرُومُهُ الرَّائِمُونَ، وَلَيْسَ فِيهِ اسْتِلْزَامُ شَيْءٍ لِنَقِيضِهِ، بَلِ امْتِنَاعُهُ لِكَوْنِهِ فِي قُوَّةِ النَّقِيضَيْنِ. وَبِالْجُمْلَةِ الَّذِي يُقَالُ فِي الْقِيَاسَاتِ الْخَلْفِيَّةِ أَنَّا لَوْ تَصَوَّرْنَا ذَلِكَ الشَّيْءَ وَفَرَضْنَاهُ لَعَلِمْنَا تَحَقُّقَ عَدَمِهِ، لَا أَنَّهُ لَوْ تَحَقَّقَ لَتَحَقَّقَ عَدَمُهُ، وَهُمَا بِمَا هُمَا مَتَمَثِّلَانِ فِي الْعَقْلِ، لَيْسَا بِمُسْتَحِيلَيْنِ، بَلْ مِنَ الْمُمْكِنَاتِ الْعَامَّةِ. ثُمَّ تَبَيَّنَ الْمَلْزُومُ لَيْسَ عُنْوَانًا لِشَيْءٍ مِنَ الْحَقَائِقِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ؛ لِكَوْنِ لَازِمِهِ كَذَلِكَ، فَرَجَعَ إِلَى الِاسْتِدْلَالِ بِانْتِفَاءِ اللَّازِمِ عَلَى انْتِفَاءِ الْمَلْزُومِ فَمَرْدُودٌ بِمَا قِيلَ: إِنَّهُ إِنْ أَرَادَ أَنَّ اسْتِلْزَامَ الْمُقَدَّمِ لِنَقِيضِهِ لَيْسَ ضَرُورِيًّا، بَلْ إِنَّمَا يَظْهَرُ بِالْبُرْهَانِ، فَذَلِكَ غَيْرُ مَا نَحْنُ بِصَدَدِهِ، وَإِنْ أَرَادَ أَنَّ لَيْسَ فِي الْوَاقِعِ لُزُومٌ، وَإِنَّمَا اللُّزُومُ فِي مَضَرِّ التَّحَقُّقِ الذِّهْنِيِّ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْهُ اسْتِحَالَةُ تَحَقُّقِهِ الْخَارِجِيِّ؛ لِاسْتِحَالَةِ تَحَقُّقِ لَازِمِهِ لِعَدَمِ كَوْنِهِ لَازِمًا فِي الْوَاقِعِ، فَحِينَئِذٍ بَطَلَ الْقِيَاسُ الْخَلْفِيُّ.
And in it: That the sum of that reduces to two universal affirmative premises, the consequent of one being the contradictory of the consequent of the other, and the opponent does not concede the contradiction between them. And how can he concede? For the utmost that follows from it is the conjunction of two contradictories given an impossible antecedent, and there is no impossibility in that. Then, the strongest evidence for the falsity of this division is the reality of reductio arguments, as is said: 'If a substance with position were indivisible, it would be divisible,' and the consequent is false. And what was said in 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin': If what is intended there is to show that if the impossible occurred, its non-existence would occur, and if the actual were so, its contradictory would be actual, then that is from the false invalidities—nothing is built upon it. Rather, if those suppositions occurred, they would occur absolutely without their contradictory. And if what is intended is that it is shown by demonstrations that if the materials were supposed, there would be something leading to its being non-conforming, from the perspective that it is a supposition of two contradictories, then that is what the seekers aim for, and there is no implication of something for its contradictory in it, but rather its impossibility due to being in the power of two contradictories. And in summary, what is said about reductio arguments is that if we conceive of that thing and suppose it, we know the realization of its non-existence, not that if it existed, its non-existence would exist. And they, as they are represented in the mind, are not impossible, but rather from the general possibles. Then the entailed (malzum) is not a title for any of the real realities, because its necessary consequent (lazim) is like that. So it returns to inference by the non-existence of the necessary consequent from the non-existence of the entailed, and it is refuted by what was said: If he means that the implication of the antecedent for its contradictory is not necessary, but rather appears by proof, then that is not what we are about. And if he means that there is no implication in reality, but rather the implication is in the domain of mental realization, then the impossibility of its external realization does not follow from it, because of the impossibility of the realization of its necessary consequent due to its not being a necessary consequent in reality. So then the reductio argument is invalid.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage critically examines reductio arguments (qiyas khalfi). The author begins by noting that the earlier opinion reduces to two universal affirmative premises where the consequent of one is the contradictory of the consequent of the other. The opponent does not accept the contradiction between them, because at most it gives a conjunction of contradictories given an impossible antecedent, which is not itself impossible. The strongest proof against the earlier view is the existence of valid reductio arguments, e.g., 'If a substance with position (jawhar dhu wad') were indivisible, it would be divisible'—the consequent is false, so the antecedent must be false. But the author then discusses a critique from 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin': if the reductio is intended to show that if the impossible occurred, its non-existence would occur (i.e., 'if it existed, it would not exist'), that is nonsense. Rather, the suppositions would occur just as they are supposed, not their contradictories. Alternatively, if the reductio shows by demonstration that the supposition leads to a contradiction (i.e., being non-conforming), then it is a valid inference to the impossibility of the antecedent, but not because the antecedent implies its own contradictory—rather, the impossibility is due to the supposition containing a contradiction in itself. The author concludes that in reductio arguments, we suppose the thing and thereby know its non-existence (in mental representation), not that if it existed externally, its non-existence would follow. The mental representation of both the thing and its non-existence are possible; the inference is from the non-existence of the necessary consequent (lazim) to the non-existence of the entailed (malzum). But if the implication is only mental, not real, then the reductio is invalid for external existence. Thus the author defends the validity of reductio in mental reasoning but limits its application to real existence.

The reductio argument is valid in mental reasoning but does not necessarily establish impossibility in external reality if the implication is only mental.
