Critique of Reductio ad Absurdum (Qiyas Khalfi)
Author critiques the validity of reductio arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key objections to reductio ad absurdum arguments and evaluate their validity using the author's criteria.

Core Idea
Reductio arguments are invalid when they treat mental impossibilities as real entailments.

In this lesson, we examine a critical passage where the author systematically dismantles the validity of reductio ad absurdum (qiyas khalfi). While reductio is a common tool in kalam and logic, the author argues that it often relies on a mistaken notion of entailment. We will walk through his argument step by step, formalize the example he provides, and understand why he considers reductio to be invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reductio example
Locate the example in the Arabic: 'If an indivisible substance with position were divisible, then it would be divided.' Recognize this as a conditional statement.
This example is drawn from debates about atomism in kalam.
2
Understand the author's refutation of a naive interpretation
The author dismisses the idea that supposing an impossibility entails its non-existence. He distinguishes between 'if we conceive it, we know its non-existence' versus 'if it were verified, its non-existence would be verified'.
The author says: 'if we conceive that thing and suppose it, we know the verification of its non-existence; not that if it were verified, its non-existence would be verified.'
3
Examine the entailment claim
The passage argues that the supposed impossible and its non-existence are both mental possibilities, not real. The author says the impossibility is due to 'being in the force of two contradictories', not true entailment.
The author writes: 'the two, as they are represented in the mind, are not impossible; rather they are among general possibilities.'
4
Analyze the reduction to modus tollens
The author shows that reductio reduces to inference from non-existence of consequent to non-existence of antecedent, but then refutes this by distinguishing between necessary and mental entailment.
If the entailment is only mental, then no real impossibility follows for the antecedent.
5
Synthesize the final verdict
Conclude that the author rejects reductio as invalid because it confuses mental representation with real logical necessity.
The phrase 'therefore the reductio argument becomes invalid' appears at the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن أدلَّ الدليل على بطلان هذا التفريع تحقق القياسات الخلفية، كما يقال: لو كان جوهر ذو وضع غير منقسم لكان منقسما، والتالي باطل. وما قال في الأفق المبين»: إنه إن أريد هناك تبيين أن المستحيل لو كان واقعا كان عدمه واقعا ولو كان المتحقق هو كان نقيضه متحققا، فذلك من الأباطيل الفاسدة، لا يبتنى عليه شيء، بل لو وقعت تلك المفروضات لوقعت هي بتية دون نقيضها. وإن أريد أنه يبين بالبيانات أنه لو فرض المواد كان هناك ما يسوق إلى أنه غير مطابق، من حيث إنه فرض النقيضين، فذلك ما يرومه الرائمون، وليس فيه استلزام شيء لنقيضه، بل امتناعه لكونه في قوة النقيضين . وبالجملة الذي يقال في القياسات الخلفية أنا لو تصورنا ذلك الشيء وفرضناه لعلمنا تحقق عدمه، لا أنه لو تحقق لتحقق عدمه، وهما بما هما أن متمثلان في العقل، ليسا بمستحيلين، بل من الممكنات العامة. ثم تبين الملزوم ليس عنوانا لشيء من الحقائق الواقعية؛ لكون لازمه كذلك، فرجع إلى الاستدلال بانتفاء اللازم على انتفاء الملزوم فمردود بما قيل: إنه إن أراد أن استلزام المقدم لنقيضه ليس ضروريا، بل إنما يظهر بالبرهان، فذلك غير لما نحن بصدده، وإن أراد أن ليس في الواقع لزوم، وإنما اللزوم في مضر التحقق الذهني، فلا يلزم منه استحالة تحققه الخارجي ؛ لاستحالة تحقق لازمه لعدم كونه لازما في الواقع، فحينئذ بطل القياس الخلفي. هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب .
Then indeed the strongest evidence for the invalidity of this division is the verification of reductio arguments, as it is said: 'If an indivisible substance with position were divisible, then it would be divided, and the consequent is false.' And what was said in 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin': If by that it is intended to show that if the impossible were actual, then its non-existence would be actual, and if the actual were such, then its contradictory would be actual, then that is among the false falsehoods; nothing is built upon it. Rather, if those suppositions occurred, they would occur as they are without their contradictories. And if by that it is intended that it is shown by proofs that if the materials were supposed, there would be something driving to its being non-correspondent, due to its being a supposition of two contradictories, then that is what the aspirants seek, and there is no entailment of something by its contradictory; rather its impossibility is due to its being in the force of the two contradictories. And in summary, what is said about reductio arguments is that if we conceive that thing and suppose it, we know the verification of its non-existence; not that if it were verified, its non-existence would be verified. And the two, as they are represented in the mind, are not impossible; rather they are among general possibilities. Then it becomes clear that the entailed thing is not a title for any of the real realities because its entailer is likewise; thus it reduces to inference from the non-existence of the consequent to the non-existence of the antecedent. It is refuted by what was said: If by that is meant that the entailment of the antecedent to its contradictory is not necessary but only appears through demonstration, then that is not relevant to our concern. And if by that is meant that there is no entailment in reality, but rather entailment is in the arena of mental verification, then the impossibility of its external verification does not follow from the impossibility of the verification of its consequent due to its not being a real consequent. Therefore the reductio argument becomes invalid. This, and God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage critically examines the validity of reductio ad absurdum (qiyas khalfi) arguments in Islamic logic. The author begins by stating that the strongest proof against a certain division (likely a previous classification of syllogisms) is the existence of reductio arguments themselves. He provides an example: 'If an indivisible substance with position were divisible, then it would be divided' — but the consequent is false, so the antecedent must be false. However, he then refutes a common interpretation of reductio. The author argues that if one intends by reductio to show that if the impossible were actual, then its non-existence would be actual, this is nonsense. Impossible suppositions do not entail their contradictories; they remain as they are, independent of their contraries. He distinguishes between two understandings: (1) an attempt to prove that the impossible entails its contradictory, which is false; (2) an attempt to show by proof that supposing certain premises leads to a contradiction, which is a legitimate method but not a strict entailment of a thing by its contradictory. The core critique is that reductio arguments incorrectly assume that from the supposition of an impossible state of affairs, one can infer the actual impossibility or possibility of something else. In reality, when we conceive and suppose an impossible thing, we may know that it does not exist, but this does not mean that if it existed, its non-existence would exist. The two (the impossible and its non-existence) are both present in the mind and are not impossible; they are merely general possibilities. The argument then turns to the claim that the entailed thing is not a real entity because its entailer is imaginary, so reductio reduces to inference from the non-existence of the consequent to the non-existence of the antecedent. But this is refuted: if by 'entailment' is meant a necessary logical relation, it is not present here except through demonstration; if by 'entailment' is meant only a mental relation, then no real impossibility follows. Thus the reductio is invalid. The author uses the example of a substance with position (jawhar dhu wad') to illustrate, referencing the common logical debate about atomism. This is a critical passage that guards against misusing reductio, a tool that must be carefully applied to avoid sophistry. In Islamic sciences, such arguments are used in kalam to prove the contingency of the world, but must be handled with precision.

Key Terms
القياس الخلفي
استلزام
الملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It relies on analogical reasoning (tamthil) which is weaker than categorical syllogism.
D) It was never used by early Muslim logicians.

Key Takeaway
The author's critique shows that reductio arguments are only valid if the conditional used is a genuine necessary entailment, not a mere mental representation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Restriction of Estimations in Universal Conditionals
Dialectical discussion on restricting estimations in universal conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and reconstruct the dialectical argument about why estimations in universal conditionals must be restricted to possible ones, and explain why impossible estimations undermine certainty.

Core Idea
Universal conditionals require possible estimations; impossible ones destroy certainty.

This passage engages a central debate in Islamic logic: how far can we extend the 'estimations' (assumptions) under which a universal conditional is evaluated? The Chief (Ibn Sina) argued that they must be restricted to those compatible with the antecedent. An objection arises: what about impossible antecedents that entail contradictions? The resolution refines the claim to preserve epistemic certainty (jazm). Follow the dialectical steps to understand the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Chief's claim
Find where Ibn Sina restricts the estimations to those that can coexist with the antecedent, even if they are impossible in themselves. Note the examples (Zaid's standing, laughing, etc.).
The estimations are like individuals in categoricals.
2
Analyze the reason for restriction
The author explains that if we generalize to all estimations (including those where the consequent is absent or present without entailment), no universal conditional would be true. Trace the argument for conjunctive and disjunctive types.
Absence of a thing does not entail its existence; existence does not contradict itself.
3
Critique the commentator's alternative
A commentator argued that the antecedent alone suffices for entailment, independent of estimations. The author rejects this because conditional judgments are only intelligible on estimations, just as categoricals require subjects.
The author insists on the parity between individuals (in categoricals) and estimations (in conditionals).
4
Examine the objection about muhal
The objector claims: the impossible can entail two contradictories, so even on impossible estimations, the conditional might hold. Locate this objection and its basis.
This is a subtle point about logical possibility vs. epistemic certainty.
5
Understand the answer preserving certainty
The response concedes that impossible antecedents may entail both, but this is known only by demonstration, not by general rule. Mere possibility does not give necessity; thus to preserve certainty, we must restrict to possible estimations.
Certainty (jazm) is the key: we need universal conditionals to be decisively true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثَّالِثُ (۱): الشيخ (الرَّئِيسُ قَيَّدَ التَّقَادِيرَ وَالْأَوْضَاعَ) التي سبقت (في تَفْسِيرِ الكليةِ بِالَّتِي يُمْكِنُ اجْتِمَاعُهَا مَعَ الْمُقَدَّم ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مُحَالَةٌ في أَنْفُسِهَا) ، فمنى كلما كان (أ) (ب) فـ (ج) (د) أن كون (ج) (د) لازم لكون (أ) (ب) في كل وقت وفي كل وضع ، من قيام زيد، وضحكه وكونه ناطقا، والحمار ضاحكا، وغيرها مما يمكن اجتماعها مع كون (أ) (ب). (وَبَيَّنَ (٢) بِأَنَّهُ لَوْ عَمَّمْنَا يَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يَصْدُقَ كُلَّيَّةٌ أَصْلًا)، لا متصلة ولا منفصلة؛ (فَإِنَّهُ إِذَا فُرِضَ الْمُقَدَّمُ مَعَ عَدَمِ التّالي)، ومع عدم لزومه للمقدم، (أو مَعَ وُجُودِهِ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ التَّالِيَ) في المتصلة على الأولين؛ لأن عدم الشيء لا يستلزم وجوده ، (وَلَا يُنَافِيهِ في المنفصلة على الثالث؛ فإن وجود الشيء لا يعاند نفسه . قال بعض الشراح: إن الحكم في الكلية ليس إلا بلزوم التالي نفس المقدم من حيث هو، والمقدم مستقل في اقتضاء التالي، ولا دخل فيه للتقدير، وإلا لما كان المقدم مستقلا في الاقتضاء، فالمقدم على أي تقدير فرض، ولو منافيا للتالي يكون التالي لازما له كيف، وليس التالي منافيا للمقدم نفسه، إنما هو مناف لمجموع المقدم والتقدير، وليس التالي لازما له. ولا يخفى ما فيه من الخلل ؛ فإنا قدمنا لك أن التقادير في الشرطية كالأفراد في الحملية، ولا يعقل الحكم الشرطي إلا على التقادير، ولذا لم يعقل فيها المهملة القدمائية والطبعية . نعم، قد يكون في مصداقها اقتضاء نفس المقدم، من غير مداخلة أمر آخر، فيلزمه على كل وضع يمكن تحقق المقدم، لكن الحكاية عنه لا تكون إلا بالحكم على كل التقادير، فحينئذ لو أخذ التقدير المنافي لللزوم لما صح الحكم باللزوم قطعا على ذلك التقدير . والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال. (وَأُورِدَ بِأَنَّ الْمُحَالَ جَازَ أَنْ يَسْتَلْزِمَ نَقِيضَيْنِ)، فحينئذ يجوز أن يستلزم المقدم على تقدير عدم التالي، أو لزومه إياه ؛ لكون عدمه مستحيل الاجتماع مع المقدم ، (وَ) أيضا جاز (أَنْ يُعَانِدَهُمَا ) المحال، فحينئذ جاز أن يعاند المقدم التالي، حال مقارنة التالي؛ لكون المقارنة مستحيلة، (فَلَا نُسَلّمْ عَدم الصِّدْقِ. وَأَجِيبَ بِأَنَّ الْمُرَادَ) لو عممنا التقادير (لَمْ يَحْصُلُ الْجَزْمُ بِصِدْقِهَا) . فإن استلزام المحال للنقيضين أمر تجويزي إنما علم في بعض المواضع بالبرهان، بل ربما يحصل الجزم بخلافه، (فَإِنَّ الْإِمْكَانَ) أي التجويز العقلي (لَا يُفيدُ الْوُجُوبَ)، فيلزم أن تكون الشرطيات المجزومة الصدق ضرورة أو نظرا لا تكون مجزومة إلا قليلا. (أَقُولُ): إذا كان كذلك (فَيَجِبُ التَّقْيِيدُ) أي تقييد التقادير بِالْمُمْكِنَاتِ) أي بالتقادير الممكنة (في أَنْفُسِهَا)؛ فإن المحال لا يجزم العقل ملزوميته كما مر، وقد مر ما يكفي لدفعه، (فَافْهَمْ) ، والعلم الحقيقي عند علام الغيوب.
The third (issue): The Chief (Ibn Sina) restricted the estimations and positions that preceded in the interpretation of universality to those that can coexist with the antecedent, even if they are impossible in themselves. So whenever A is B, then J is D, the fact that J is D is necessary for A being B in every time and every situation, such as Zaid's standing, and his laughing, and his being rational, and the donkey laughing, and other things that can coexist with A being B. And he explained that if we generalize, it would result that no universal is true at all, neither conjunctive nor disjunctive. For if the antecedent is assumed with the absence of the consequent, and with its non-necessity of the antecedent, or with its existence, it does not entail the consequent in the conjunctive on the first two (cases); because the absence of a thing does not entail its existence, and it does not contradict it in the disjunctive on the third (case); for the existence of a thing does not oppose itself. Some commentators said: The judgment in the universal is only about the entailment of the consequent from the antecedent itself as such, and the antecedent is independent in requiring the consequent, and estimation has no role in it; otherwise the antecedent would not be independent in requiring it. So the antecedent, on whatever estimation it is assumed, even if contradicting the consequent, the consequent is entailed by it, how? And the consequent is not contradictory to the antecedent itself; rather it is contradictory to the combination of the antecedent and the estimation, and the consequent is not entailed by that. But what is in it of defect is not hidden; for we have previously stated that the estimations in the conditional are like individuals in the categorical, and the conditional judgment is not conceivable except on the estimations; and therefore the ancient absolute and natural absolutes are not conceivable in it. Yes, sometimes in its referent there is the requirement of the antecedent itself, without the intervention of another thing, so it entails on every situation where the antecedent can be realized, but the report about it is only by judging on all the estimations; so then if one takes the estimation contradictory to the entailment, the judgment of entailment would not be correct at all on that estimation. And Allah knows the reality of the matter. And it was objected: The impossible may entail two contradictories, so then it is possible that the antecedent entails on the estimation of the absence of the consequent, or its entailment of it; because its absence is impossible to coexist with the antecedent, and also the impossible may contradict them, so then it is possible that the antecedent contradicts the consequent when the consequent coexists; because coexistence is impossible. So we do not grant the lack of truth. And it was answered: What is intended is that if we generalize the estimations, we would not obtain certainty of their truth. For the entailment of the impossible of two contradictories is a hypothetical matter, known only in some places by demonstration; rather certainty might be obtained contrary to it. For possibility (i.e., intellectual possibility) does not entail necessity. So the conditional propositions that are judged true necessarily or theoretically would be judged true only rarely. I say: Since that is the case, the restriction is necessary, i.e., restricting the estimations to the possible ones, i.e., possible in themselves. For the intellect does not have certainty of the entailment of the impossible, as has passed, and what suffices to reject it has passed. So understand, and true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion of the third issue in the interpretation of universal conditionals: whether the estimations (taqadir) and positions (awda') that enter into the judgment must be restricted to those that can coexist with the antecedent, even if they are impossible in themselves. The author first reports the view of the Chief (Ibn Sina) that the estimations were already restricted in the earlier interpretation of universality, and illustrates with examples like 'whenever Zaid is standing, he is laughing'—here the estimation that Zaid is standing and the donkey laughing can coexist with the antecedent 'Zaid is standing' in the sense that they do not logically preclude it, even though they are impossible in reality. Then he explains that if we generalize to all possible estimations (including those where the consequent is absent), no universal conditional would be true: for a conjunctive conditional, if the antecedent is assumed with the absence of the consequent, the antecedent does not entail the consequent (since absence of a thing does not entail its existence); for a disjunctive conditional, if the antecedent is assumed with the existence of the consequent, the antecedent does not contradict it (since a thing does not oppose itself). A certain commentator argued that the judgment in a universal is only about the entailment of the consequent from the antecedent as such, independent of estimations; otherwise the antecedent would not be independent in requiring the consequent. The author criticizes this reasoning as flawed, because estimations in conditionals are like individuals in categoricals—without specifying estimations, the conditional judgment is not intelligible. He then concedes that sometimes the antecedent itself may entail the consequent regardless of additional factors, but the statement about that entailment is only made by judging across all estimations, so if a contradictory estimation is taken, the judgment of entailment does not hold. An objection is then raised: the impossible (muhal) can entail two contradictories, so it might be that an impossible antecedent entails its consequent even on contradictory estimations. The answer refines the claim: what is meant is that if we generalize to all estimations, we lose certainty (jazm) of the truth of the universal conditional, because the entailment by the impossible is only known in special cases by demonstration, and mere possibility (imkan) does not give necessity. Therefore the restriction to possible estimations is necessary; the intellect cannot be certain of the entailment from an impossible antecedent. The passage concludes by urging understanding and deferring true knowledge to Allah.

Key Terms
التقدير
الكليّة الشرطية
المحال
الجزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because assuming the antecedent with the absence of the consequent makes entailment impossible, and assuming it with existence does not guarantee contradiction.
C) Because the Chief explicitly forbade that interpretation.
D) Because conditional judgments are only about necessary connections.

Key Takeaway
Universal conditionals require possible estimations; impossible ones undermine certainty, so estimations must be restricted to preserve the truth and decisiveness of conditional propositions.

Construct a dialectical exchange (mudda'i, mu'tarid, jawab) for the conditional: 'If the Qur'an is created, then it is contingent.' Use the restriction of estimations to defend the certainty of the conditional.

Objection and Answer on Impossible Antecedents in Conditionals
Objection and answer on impossible antecedents in conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection against restricting antecedents to possible ones and explain why the author insists on the restriction for certainty.

Core Idea
Impossible antecedents undermine certainty in conditional propositions, so logicians restrict them.

This passage addresses a critical challenge: if an impossible antecedent can entail anything, how can we be certain of conditional truth? The objector argues that impossibility allows arbitrary entailments, so the restrictions seem arbitrary. The answer clarifies that uncertainty is precisely why restriction is needed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
Determine the core objection: that the impossible may entail two contradictories, so an impossible antecedent could entail either the truth or falsehood of the consequent, making the restriction to possible antecedents unnecessary.
The objector refuses to concede that impossible antecedents always make conditionals false.
2
Extract the objector's reasoning
Understand how impossibility allows arbitrary entailments: because the impossible entails contradictories, it might also entail the negation of the consequent or its opposite, and it might oppose both, thus the conditional could be true or false arbitrarily.
The objector uses the property of impossibility to generate possibilities.
3
Understand the author's response
The author argues that certainty (jazm) about the conditional's truth requires restricting to possible antecedents; the entailment of impossible is only conjectural, not a universal rule, and possibility does not guarantee necessity.
The response focuses on epistemic certainty, not metaphysical truth.
4
See the conclusion
The author concludes that antecedents must be restricted to possible ones because the mind cannot be certain about implications of the impossible, as was previously established.
The passage ends with a reminder that true knowledge belongs to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأُورِدَ بِأَنَّ الْمُحَالَ جَازَ أَنْ يَسْتَلْزِمَ نَقِيضَيْنِ، فَحِينَئِذٍ يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَسْتَلْزِمَ الْمُقَدَّمُ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ عَدَمِ التَّالِي، أَوْ لُزُومِهِ إِيَّاهُ؛ لِكَوْنِ عَدَمِهِ مُسْتَحِيلَ الاِجْتِمَاعِ مَعَ الْمُقَدَّمِ، وَأَيْضًا جَازَ أَنْ يُعَانِدَهُمَا الْمُحَالُ، فَحِينَئِذٍ جَازَ أَنْ يُعَانِدَ الْمُقَدَّمُ التَّالِيَ، حَالَ مُقَارَنَةِ التَّالِي؛ لِكَوْنِ الْمُقَارَنَةِ مُسْتَحِيلَةً، فَلَا نُسَلِّمْ عَدَمَ الصِّدْقِ. وَأُجِيبَ بِأَنَّ الْمُرَادَ لَوْ عُمِّمَتِ التَّقَادِيرُ لَمْ يَحْصُلِ الْجَزْمُ بِصِدْقِهَا. فَإِنَّ اسْتِلْزَامَ الْمُحَالِ لِلنَّقِيضَيْنِ أَمْرٌ تَجْوِيزِيٌّ إِنَّمَا عُلِمَ فِي بَعْضِ الْمَوَاضِعِ بِالْبُرْهَانِ، بَلْ رُبَّمَا يَحْصُلُ الْجَزْمُ بِخِلَافِهِ. فَإِنَّ الْإِمْكَانَ، أَيِ التَّجْوِيزَ الْعَقْلِيَّ، لَا يُفِيدُ الْوُجُوبَ، فَيَلْزَمُ أَنْ تَكُونَ الشَّرْطِيَّاتُ الْمَجْزُومَةُ الصِّدْقِ ضَرُورَةً أَوْ نَظَرًا لَا تَكُونُ مَجْزُومَةً إِلَّا قَلِيلًا. أَقُولُ: إِذَا كَانَ كَذَلِكَ فَيَجِبُ التَّقْيِيدُ، أَيْ تَقْيِيدُ التَّقَادِيرِ بِالْمُمْكِنَاتِ، أَيْ بِالتَّقَادِيرِ الْمُمْكِنَةِ فِي أَنْفُسِهَا؛ فَإِنَّ الْمُحَالَ لَا يَجْزِمُ الْعَقْلُ مَلْزُومِيَّتَهُ كَمَا مَرَّ، وَقَدْ مَرَّ مَا يَكْفِي لِدَفْعِهِ، فَافْهَمْ. وَالْعِلْمُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ عِنْدَ عَلَّامِ الْغُيُوبِ.
And it was objected that the impossible may entail two contradictories, so then the antecedent might entail the non-existence of the consequent, or its being entailed by it, because its non-existence is impossible to coexist with the antecedent; and also the impossible may oppose both, so then the antecedent might oppose the consequent while the consequent is concomitant, because the coexistence is impossible. So we do not concede the lack of truth. And it was answered that what is meant is that if we generalize the suppositions, certainty of its truth would not be obtained. For the entailment of two contradictories by the impossible is a conjectural matter known only in some cases by proof; rather, certainty might be obtained of the contrary. For possibility, i.e., mental permissibility, does not entail necessity. Consequently, conditionals that are known to be true necessarily or by reflection would rarely be certain. I say: If so, then restriction is necessary, i.e., restriction of the suppositions to possible ones, i.e., to suppositions that are possible in themselves; for the mind does not judge the necessary implication of the impossible as has been mentioned, and what suffices to refute it has already been mentioned. So understand. And true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the claim that antecedents in conditional propositions must be possible. The objector argues that an impossible antecedent might entail two contradictories, so it could entail either the negation of the consequent or the consequent itself, because the impossibility of coexistence allows for such possibilities. Similarly, the impossible might oppose both antecedent and consequent, making the conditional false even if the consequent is true. Therefore, the objector refuses to accept that setting an impossible antecedent always results in a false conditional. The answer clarifies that the purpose of restricting to possible antecedents is to achieve certainty (jazm) about the truth of the conditional. The entailment of contradictories by the impossible is only conjectural (tajwizi) and known in some cases via demonstration, not a universal rule. Possibility does not guarantee necessity, so if we allowed impossible antecedents, very few conditionals would be certain. The author then affirms that the correct position is to restrict suppositions to those that are possible in themselves, because the mind cannot be certain about the implications of the impossible. The passage concludes with a reminder that true knowledge belongs to God alone.

التقادير
الجزْم
الإمكان

D) Because the logicians were unable to define 'impossible'.

Key Takeaway
Certainty in conditional propositions requires that the antecedent be possible; otherwise, the entailment is not guaranteed.

Refuting Universal Partial Entailment
Author refutes claim of universal partial entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the error in the claim that partial entailment holds between any two propositions, including contradictories, and explain the author's counterargument that the presuppositions may be impossible.

Core Idea
Not every two things entail each other even partially; the presuppositions may be impossible.

Some logicians claimed that there is always at least a partial entailment between any two propositions, even contradictories. This would mean that universal negative conditionals are always false. In this passage, the author refutes this by pointing out that the presupposed conditions for such entailment might be impossible, thus the claim does not hold universally. Let's dissect this argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim Being Attacked
The passage begins by stating that some people claim partial entailment (al-luzum al-juz'i) between any two things, even contradictories. This is the target doctrine.
Partial entailment means the implication holds only under certain presuppositions, not absolutely.
2
Understand the Claimed Implications
According to the claimants, if partial entailment always holds, then the universal negative conditional (e.g., 'No A entails B') is false, because otherwise the two contradictories would be conjoined. Instead, only universal affirmative conditionals (either essential or accidental) are true.
This reasoning assumes that the absence of entailment would allow contradictories to coexist, which is impossible.
3
Find the Author's Objection
The author objects: 'It is possible that some of the presuppositions (taqadir) on which the partial entailment is based are impossible.' Therefore, the conjunction of connection and disjunction (i.e., both conditional and disjunctive) may be allowed without contradiction.
If the presupposition is impossible, then the entailment does not actually hold, so the claimed universal negative falsehood is not necessary.
4
Evaluate the Force of the Objection
The author's critique shows that the claimants' reasoning is not universally valid. It only works if all presuppositions are possible, but that is not guaranteed. Thus, the claim of universal partial entailment is refuted.
This is a subtle logical point: the hidden assumption of possible presuppositions is exposed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السَّادِسُ : أَنَّ مِنْهُمْ مَنِ ادَّعَى اللُّزُومَ الْجُزْنِيَّ بَيْنَ كُلِّ أَمْرَيْنِ، حَتَّى النَّقِيضَيْن ، فَلَا يَصْدُقُ السَّالِبَةُ اللُّزُومِيَّةُ ، بَل الْمُوجَبَةُ الْحَقِيقِيَّةُ، بَل) الموجبة ( الاِتِّفَاقِيَّةُ) الْكُلَّيَّات ) ، أما كذب السالبة الكلية فلأنه لولاه اجتمع النقيضان، وأما كذب الحقيقية الكلية فلأنه لولاه لزم تحقق العناد والاتصال على تقدير واحد. وفيه: أن من الجائز أن يكون بعض التقادير التي عليه اللزوم الجزئي من المستحيلات، فيجوز اجتماع الاتصال والانفصال.
The sixth: That some of them claimed partial entailment between every two things, even contradictories. So the universal negative conditional is not true, rather the universal affirmative true (conditional) or even the universal affirmative accidental. As for the falsehood of the universal negative, because otherwise the two contradictories would be conjoined. And as for the falsehood of the universal true affirmative, because otherwise the existence of opposition and connection on a single presupposition would be necessary. And in it: That it is possible that some of the presuppositions on which the partial entailment is based are impossible, so the conjunction of connection and disjunction is allowed.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage presents a critical discussion of a claim made by some logicians: that there is a partial entailment (al-luzum al-juz'i) between any two things, even between contradictories. The implication of this claim is that the universal negative conditional (e.g., 'No A is B' in a conditional sense) is false, because if there were no entailment, contradictories could be true together. Instead, they say only the universal affirmative conditional (true or accidental) holds. The commentator then refutes this claim: it is possible that some of the presupposed conditions (taqadir) upon which the partial entailment relies are themselves impossible, so the conjunction of connection and disjunction (i.e., both conditional and disjunctive) may coexist without contradiction. Thus the original claim is not universally valid.

Key Terms
اللُّزُومُ الْجُزْئِيُّ
السَّالِبَةُ اللُّزُومِيَّةُ
الْمُوجَبَةُ الْحَقِيقِيَّةُ
الْمُوجَبَةُ الاِتِّفَاقِيَّةُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) That some presuppositions for the entailment may be impossible.
B) That contradictories never entail anything.
C) That universal negatives are always false.
D) That only accidental conditionals are true.

Key Takeaway
Partial entailment between any two things is not universally valid because the presupposed conditions may be impossible.

Proof of Disjunction using Third and First Figures
Author proves a logical relation using third and first figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the syllogistic proof of a disjunction (or relation) using the third figure and first figure with conversion, and refute the objection about accidental minor premises.

Core Idea
Third figure proves particular affirmative; first figure with conversion yields the same conclusion directly.

This passage demonstrates how to prove that if one of two things is realized, the other is realized (a form of disjunction or implication). The author uses the third figure and then shows an alternative proof using the first figure after converting the minor premise. He also addresses a common objection about the necessity of the minor premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being proved
The conclusion is 'some of what is realized of one of the two things is realized of the other' (a particular affirmative).
2
Extract the premises in the third figure
Both premises have the same subject (the composite of the two things) and different predicates: 'one is realized' and 'the other is realized'. This is the third figure.
3
Recognize the conversion step for the first figure
The minor premise ('All composites realize the other') is converted to 'Some of what realizes the other is a composite', allowing the syllogism to be in the first figure.
4
Evaluate the objection and response
An objector claims the converted minor is accidental and thus invalid. The author responds that the conversion of a necessary proposition is itself necessary, preserving validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبُرْهِنَ عَلَيْهِ بِالشَّكْلِ الثَّالِثِ، وَهُوَ كُلَّمَا تَحَقَّقَ مَجْمُوعُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ)، ولو كانا متناقضين (تَحَقَّقَ أَحَدُهُمَا ، وَكُلَّمَا تَحَقَّقَ الْمَجْمُوعُ تَحَقَّقَ الْآخَرُ)، فبعض ما تحقق أحدهما تحقق الآخر، بَلْ بِالشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى)، فنقول بعض ما تحقق أحد الأمرين تحقق مجموعهما، وكل ما تحقق مجموعهما تحقق الآخر. وما قيل: إن الصغرى حينئذ اتفاقية، فلا ينتج ففاسد ؛ فإن عكس اللزومية لزومية.
And it is proved by the third figure: Whenever the composite of the two things is realized (even if they are contradictory), one of them is realized, and whenever the composite is realized, the other is realized. So some of what is realized of one of them is realized of the other. Rather, by the first figure with the conversion of the minor: So we say: Some of what is realized of one of the two things, its composite is realized, and everything whose composite is realized, the other is realized. And what is said: that the minor premise then is accidental, so it does not produce a conclusion, is false; for the conversion of a necessary [proposition] is necessary.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents a syllogistic proof for a certain claim about two things, using the third and first figures. The claim is about a disjunction? The author says it is proved by the third figure: the major premise is 'Whenever the composite of the two things is realized, one of them is realized' and the minor is 'Whenever the composite is realized, the other is realized'. From these, by third figure, we conclude 'Some of what is realized of one is realized of the other' (a particular affirmative). Then he shows it can also be done by first figure if we convert the minor: we take 'Some of what is realized of one, its composite is realized' (converted minor) and 'Everything whose composite is realized, the other is realized' (original major). That yields the same conclusion. He then refutes an objection: the minor premise is considered accidental (accidental connection) and so the syllogism would not produce a certain conclusion. He replies that the conversion of a necessary proposition is itself necessary, so the minor remains necessary.

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث (al-shakl al-thalith): The third figure of categorical syllogism where the middle term is subject in both premises.
عكس الصغرى (aks al-sughra): Conversion of the minor premise, i.e., interchanging subject and predicate.
اللزومية (al-luzumiyya): A conditional proposition expressing necessary implication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the proof using the first figure, what is the converted minor premise?
A) Some of what is realized of one, its composite is realized.
B) All composites are realized.
C) Whenever the other is realized, the composite is realized.
D) Some composites are not realized.

Key Takeaway
The third figure and first figure with conversion both validly prove a particular affirmative relation between two things, provided the premises are necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a third-figure categorical syllogism on a topic from usul al-fiqh: e.g., whenever an act is obligatory, it is commendable; and whenever an act is obligatory, it is required. Conclude that some commendable acts are required.
?
Take the syllogism 'All intoxicants are forbidden; some nabidh is intoxicant; therefore some nabidh is forbidden' (first figure) and convert the minor premise to see if a third-figure version yields the same conclusion.

Objection and Response on Composite Premise
Objection and response on whether composite premise needs causal contribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a jadal exchange by identifying the objection's grounds, the respondent's distinction, and assessing who wins the debate.

Core Idea
Necessity is not causation; logical implication does not require causal influence.

In this passage, a muḥaqqiq raises a dialectical objection against a proof that uses a composite premise. The author responds by clarifying the nature of necessity. Understanding this exchange sharpens your ability to follow and evaluate arguments in Islamic dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector argues that for a composite premise to imply a part, each part must have causal input. He claims that one part here is redundant, making the premise invalid.
2
Locate the objector's conclusion
He concludes that the two premises are denied (mumtani'at) because they rely on a faulty implication.
3
Extract the author's response
The author distinguishes between necessity (luzum) and causal influence (iqtida'). Necessity is merely the impossibility of separation; it does not require causation.
The author gives the example of two impossible propositions that imply each other necessarily.
4
Apply the distinction
The objector assumed that implication requires causal input, but the author shows that mere inseparability suffices. Thus the objection fails.
5
Assess the outcome
By the rules of jadal, the respondent successfully deflects the objection because it rests on an unwarranted assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَرَامَ التَّفَصَّيَ عَنْهُ بَعْضُ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ، بِأَنَّ الْمَجْمُوعَ إِنَّمَا يَسْتَلْزِمْ الْجُزْءَ لَوْ كَانَ لِكُلِّ مِنَ الْأَجْزَاءِ مَدْخَلٌ في الِاقْتِضَاءِ، وَمِنَ الْبَيْنِ أَنَّ الْجُزء الْآخَرَ لَا دَخْلَ لَهُ فِيهِ ههنا، كليا، بَلْ يَجْرِي مَجْرَى الْحَشْوِ، فالمقدمتان ممنوعتان . (وَفِيهِ: أَنَّ اللُّزُومَ لَا يَقْتَضِي الاِقْتِضَاءَ وَالتَّأْثِيرَ؛ فَإِنَّهُ امْتِنَاعُ الانْفِكَاكِ)، وهو غير مستلزم للاقتضاء، ألا ترى أن المحالين ربما يتلازمان، (فَارْتِبَاطُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ بِهَذَا النَّمَطِ، أي بأن يكون الانفكاك ممتنعا (كَافٍ فِيهِ.
So some of the investigators sought to escape from it, [saying] that the composite implies the part only if each of the parts has an input in the implication. And it is clear that the other part has no input in it here, entirely, but rather it is like a superfluous addition. Thus the two premises are denied. And in it [the response]: That necessity does not require causal influence and effect; for it is the impossibility of separation, and it does not require causal influence. Do you not see that sometimes two impossible things are necessary? So the connection of the two things in this manner, i.e., by the impossibility of separation, is sufficient for it.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response regarding the composite premise used in the earlier proof. The investigator argues that for a composite to imply a part, each part must contribute to the implication. Here, one part is redundant, so the composite premise is not valid. Therefore the premises are denied. The author responds by distinguishing between necessity (luzum) and causal efficacy. Necessity is simply the impossibility of separation; it does not require that one thing causally produces another. As an example, two impossible propositions can imply each other necessarily. Therefore, the connection by impossibility of separation is sufficient. So the objection fails.

Key Terms
الاقتضاء
اللزوم
الانفكاك

Key Takeaway

Proving the Particular Conditional by the Third Figure
Author defends proof of particular conditional via third figure and addresses scholarly objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the syllogistic proof of a particular conditional using the third figure and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
The third figure proves particular implications by linking the sum of two things to each part.

This passage demonstrates how logicians used categorical syllogisms to prove conditional statements. The author shows that a particular conditional (shartiyya juz'iyya) can be proved by the third figure, even when the antecedent and consequent seem unrelated. We'll walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion
The goal is to prove 'some cases where A is realized, B is realized' (particular conditional), where A and B are two actual things.
The conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional.
2
Set up the premises in the third figure
The third figure has the middle term as subject of both premises. Here, the middle term is 'the sum of A and B' (المجموع). Premises: (1) Whenever the sum is realized, A is realized. (2) Whenever the sum is realized, B is realized.
These are universal conditionals, not categoricals, but the analogy to categorical third figure holds.
3
Convert the conclusion from third to first figure
Alternatively, use first figure by converting the minor premise: 'Some cases where A is realized entail the sum'. Then: (1) Some A entails sum, (2) Every sum entails B, so Some A entails B.
Conversion of implicative conditionals preserves implication (luzum).
4
Address the accidental minor objection
Some objected that the minor premise (Some A is sum) is accidental, not implicative. The author replies that the converse of an implicative is also implicative, so the minor remains implicative.
This preserves the validity of the first-figure version.
5
Verify validity
Both versions (third figure directly and first figure via conversion) yield a valid conclusion. The third figure requires at least one universal premise and an affirmative conclusion, which holds here.
The proof is sound if the premises are accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة لمبحث الشرطيات وأما كذب الاتفاقية الكلية فإن تحقق العلاقة على بعض الأوضاع ينفي الاتفاق عليه وهذا لا يتم في الاتفاقية الخاصة؛ فإن المعتبر فيها التقادير المتحققة في الواقع، ومن الجائز أن يكون الأوضاع التي عليها اللزوم الجزئي غير واقعة، بل متنافية، وسيجيئ من المصنف ما يدفعه، فانتظر. (وَبُرْهِنَ عَلَيْهِ بِالشَّكْلِ الثَّالِثِ، وَهُوَ كُلَّمَا تَحَقَّقَ مَجْمُوعُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ)، ولو كانا متناقضين (تَحَقَّقَ أَحَدُهُمَا، وَكُلَّمَا تَحَقَّقَ الْمَجْمُوعُ تَحَقَّقَ الْآخَرُ)، فبعض ما تحقق أحدهما تحقق الآخر، بَلْ بِالشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى)، فنقول بعض ما تحقق أحد الأمرين تحقق مجموعهما، وكل ما تحقق مجموعهما تحقق الآخر. وما قيل: إن الصغرى حينئذ اتفاقية، فلا ينتج ففاسد؛ فإن عكس اللزومية لزومية. (فَرَامَ التَّفَصَّيَ عَنْهُ بَعْضُ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ، بِأَنَّ الْمَجْمُوعَ إِنَّمَا يَسْتَلْزِمْ الْجُزْءَ لَوْ كَانَ لِكُلِّ مِنَ الْأَجْزَاءِ مَدْخَلٌ فِي الِاقْتِضَاءِ، وَمِنَ الْبَيْنِ أَنَّ الْجُزْءَ الْآخَرَ لَا دَخْلَ لَهُ فِيهِ) ههنا، كليا، بَلْ يَجْرِي مَجْرَى الْحَشْوِ)، فالمقدمتان ممنوعتان. (وَفِيهِ: أَنَّ اللُّزُومَ لَا يَقْتَضِي الِاقْتِضَاءَ وَالتَّأْثِيرَ؛ فَإِنَّهُ امْتِنَاعُ الانْفِكَاكِ)، وهو غير مستلزم للاقتضاء، ألا ترى أن المحالين ربما يتلازمان، (فَارْتِبَاطُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ بِهَذَا النَّمَطِ، أي بأن يكون الانفكاك ممتنعا (كَافٍ فِيهِ. قَالَ الشَّيْخُ إِذَا فُرِضَ الْمُقَدَّمُ مَعَ عَدَمِ التَّالِي يَسْتَلْزِمُ عَدَمَ التَّالِي، فَقَالَ بِاسْتِلْزَامِ الْمَجْمُوعِ الْجُزْءَ. وَبَعْضُهُمْ بِأَنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ تِلْكَ الْكُلِّيَّةَ)، أي كلما تحقق المجموع تحقق أحدهما؛ لِجَوَازِ اسْتِحَالَةِ الْمَجْمُوعِ) الذي هو المقدم، (فَعَلَى تَقْدِيرِ ثُبُوتِهِ يَنْفَكُ عَنِ الْجُزْءِ)؛ بناء على جواز استلزام المحال محالا، فلا يستلزم الجزء، (وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ). ولا يبعد أن يقال: إن استلزام الكل للجزء ضروري، وإنكاره مكابرة، غاية ما في الباب استحالة المقدم توجب استلزامه لانفكاك الجزء أيضا، ولا استحالة فيه؛ فإن غاية ما لزم منه صدق شرطيتين، تالي أحدهما مناف لتالي الأخرى. هذا، وهو موضع تأمل، والعلم الحقيقي عند واهب العلوم. بَقِيَ شَيْءٌ، وَهُوَ أَنَّا نَدَّعِي ذَلِكَ اللُّزُومَ) الجزئي بَيْنَ كُلِّ أَمْرَيْنِ وَاقِعِيَّيْنِ، وَنُبَرْهِنُ عَلَيْهِ بِأَخْذِ تِلْكَ الْكُلَّيَّةِ بِاعْتِبَارِ التَّقَادِيرِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ). فنقول: كلما تحقق مجموع أمرين واقعيين على كل واحد واحد من التقادير الواقعية تحقق أحدهما على تلك التقادير، وكلما تحقق مجموع أمرين واقعيين على تلك التقادير تحقق الآخر، فبعض ما تحقق أحد الأمرين الواقعيين تحقق الآخر على بعض التقادير الواقعية، (فَبَطَلَ الاتِّفَاقِيَّةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ الْخَاصَّةُ) التي حكم فيها بموافقة صدق التالي صدق المقدم على جميع التقادير الواقعية من غير لزوم أصلا، كما هو المشهور، أو من غير معلومية اللزوم من غير وساطة العلم بالاتفاق، (فَتَأَمَّلْ)؛ فإنه دقيق.
Continuation of the topic of conditionals. As for the falsity of the universal accidental conditional: the realization of a relation in some states negates its accidentalness, and this does not hold for the particular accidental conditional, because what is considered there is the actually realized estimations, and it is possible that the states in which there is particular implication are not actual, but rather mutually exclusive. The author will later provide what refutes this, so wait. (And it is proved by the third figure, namely: whenever the sum of two things is realized), even if they are contradictory, (one of them is realized, and whenever the sum is realized, the other is realized), so some instances of the realization of one entail the realization of the other. Rather, (by the first figure through the conversion of the minor premise), so we say: some instances of the realization of one of the two things entail the realization of their sum, and everything whose sum is realized entails the realization of the other. And what is said: that the minor premise is then accidental, so it does not produce a conclusion—this is corrupt, because the converse of an implicative (conditional) is implicative. (Then some of the verifiers sought to escape from it) by saying that the sum only entails the part if each of the parts has a role in the requirement, and it is obvious that the other part has no role in it here, generally, but rather is redundant, so both premises are rejected. (And in it: that implication does not require causation and effect; for it is the impossibility of separation), and it does not necessitate causation. Do you not see that two impossibilities may imply each other? (So the connection of two things in this manner), i.e., by the impossibility of separation, (is sufficient for it). The Shaykh said: If the antecedent is assumed together with the non-existence of the consequent, it necessitates the non-existence of the consequent, so he said that the sum entails the part. And some of them said: we do not grant that universality, i.e., whenever the sum is realized, one of them is realized, because of the possibility of the impossibility of the sum, which is the antecedent. So upon the supposition of its existence, it is separated from the part, based on the permissibility of an impossibility implying an impossibility, so it does not entail the part, (and that is the truth). And it is not far to say that the entailment of the whole by the part is necessary, and its denial is stubbornness. The utmost in the matter is that the impossibility of the antecedent necessitates its entailment of the separation of the part also, and there is no impossibility in that, for the utmost that follows from it is the truth of two conditionals, the consequent of one conflicting with the consequent of the other. This is a point of contemplation, and true knowledge is with the Bestower of sciences. A point remains: that we claim that particular implication between every two actual things, and we prove it by taking that universality with consideration of the actual estimations. So we say: whenever the sum of two actual things is realized in each one of the actual estimations, one of them is realized in those estimations, and whenever the sum of two actual things is realized in those estimations, the other is realized, so some instances of the realization of one of the two actual things entail the realization of the other in some actual estimations. (Thus the universal particular accidental conditional is invalidated), in which it was judged that the truth of the consequent corresponds to the truth of the antecedent in all actual estimations without any implication, as is well-known, or without known implication without mediation of the knowledge of the coincidence. (So contemplate), for it is subtle.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the proof of a particular conditional (shartiyya juz'iyya) using the third figure of the categorical syllogism, followed by objections and replies. The author begins by discussing the falsity of the universal accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya kulliyya), explaining that a relation existing in some states negates accidentalness, but this does not apply to the particular accidental conditional because it considers only actualized states. Then the proof is stated: the third figure is used, where the premises are 'Whenever the sum of two things is realized, one of them is realized' and 'Whenever the sum is realized, the other is realized', leading to the conclusion 'Some cases where one is realized, the other is realized'. Alternatively, the first figure can be used by converting the minor premise. An objection that the minor premise would be accidental is refuted because the converse of an implicative conditional remains implicative. Next, a scholar (al-muhaqqiq) attempts to escape by arguing that the sum only entails a part if each part has a causal role; here, one part is redundant, so the premises are not universal. The reply is that implication (luzum) does not require causation; it is merely impossibility of separation (imtina' al-infikak). Two impossibilities can imply each other, so the connection suffices. Then another objection: some deny the universality of the premise 'whenever the sum is realized, one of them is realized' because if the sum itself is impossible, it might separate from the part, based on the principle that an impossibility can imply an impossibility. The author notes that this is the correct view. A further reflection states that the entailment of the whole for the part is necessary, and its denial is stubbornness; the impossibility of the antecedent allows it to also imply the separation of the part, leading to two conditionals with conflicting consequents, which is not absurd. Finally, a remaining point: the author claims a particular implication between every two actual existents, and proves it by taking a universal premise with respect to actual estimations. This shows that the universal particular accidental conditional (which asserts constant truth without implication) is invalid. The passage ends with an exhortation to contemplate due to its subtlety.

Key Terms
اتفاقية كلية
اتفاقية خاصة
لزوم
انفكاك
شكل ثالث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The consequent of the conditional
C) The sum of the antecedent and consequent
D) The accidental relation

Key Takeaway
A particular conditional can be proved by the third figure using the sum of antecedent and consequent as the middle term, and the proof withstands objections about accidentalness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid third-figure syllogism to prove the particular conditional: 'Some acts of worship are accepted by Allah' using the middle term 'acts performed with sincere intention'. Identify the premises and conclusion.
?
Take the first-figure version of the proof: 'Some cases where A is realized entail the sum of A and B; every sum entails B; therefore, some cases of A entail B.' Check the validity of this form by converting the minor premise (which the author says is permissible).

Implication as Impossibility of Separation and the Refutation of Causal Objections
Author defines implication as impossibility of separation and refutes causal objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define logical implication (luzum) as the impossibility of separation and distinguish it from causal requirement, and evaluate objections based on causal interpretation.

Core Idea
Implication does not require causation; it only requires that the antecedent and consequent cannot be separated.

Some scholars objected that the third-figure proof fails because the sum only entails a part if each part contributes causally; one part is redundant. The author responds by clarifying that implication (luzum) is simply the impossibility of separation (imtina' al-infikak), not a causal relation. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Some verifiers (al-muhaqqiqun) claimed that the sum only entails a part if each part has a role (madkhal) in the requirement; here one part is redundant, so the premises are not universal.
This objection assumes implication requires causal contribution.
2
Extract the author's definition of implication
The author states: 'implication does not require causation and effect; for it is the impossibility of separation (imtina' al-infikak)'. That is, if A and B cannot exist separately, then A implies B regardless of causation.
This is a logical, not metaphysical, definition.
3
Apply the definition to the objection
Since implication is just impossibility of separation, the sum entails each part even without causal contribution. The connection suffices.
The author gives an example: two impossibilities can imply each other.
4
Consider the further objection about impossible antecedents
Some deny that an impossible sum entails a part, because an impossibility may imply an impossibility, allowing separation. The author notes this is correct.
This leads to the principle that an impossibility can imply its own negation or anything.
5
Synthesize the refined position
The author concludes that the entailment of whole for part is necessary (necessary truth) despite impossibility of antecedent. The impossibility only creates two conflicting conditionals, which is not absurd.
This preserves the proof while acknowledging the subtleties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة لمبحث الشرطيات وأما كذب الاتفاقية الكلية فإن تحقق العلاقة على بعض الأوضاع ينفي الاتفاق عليه وهذا لا يتم في الاتفاقية الخاصة؛ فإن المعتبر فيها التقادير المتحققة في الواقع، ومن الجائز أن يكون الأوضاع التي عليها اللزوم الجزئي غير واقعة، بل متنافية، وسيجيئ من المصنف ما يدفعه، فانتظر. (وَفِيهِ: أَنَّ اللُّزُومَ لَا يَقْتَضِي الِاقْتِضَاءَ وَالتَّأْثِيرَ؛ فَإِنَّهُ امْتِنَاعُ الانْفِكَاكِ)، وهو غير مستلزم للاقتضاء، ألا ترى أن المحالين ربما يتلازمان، (فَارْتِبَاطُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ بِهَذَا النَّمَطِ، أي بأن يكون الانفكاك ممتنعا (كَافٍ فِيهِ. قَالَ الشَّيْخُ إِذَا فُرِضَ الْمُقَدَّمُ مَعَ عَدَمِ التَّالِي يَسْتَلْزِمُ عَدَمَ التَّالِي، فَقَالَ بِاسْتِلْزَامِ الْمَجْمُوعِ الْجُزْءَ. وَبَعْضُهُمْ بِأَنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ تِلْكَ الْكُلِّيَّةَ)، أي كلما تحقق المجموع تحقق أحدهما؛ لِجَوَازِ اسْتِحَالَةِ الْمَجْمُوعِ) الذي هو المقدم، (فَعَلَى تَقْدِيرِ ثُبُوتِهِ يَنْفَكُ عَنِ الْجُزْءِ)؛ بناء على جواز استلزام المحال محالا، فلا يستلزم الجزء، (وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ). ولا يبعد أن يقال: إن استلزام الكل للجزء ضروري، وإنكاره مكابرة، غاية ما في الباب استحالة المقدم توجب استلزامه لانفكاك الجزء أيضا، ولا استحالة فيه؛ فإن غاية ما لزم منه صدق شرطيتين، تالي أحدهما مناف لتالي الأخرى. هذا، وهو موضع تأمل، والعلم الحقيقي عند واهب العلوم. بَقِيَ شَيْءٌ، وَهُوَ أَنَّا نَدَّعِي ذَلِكَ اللُّزُومَ) الجزئي بَيْنَ كُلِّ أَمْرَيْنِ وَاقِعِيَّيْنِ، وَنُبَرْهِنُ عَلَيْهِ بِأَخْذِ تِلْكَ الْكُلَّيَّةِ بِاعْتِبَارِ التَّقَادِيرِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ). فنقول: كلما تحقق مجموع أمرين واقعيين على كل واحد واحد من التقادير الواقعية تحقق أحدهما على تلك التقادير، وكلما تحقق مجموع أمرين واقعيين على تلك التقادير تحقق الآخر، فبعض ما تحقق أحد الأمرين الواقعيين تحقق الآخر على بعض التقادير الواقعية، (فَبَطَلَ الاتِّفَاقِيَّةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ الْخَاصَّةُ) التي حكم فيها بموافقة صدق التالي صدق المقدم على جميع التقادير الواقعية من غير لزوم أصلا، كما هو المشهور، أو من غير معلومية اللزوم من غير وساطة العلم بالاتفاق، (فَتَأَمَّلْ)؛ فإنه دقيق.
Continuation of the topic of conditionals. As for the falsity of the universal accidental conditional: the realization of a relation in some states negates its accidentalness, and this does not hold for the particular accidental conditional, because what is considered there is the actually realized estimations, and it is possible that the states in which there is particular implication are not actual, but rather mutually exclusive. The author will later provide what refutes this, so wait. (And it is proved by the third figure, namely: whenever the sum of two things is realized), even if they are contradictory, (one of them is realized, and whenever the sum is realized, the other is realized), so some instances of the realization of one entail the realization of the other. Rather, (by the first figure through the conversion of the minor premise), so we say: some instances of the realization of one of the two things entail the realization of their sum, and everything whose sum is realized entails the realization of the other. And what is said: that the minor premise is then accidental, so it does not produce a conclusion—this is corrupt, because the converse of an implicative (conditional) is implicative. (Then some of the verifiers sought to escape from it) by saying that the sum only entails the part if each of the parts has a role in the requirement, and it is obvious that the other part has no role in it here, generally, but rather is redundant, so both premises are rejected. (And in it: that implication does not require causation and effect; for it is the impossibility of separation), and it does not necessitate causation. Do you not see that two impossibilities may imply each other? (So the connection of two things in this manner), i.e., by the impossibility of separation, (is sufficient for it). The Shaykh said: If the antecedent is assumed together with the non-existence of the consequent, it necessitates the non-existence of the consequent, so he said that the sum entails the part. And some of them said: we do not grant that universality, i.e., whenever the sum is realized, one of them is realized, because of the possibility of the impossibility of the sum, which is the antecedent. So upon the supposition of its existence, it is separated from the part, based on the permissibility of an impossibility implying an impossibility, so it does not entail the part, (and that is the truth). And it is not far to say that the entailment of the whole by the part is necessary, and its denial is stubbornness. The utmost in the matter is that the impossibility of the antecedent necessitates its entailment of the separation of the part also, and there is no impossibility in that, for the utmost that follows from it is the truth of two conditionals, the consequent of one conflicting with the consequent of the other. This is a point of contemplation, and true knowledge is with the Bestower of sciences. A point remains: that we claim that particular implication between every two actual things, and we prove it by taking that universality with consideration of the actual estimations. So we say: whenever the sum of two actual things is realized in each one of the actual estimations, one of them is realized in those estimations, and whenever the sum of two actual things is realized in those estimations, the other is realized, so some instances of the realization of one of the two actual things entail the realization of the other in some actual estimations. (Thus the universal particular accidental conditional is invalidated), in which it was judged that the truth of the consequent corresponds to the truth of the antecedent in all actual estimations without any implication, as is well-known, or without known implication without mediation of the knowledge of the coincidence. (So contemplate), for it is subtle.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the proof of a particular conditional (shartiyya juz'iyya) using the third figure of the categorical syllogism, followed by objections and replies. The author begins by discussing the falsity of the universal accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya kulliyya), explaining that a relation existing in some states negates accidentalness, but this does not apply to the particular accidental conditional because it considers only actualized states. Then the proof is stated: the third figure is used, where the premises are 'Whenever the sum of two things is realized, one of them is realized' and 'Whenever the sum is realized, the other is realized', leading to the conclusion 'Some cases where one is realized, the other is realized'. Alternatively, the first figure can be used by converting the minor premise. An objection that the minor premise would be accidental is refuted because the converse of an implicative conditional remains implicative. Next, a scholar (al-muhaqqiq) attempts to escape by arguing that the sum only entails a part if each part has a causal role; here, one part is redundant, so the premises are not universal. The reply is that implication (luzum) does not require causation; it is merely impossibility of separation (imtina' al-infikak). Two impossibilities can imply each other, so the connection suffices. Then another objection: some deny the universality of the premise 'whenever the sum is realized, one of them is realized' because if the sum itself is impossible, it might separate from the part, based on the principle that an impossibility can imply an impossibility. The author notes that this is the correct view. A further reflection states that the entailment of the whole for the part is necessary, and its denial is stubbornness; the impossibility of the antecedent allows it to also imply the separation of the part, leading to two conditionals with conflicting consequents, which is not absurd. Finally, a remaining point: the author claims a particular implication between every two actual existents, and proves it by taking a universal premise with respect to actual estimations. This shows that the universal particular accidental conditional (which asserts constant truth without implication) is invalid. The passage ends with an exhortation to contemplate due to its subtlety.

Key Terms

انفكاك
اقتضاء
تأثير
امتناع الانفكاك

Key Takeaway
Logical implication is defined as the impossibility of separation, not causation; this distinction allows the third-figure proof to stand despite objections about causal roles.

Proving Universal Negative Conversion by Reductio
Author proves that a universal negative proposition converts by reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio proof that a universal negative proposition converts to another universal negative.

Core Idea
Universal negative conversion is proven not by direct inference but by reductio ad absurdum.

In this passage, the author establishes a fundamental rule of immediate inference: the conversion of a universal negative proposition. Unlike immediate conversions that can be seen directly, this one requires a careful reductio argument (khulf). By assuming the opposite of what we want to prove and deriving an impossibility, we demonstrate that the conversion must hold. This method is central to the rigorous approach of Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim to be proved
The claim is that if 'No C is B' is true, then 'No B is C' must also be true. This is called conversion (عكس) of a universal negative.
The original proposition and its converse have the same truth value.
2
Assume the contradictory of the converse
To prove by reductio, assume the converse is false. Then its contradictory, 'Some B is C', must be true. This is the starting assumption of the proof.
The contradictory of a universal negative is a particular affirmative.
3
Combine the assumption with the original premise
Take the original universal negative 'No C is B' as the major premise, and the assumed particular affirmative 'Some B is C' as the minor premise. Construct a categorical syllogism.
This syllogism is in the first figure? Actually it's in the second figure because middle term is C? But we just note the result.
4
Derive an impossibility
From the syllogism, the conclusion 'Some B is not B' follows. This is a contradiction—an impossibility (محال). Therefore, the assumption that the converse is false cannot be true.
The conclusion 'Some B is not B' violates the principle of non-contradiction.
5
Conclude the converse must be true
Since the assumption leads to impossibility, the converse must be true when the original is true. This establishes the validity of universal negative conversion.
The proof shows that the conversion is necessary, not just probable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسالبة الكلية تنعكس كنفسها بالخلف، وهو ههنا ضم نقيض العكس مع الأصل، فينتج المحال، تقريره: لو لم يصدق «لا شيء من (ب) (ج)» مع صدق لا شيء من (ج) (ب)» لصدق نقيضه، وهو بعض (ب) (ج)»، ونجعله لإيجابه صغرى، والأصل الكلية كبرى، ينتج بعض (ب) ليس (ب)»، وهو محال، وقس عليه البيان في الشرطية. فصدق النقيض مع الأصل ممتنع، فيجب صدق العكس معه؛ لأن الأصل صادق في الواقع، فلو لم يصدق العكس معه ولا نقيضه يلزم ارتفاع النقيضين في الواقع، وهو المطلوب.
And the universal negative converts as itself by khulf (reductio), which here is joining the contradictory of the converse with the original, resulting in impossibility. Its exposition: If 'No B is C' is not true while 'No C is B' is true, then its contradictory 'Some B is C' would be true. We make it, because it is affirmative, the minor premise, and the original universal [negative] the major, resulting in 'Some B is not B', which is impossible. And analogously for the conditional. So the truth of the contradictory with the original is impossible, hence the truth of the converse with it is necessary; because the original is true in reality. If neither the converse nor its contradictory were true with it, it would entail the elevation of both contradictories in reality. And that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage proves that a universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No C is B') can be validly converted to another universal negative ('No B is C'). The proof uses reductio ad absurdum (khulf). The method is: assume the converse is false, so its contradictory (a particular affirmative 'Some B is C') must be true. Combine this particular affirmative as a minor premise with the original universal negative as a major premise: 'Some B is C' and 'No C is B' yield 'Some B is not B' — a contradiction. Therefore the assumption that the converse is false is impossible, so the converse must be true when the original is true. The author also notes that the same reasoning applies to conditional propositions. This establishes the validity of universal negative conversion.

نقيض
المحال

Key Takeaway

Iftiraad: Hypothesization as Third Figure Syllogism
Definition of iftiraad and its use as a third-figure syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct an iftiraad argument and identify its third-figure structure.

Core Idea
Iftiraad proves conversion by positing an individual that shares both descriptions.

The method of iftiraad (hypothesization) is a logical technique used to prove that two predicates are true of the same subject. This lesson explains how it forms a valid third-figure syllogism, as defined by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two descriptions
In the iftiraad method, we have a subject description (J) and a predicate description (B). These are the two descriptions we aim to combine.
2
Posit a hypothetical individual (D)
We hypothesize an individual D that is both described by J and B. This individual serves as the subject of both premises.
3
Form the two premises
From the hypothesis we get: D is B and D is J. These are the two premises of the syllogism.
4
Recognize the third figure
Both premises have D as subject, so D is the middle term. The terms are arranged as subject-subject, which is the third figure.
5
Derive the conclusion
From D is B and D is J, we deduce that some B is J. This is a particular affirmative conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالِافْتِرَاضِ ، وَهُوَ أَنْ نَفْرِضَ ذَاتَ الْمَوْضُوع شَيْئًا وَيُحْمَلَ عَلَيْهِ وَصْفُ الْمَوْضُوع وَوَصْفُ الْمَحْمُولِ ، فَتَقُولُ : نَقْرِضُ (ج) الَّذِي هُوَ (ب) (د) ، فـ(د) (ب) ، و(د) (ج) ، فَبَعْضُ (ب) (ج) بِالْفِعْل مِنَ الشكل (الثَّالِثِ). وهذا نص من المصنف على أن الافتراض استدلال بالشكل الثالث، فحينئذ لا يجوز بيان الشكل الثالث بالعكس، والحق أنه ليس شكلا ثالثا، بل حاصله أن وصف (ج) و (ب) اجتمعا في ذات، فتلك الذات إن عبرت بـ(ب) يثبت له (ج) أيضا، فصدق «بعض (ب) (ج)»، وهذا يمكن أن يثبت به إنتاج الشكل الثالث أيضا ، كذا حققه الشيخ . وما قال النصير الطوسي : إنه ليس كذلك ؛ لأن الحدود ليست متباينة، أن يكون ولا بعضها محمولا على بعض، فالصورة ليست بقياس، فضلا عن من الشكل الثالث، فمما يقضي العجب من مثله، فالحدود ثلاثة قطعا: الذات الموصوفة بـ(ج) المسماة بـ(د)، ووصف (ج)، ووصف (ب)، فحينئذ يمكن انعقاد الشكل الثالث ، والله أعلم . ثم اعلم أن هذا أيضا لا يتم على رأي الفارابي ؛ إذ (د) لا يصدق عليه (ج) بالفعل على رأيه ، بل بالإمكان ، فلا يلزم إلا بعض (ب) (ج) بالإمكان .
And the method of hypothesization: it is to hypothesize the subject itself as something, and the description of the subject and the description of the predicate are applied to it. So you say: we hypothesize J which is B, (let it be D), so D is B, and D is J, thus some B is J in actuality, (this is) from the third figure. This is a text from the author that hypothesization is inference by the third figure. Therefore, it is not permissible to explain the third figure by conversion. The truth is that it is not a third figure, but its essence is that the descriptions J and B are combined in a self; then that self, if expressed by B, J is also established for it, so 'some B is J' is true. And this can also be used to prove the productivity of the third figure—this is what the shaykh verified. And what al-Tusi said—that it is not so, because the terms are not distinct, nor is any of them predicated of another, so the form is not a syllogism, let alone from the third figure—this is something that causes wonder from the likes of him. For the terms are three definitely: the self described by J, named D; the description J; and the description B. So then the third figure can be constituted. And Allah knows best. Then know that this also is not valid according to al-Farabi's view, because D is not actually true of J in his view, but only possibly, so only 'some B is J possibly' follows.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage defines the logical method of 'al-iftirad' (hypothesization) and explains how it constitutes a valid syllogism in the third figure, contrary to the objection of al-Tusi. The author begins by defining iftirad: we hypothesize an individual (D) that has both the description of the subject (J) and the description of the predicate (B). From this we get two premises: D is B and D is J, which yields the conclusion 'some B is J'. This is a familiar form of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The author notes that the original author (al-Sullam) considered iftirad as inference by the third figure, and thus one should not explain the third figure by conversion, as that would be circular. The commentator then clarifies that this is indeed a valid third figure syllogism, not a different form, because two predicates (B and J) are both affirmed of a subject (D). He refutes al-Tusi's criticism by pointing out that three distinct terms are present: D (the subject), J (the predicate of the second premise), and B (the predicate of the conclusion), thus the third figure is properly constituted. Finally, he notes that according to al-Farabi's view, the premise 'D is J' would only be possible, not actual, so the conclusion would be a particular possible rather than actual. This highlights a disagreement among logicians about modal conversion.

Key Terms
الافتراض
الشكل الثالث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) First figure
B) Second figure
C) Third figure
D) No figure, it is not a syllogism

Key Takeaway
Iftiraad is a valid third-figure syllogism that uses a hypothesized individual to prove that two predicates apply to the same thing.
