Objections to Iftiraad: Al-Tusi and Al-Farabi
Refutation of al-Tusi's objection and qualification regarding al-Farabi's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objections raised by al-Tusi and al-Farabi against iftiraad and explain why the commentator finds them invalid or conditional.

Core Idea
Al-Tusi's objection misses that three distinct terms exist; Al-Farabi's view requires modal qualification.

The commentator defends the syllogistic validity of iftiraad against two important critics: Nasir al-Din al-Tusi and Abu Nasr al-Farabi. This lesson examines their objections and the commentator's responses.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Tusi's objection
Al-Tusi argued that iftiraad is not a syllogism because the terms are not distinct – the predicate and subject descriptions are not separate from each other, and none is predicated of the other.
2
Analyze the commentator's refutation
The commentator responds that there are in fact three distinct terms: the self (D), the description J, and the description B. D is the subject of both premises, J and B are predicates, so the third figure is properly constituted.
3
Understand al-Farabi's modal restriction
Al-Farabi held that the premise 'D is J' is not true in actuality but only in possibility, affecting the modality of the conclusion.
4
Evaluate the impact on iftiraad
Under al-Farabi's view, the conclusion becomes 'some B is J possibly' rather than actually, but the syllogistic form remains valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالِافْتِرَاضِ ، وَهُوَ أَنْ نَفْرِضَ ذَاتَ الْمَوْضُوع شَيْئًا وَيُحْمَلَ عَلَيْهِ وَصْفُ الْمَوْضُوع وَوَصْفُ الْمَحْمُولِ ، فَتَقُولُ : نَقْرِضُ (ج) الَّذِي هُوَ (ب) (د) ، فـ(د) (ب) ، و(د) (ج) ، فَبَعْضُ (ب) (ج) بِالْفِعْل مِنَ الشكل (الثَّالِثِ). وهذا نص من المصنف على أن الافتراض استدلال بالشكل الثالث، فحينئذ لا يجوز بيان الشكل الثالث بالعكس، والحق أنه ليس شكلا ثالثا، بل حاصله أن وصف (ج) و (ب) اجتمعا في ذات، فتلك الذات إن عبرت بـ(ب) يثبت له (ج) أيضا، فصدق «بعض (ب) (ج)»، وهذا يمكن أن يثبت به إنتاج الشكل الثالث أيضا ، كذا حققه الشيخ . وما قال النصير الطوسي : إنه ليس كذلك ؛ لأن الحدود ليست متباينة، أن يكون ولا بعضها محمولا على بعض، فالصورة ليست بقياس، فضلا عن من الشكل الثالث، فمما يقضي العجب من مثله، فالحدود ثلاثة قطعا: الذات الموصوفة بـ(ج) المسماة بـ(د)، ووصف (ج)، ووصف (ب)، فحينئذ يمكن انعقاد الشكل الثالث ، والله أعلم . ثم اعلم أن هذا أيضا لا يتم على رأي الفارابي ؛ إذ (د) لا يصدق عليه (ج) بالفعل على رأيه ، بل بالإمكان ، فلا يلزم إلا بعض (ب) (ج) بالإمكان .
And the method of hypothesization: it is to hypothesize the subject itself as something, and the description of the subject and the description of the predicate are applied to it. So you say: we hypothesize J which is B, (let it be D), so D is B, and D is J, thus some B is J in actuality, (this is) from the third figure. This is a text from the author that hypothesization is inference by the third figure. Therefore, it is not permissible to explain the third figure by conversion. The truth is that it is not a third figure, but its essence is that the descriptions J and B are combined in a self; then that self, if expressed by B, J is also established for it, so 'some B is J' is true. And this can also be used to prove the productivity of the third figure—this is what the shaykh verified. And what al-Tusi said—that it is not so, because the terms are not distinct, nor is any of them predicated of another, so the form is not a syllogism, let alone from the third figure—this is something that causes wonder from the likes of him. For the terms are three definitely: the self described by J, named D; the description J; and the description B. So then the third figure can be constituted. And Allah knows best. Then know that this also is not valid according to al-Farabi's view, because D is not actually true of J in his view, but only possibly, so only 'some B is J possibly' follows.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage defines the logical method of 'al-iftirad' (hypothesization) and explains how it constitutes a valid syllogism in the third figure, contrary to the objection of al-Tusi. The author begins by defining iftirad: we hypothesize an individual (D) that has both the description of the subject (J) and the description of the predicate (B). From this we get two premises: D is B and D is J, which yields the conclusion 'some B is J'. This is a familiar form of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The author notes that the original author (al-Sullam) considered iftirad as inference by the third figure, and thus one should not explain the third figure by conversion, as that would be circular. The commentator then clarifies that this is indeed a valid third figure syllogism, not a different form, because two predicates (B and J) are both affirmed of a subject (D). He refutes al-Tusi's criticism by pointing out that three distinct terms are present: D (the subject), J (the predicate of the second premise), and B (the predicate of the conclusion), thus the third figure is properly constituted. Finally, he notes that according to al-Farabi's view, the premise 'D is J' would only be possible, not actual, so the conclusion would be a particular possible rather than actual. This highlights a disagreement among logicians about modal conversion.

Key Terms
الافتراض
الشكل الثالث
النصير الطوسي
الفارابي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The conclusion is not certain
B) The terms are not distinct
C) The middle term is not distributed
D) The figure is impossible

Key Takeaway
The objections of al-Tusi and al-Farabi challenge but do not ultimately refute the logical validity of iftiraad, though al-Farabi's view modalizes the conclusion.

The Definition of Qiyas: Statement, Composition, and Plurality
Author defines qiyas and explains why 'mu'allaf' and plural are necessary in the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three necessary components of the qiyas definition and explain why each is included.

Core Idea
A syllogism is not just any inference; it must be composed of multiple propositions.

The author begins by stating that the mainstay of logic is the syllogism (qiyas). He then provides a precise definition and immediately clarifies potential misunderstandings. Understanding why each word is chosen is the key to mastering the concept of qiyas in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined. The author defines 'al-qiyas' (the syllogism). The definition phrase is: 'qawlun mu'allafun min qadaya yalzamu 'anha li-dhatiha qawlun akhar'.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'qawl mu'allaf' (a composed statement). The word 'mu'allaf' is deliberately used to avoid the misinterpretation that the particle 'min' (from) is partitive, i.e., that the syllogism is merely some propositions rather than a whole composed of them.
Without 'mu'allaf', one might think a syllogism is just a part of the premises, not the composite whole.
3
Identify the differentia
The differentia is 'yalzamu 'anha li-dhatiha qawlun akhar' (another statement follows from it by itself). This distinguishes qiyas from other composed statements that do not entail a conclusion necessarily.
The phrase 'li-dhatiha' (by itself) emphasizes that the implication is intrinsic to the premises, not due to an external factor.
4
Examine the plural requirement
The word 'qadaya' (propositions) is plural. This excludes a single proposition that might imply another, such as in immediate inference (al-'aks). A syllogism requires at least two premises. A compound proposition is not conventionally called 'propositions' (plural).
The plural 'qadaya' ensures that the definition covers only arguments with multiple premises, not simple conversions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف القياس. وَالْعُمْدَةُ الْقِيَاسُ، وَهُوَ قَوْلٌ مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ قضايا يَلْزَمُ عَنْهَا لِذَاتِهَا. قال المصنف: ومحترزاته الأظهر ذكر المؤلف بعد القول؛ لئلا يذهب إلى أن ((مِنْ) تبعيضية). واحترز بإيراد الجمع عن القضية الواحدة المستلزمة لأخرى، كالعكس، سواء كانت بسيطة أو مركبة؛ إذ لا يقال للمركبة عرفا قضايا والمراد بالجمع ما فوق الواحد.
Definition of syllogism. The mainstay is the syllogism, and it is a statement composed of propositions from which another follows by itself. The author said: Its most apparent reserving conditions are that the author mentioned 'composed' after 'statement' to prevent misunderstanding that 'min' is partitive. And by mentioning the plural [qadaya], he reserved against a single proposition that implies another, as in conversion, whether it is simple or compound, because what is compound is not conventionally called 'propositions', and the intended meaning of 'plural' is more than one.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage defines qiyas (syllogism) as the most important type of proof. The definition is: 'a statement composed of propositions from which another follows by itself.' The author then explains the exclusory conditions. The word 'composed' (mu'allaf) is used to avoid the interpretation that 'min' (from) might indicate part-whole, i.e., that the syllogism is merely some propositions. Also, 'propositions' (qadaya) in plural excludes a single proposition that implies another, such as in immediate inference (conversion), because a syllogism requires at least two premises. And a compound proposition is not called 'propositions' in the conventional sense; the plural here means more than one.

مؤلف
قضايا

D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
A valid syllogism requires at least two premises intentionally composed, from which a conclusion necessarily follows by itself.

The Condition of Luzum in Defining Qiyas
Author distinguishes qiyas from induction and analogy by the condition of luzum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of luzum in the definition of qiyas and apply it to distinguish qiyas from incomplete induction and analogical arguments with probable premises.

Core Idea
Luzum means necessary entailment, not actual truth of premises.

In this passage, the commentator refines the definition of qiyas by emphasizing that the entailment (luzum) is a logical relation: if the premises are true, the conclusion must be true. This subtle but powerful condition distinguishes qiyas from other forms of reasoning that do not guarantee necessary conclusion even if their premises are true.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitional term: Luzum
Find the term 'اللزوم' in the Arabic. It is the key condition in the definition of qiyas that is used to exclude induction and analogy.
The author says 'احترز باللزوم عن الاستقراء والتمثيل'.
2
Understand what luzum is not: Actual truth
The passage clarifies that luzum does not mean the premises are actually true, but if they were assumed true the conclusion would follow. This preserves the inclusion of sophistical syllogisms (القياس السوفسطائي).
The commentator says: 'المراد باللزوم كون المقدمتين بحيث لو فرضتا صادقتين لزم صدق النتيجة'.
3
Apply the condition: Exclusion of incomplete induction
Incomplete induction does not guarantee the conclusion because not all particulars are examined. Thus it fails the luzum condition and is excluded from qiyas.
The author divides induction into complete (داخل في المحدود) and incomplete (لا ندعي فيه الحصر).
4
Apply the condition: Analogy with probable cause
Even if the cause is probable, the logical entailment still holds if the premises are assumed true. Luzum is about logical structure, not certainty of premises.
The commentator responds: 'هذا لا يضر؛ إذ المراد باللزوم كون المقدمتين بحيث لو فرضتا صادقتين لزم صدق النتيجة'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحجة
قَوْلُ آخَرُ، احترز باللزوم عن الاستقراء والتمثيل؛ إذ لا يلزم منهما شيء. فإن قلت: حاصل الاستقراء أن الحكم ثابت ؛ لأنه منحصر في هذا الجزئي وذلك إلى غير ذلك، وتلك الجزئيات كلها يثبت لها المحمول، فالموضوع ثبت له المحمول ؟
قلت: الاستقراء قسمان، تام ويسمى قياسا مَقْسِما، وهو داخل في المحدود والحد وناقص ولا ندعي فيه الحصر، كما يجيئ إن شاء الله تعالى، وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم منه شيء.
بقي أمر التمثيل، الظاهر أنه لا يخرج عن قيد اللزوم؛ لأن حاصله أن هذا الحكم في هذا الجزئي ثابت، كالحرمة في البنج؛ لأنه مشارك للأصل كالخمر في علة الحكم كالإسكار، وكلما هو مشارك للأصل في علة الحكم، فالحكم ثابت فيه، فهذا الجزئي الحكم ثابت فيه، وهذه المقدمات مستلزمة للنتيجة قطعا.
فإن قلت: كونه مشاركا للأصل في علة الحكم أمر مظنون؛ لجواز كون الأصل شرطا أو الفرع مانعا؟
قلت: هذا لا يضر؛ إذ المراد باللزوم كون المقدمتين بحيث لو فرضتا صادقتين لزم صدق النتيجة، لا أن المقدمات والنتيجة صوادق في نفس الأمر، وإلا خرج القياس السوفسطائي. هذا، والله أعلم.
Section on the Proof.
Another statement: He guarded [the definition] by 'entailment' against induction and analogy, for nothing is entailed from them. If you say: The result of induction is that the judgment is established because it is restricted to this particular and that, and so on, and all those particulars have the predicate affirmed of them; so the subject has the predicate affirmed of it? I say: Induction is of two kinds: complete, which is called divisive syllogism, and it is included in the definition and the definiendum; and incomplete, and we do not claim [complete] enumeration for it, as will come, God willing. And if that is the case, nothing is entailed from it.
There remains the matter of analogy. Apparently, it does not exit from the condition of entailment, because its result is that this judgment is established in this particular, like the prohibition in henbane because it shares with the original, like wine, in the cause of the judgment, like intoxication. And everything that shares with the original in the cause of the judgment, the judgment is established in it. So this particular, the judgment is established in it; and these premises necessarily entail the conclusion.
If you say: Its sharing with the original in the cause of the judgment is a matter of probability, because it is possible that the original has a condition or the branch has an impediment? I say: This does not harm because what is meant by entailment is that the two premises are such that if they were assumed true, the truth of the conclusion would be entailed, not that the premises and conclusion are actually true in themselves; otherwise the sophistical syllogism would be excluded. This is, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of qiyas (syllogism) that uses the condition of luzum (entailment) to exclude induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil) from the strict definition. The author first states that the term 'luzum' in the definition is meant to exclude istiqra' and tamthil because they do not yield necessary entailment. A potential objection is raised: in induction, the conclusion seems to follow from the premises. The commentator responds by dividing induction into complete and incomplete. Complete induction (istiqra' tam) i.e., enumeration of all particulars, is actually called 'qiyas maqsim' and is included in the definition, but incomplete induction is not claimed to have complete enumeration, so no entailment. The author then addresses analogy (tamthil). At first glance, analogy does seem to involve entailment because it uses a shared cause ('illa) to transfer the ruling from the original to the branch, as in the prohibition of henbane due to intoxication similar to wine. This appears to be a valid entailment. However, a further objection: the determination that the cause is shared is merely probable, as there could be an additional condition in the original or an impediment in the branch. The commentator's answer clarifies that the 'luzum' in the definition refers to a hypothetical logical entailment: if the premises are true, then the conclusion must be true. It does not require actual truth of the premises in reality; otherwise, the sophistical syllogism (i.e., a fallacious argument that appears valid but has false premises) would be excluded, but it should not be. Thus, the probability of the premises does not affect the entailment itself. This discussion highlights the strict epistemological criteria for qiyas in the Islamic logical tradition.

Key Terms
اللزوم
الاستقراء
التمثيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the condition of 'luzum' in the definition of qiyas primarily ensure?
A) That the premises are actually true
B) That the conclusion is entailed if premises are assumed true
C) That the argument is probable
D) That the premises are certain

Key Takeaway
The condition of luzum in defining qiyas is logical entailment, not actual truth, which allows qiyas to include arguments with probable premises while excluding incomplete induction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the following argument: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Does this argument satisfy the condition of luzum? Explain why, referencing the passage.
?

Induction and Analogy in Light of the Luzum Condition
Author distinguishes qiyas from induction and analogy by the condition of luzum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify inductive and analogical arguments as either valid qiyas or not based on the luzum condition as explained in the passage.

Core Idea
Complete induction is qiyas; incomplete induction is not. Analogy is qiyas if the entailment holds under assumption.

The commentator addresses two objections: that induction seems to entail its conclusion, and that analogy seems probable. He shows that only complete induction qualifies as qiyas, while analogy does qualify because the luzum condition is about logical entailment, not probability.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish complete from incomplete induction
The passage divides istiqra' into tam (complete) and naqis (incomplete). Complete induction covers all individuals, so the conclusion follows necessarily. Incomplete does not cover all, so no entailment.
The author says: 'الاستقراء قسمان، تام ويسمى قياسا مَقْسِما، وهو داخل في المحدود والحد وناقص ولا ندعي فيه الحصر'.
2
Identify complete induction as qiyas maqsim
Complete induction is called 'qiyas maqsim' (divisive syllogism) and is included within the definition of qiyas. It satisfies the luzum condition because enumerating all particulars yields necessary conclusion.
This shows that not all induction is excluded; only incomplete induction fails.
3
Analyse analogy's entailment
Analogy uses a shared cause ('illa). The argument form is: original has ruling R due to cause C; branch has cause C; so branch has ruling R. This is a valid syllogism if premises are true. Thus luzum holds.
The example given is henbane (بنج) being prohibited like wine due to intoxication (الإسكار).
4
Address the probability objection
Even if the presence of cause C is only probable, the logical entailment still holds if we assume it true. Luzum does not require actual certainty of premises.
The commentator responds: 'هذا لا يضر' because luzum is about hypothetical truth.

2. In a fiqh debate, someone argues: 'Wine is forbidden because it intoxicates. Nabidh also intoxicates, therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Apply the luzum condition to this argument and state whether it qualifies as qiyas according to the passage.

Essential Entailment in Syllogisms
Author clarifies condition of essential entailment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a syllogism satisfies essential entailment or relies on an extraneous premise.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism must entail its conclusion directly, without an unstated premise.

The author carefully defines 'essential entailment' (al-luzum al-dhati) to exclude arguments where the conclusion actually depends on a hidden premise. This precision safeguards the integrity of logical proof in Islamic sciences. We will learn to detect such extraneous assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Understand luzum dhati
The conclusion must follow from the premises without an intermediary in attribution (wasita fi al-‘urud). This means the entailment is direct.
2
Identify the exclusion
Arguments where the conclusion really depends on an extraneous premise (muqaddima ajnabiyya) are not considered true syllogisms in the technical sense with respect to that conclusion.
The extra premise may be unstated but necessary.
3
Examine the example
From 'A equals B' and 'B equals C' alone, 'A equals C' does not follow without the transitivity premise 'Everything equal to something equal to C is equal to C'.
This shows why the syllogism of equality is not a simple qiyas.
4
Classify relations
Some relations (entailment, dependence) often have true transitivity premises; others (halving, doubling) may have false ones, making the conclusion unreliable.
Essential entailment requires that the conclusion be entailed by the original premises alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَخْرَجُوا بِاللُّزُومِ الذَّاتِي، والمراد به كون المطلوب لازما للقضايا، من غير واسطة في العروض، ما يكون لازمة لمقدمة أجنبية، بحيث يكون لولا المقدمة الأجنبية لم يلزم منها شيء، بل يكون المطلوب لازما بالحقيقة للمقدمة الأجنبية وتلك المقدمات معا.
أما غير لازمة لأصل المقدمات في الصدق كما في قياس المساواة، وهو المركب من قضيتين متعلق محمول الأولى موضوع الأخرى، نحو (أ) مساء ل(ب)، و(ب) مساو ل(ج)، يلزم منه بواسطة كل مساء لمساو ل(ج) أن (أ) مساو ل(ج)، فهذا القياس الخالي عن تلك المقدمة لا يسمى قياسا اصطلاحا بالنسبة إلى هذه النتيجة، فحيث تصدق تلك المقدمة - كاللزوم، نحو (أ) لازم ل(ب)، و(ب) لازم ل(ج)، والتوقف، نحو (أ) موقوف على (ب)، و(ب) موقوف على (ج) تصدق تلك النتيجة؛ لأنها بالحقيقة نتيجة لمجموع تلك المقدمة والمقدمات، وفيما لا تصدق تلك المقدمة فلا يصدق النتيجة لزوما، بل قد تكذب، كالتناصف والتضاعف، نحو الواحد نصف الاثنين، والاثنان نصف الأربع، وبالعكس، وقد يصدق كما في المباينة.
And they excluded by essential entailment, by which is meant that the required [conclusion] is entailed by the propositions without an intermediary in attribution, that which is entailed by an extraneous premise, such that were it not for the extraneous premise, nothing would be entailed from them, rather the required is in reality entailed by the extraneous premise and those premises together.
As for that which is not entailed by the original premises in truth, as in the syllogism of equality, which is composed of two propositions such that the predicate of the first is related to the subject of the second, like: A is equal to B, and B is equal to C. From this, by means of [the premise] 'everything equal to what is equal to C is equal to C', it follows that A is equal to C. So this syllogism, devoid of that premise, is not called a syllogism technically with respect to this conclusion. Now when that premise is true—like entailment, e.g. A entails B, and B entails C; and dependence, e.g. A depends on B, and B depends on C—then that conclusion is true, because it is in reality the conclusion of the set of that premise and the [original] premises. And when that premise is not true, then the conclusion is not necessarily true; rather it may be false, like halving and doubling, e.g. one is half of two, and two is half of four, and conversely; and it may be true, as in disparity.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage further refines the notion of 'luzum' in the definition of qiyas. It introduces 'al-luzum al-dhati' (essential entailment) which requires that the conclusion follows directly from the premises without any 'intermediary in attribution' (wasita fi al-‘urud). The author then discusses the case where the conclusion actually depends on an extraneous premise (muqaddima ajnabiyya). If that extraneous premise is not explicitly included in the premises, then the argument is not a true syllogism in the technical sense with respect to that conclusion. As an example, the syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawah) is given: from 'A equals B' and 'B equals C', one cannot directly conclude 'A equals C' without the additional premise 'Everything equal to something equal to C is equal to C'. This extra premise is the 'extraneous premise'. In the standard account, if that premise is true, then the conclusion is true as a result of the total set. The author notes that this pattern applies also to entailment (luzum) and dependence (tawaqquf). In contrast, with relations like halving and doubling (tanasuf wa tada'uf), the additional premise may be false, leading to a false conclusion, though sometimes truth occurs by accident. The key point is that the condition of 'luzum dhati' ensures that the conclusion is entailed solely by the stated premises without needing an unstated premise. This is a technical precision ensuring the definition of qiyas captures only valid arguments where the conclusion is strictly entailed.

Key Terms
اللزوم الذاتي (al-luzum al-dhātī): Essential entailment: the conclusion follows from the premises directly without an intermediary in attribution, and not requiring an extraneous premise. [Note: This is a standard term in later mantiq works; stable meaning.]
مقدمة أجنبية (muqaddima ajnabiyya): An extraneous premise that is not among the original premises but is necessary for the entailment of the conclusion. [Note: Often used in discussions of compound syllogisms; in earlier texts, such premises are sometimes considered part of the implicit reasoning.]
قياس المساواة (qiyas al-musāwāh): Syllogism of equality: a compound syllogism requiring a transitivity premise to connect the terms. [Note: This is a specific type of compound syllogism; later works classify it under qiyas murakkab.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which condition excludes the syllogism of equality from being a true syllogism (in the technical sense) with respect to its conclusion?
A) The conclusion is false.
B) It requires an extraneous premise.
C) The premises are not true.
D) It has three premises.

Key Takeaway
Essential entailment ensures the conclusion follows solely from the stated premises, excluding arguments that rely on hidden assumptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'All intoxicants are forbidden. All nabidh is intoxicating. Therefore, nabidh is forbidden.' Does this argument satisfy essential entailment? Explain why or why not with reference to the condition of luzum dhati.

The Syllogism of Equality as a Compound Syllogism
Author discusses syllogism of equality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a syllogism of equality and state the extraneous premise it requires.

Core Idea
The syllogism of equality is a compound syllogism needing a transitivity premise to connect equals.

The author uses the well-known example of qiyas al-musawah to illustrate an argument that appears simple but actually requires an extra premise. Understanding this helps us recognize compound syllogisms in Islamic logic and prevents us from misclassifying them as simple qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the pattern
The syllogism of equality consists of two premises where the predicate of the first is the subject of the second, e.g., 'A is equal to B' and 'B is equal to C'.
2
Identify the missing premise
The transitivity premise 'Everything equal to something equal to C is equal to C' is required to deduce 'A is equal to C'. Without it, the conclusion is not entailed.
This extra premise is an extraneous premise (muqaddima ajnabiyya).
3
Check truth conditions
When the transitivity premise is true, the conclusion follows from the total set. When false, the conclusion may be false, as with halving and doubling.
The passage notes relations like entailment and dependence often have true transitivity premises.
4
Compare with simple syllogisms
In a simple syllogism, the conclusion follows directly from the two premises without any additional assumption. Qiyas al-musawah is not simple because it requires an extra premise.
This distinction is crucial for evaluating logical proofs in Islamic sciences.

Essential Implication and the Exclusion of Qiyas al-Musawat
Author defines qiyas al-musawat and explains why it is excluded from the technical definition of qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an argument satisfies the condition of essential implication and recognize arguments like qiyas al-musawat that require an extraneous premise.

Core Idea
Essential implication: conclusion must follow from premises alone, no hidden premises.

In this lesson, we explore a critical condition for a valid syllogism: the conclusion must be implied by the premises themselves, without relying on an unstated premise. The author introduces the concept of essential implication (al-luzum al-dhati) and uses the example of qiyas al-musawat (the syllogism of equality) to show what fails this condition. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to evaluate arguments in Islamic logic and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of essential implication
Read the author's definition: the conclusion must follow from the premises without an intermediary in attribution. Note that it does not require actual truth, only hypothetical truth-preservation.
2
Identify the example of qiyas al-musawat
Find the description: two propositions where the predicate of the first is the subject of the second, like A=B and B=C. Why does this not satisfy essential implication?
3
See why an extraneous premise is needed
The conclusion A=C only follows if we add the premise 'everything equal to an equal of C is equal to C'. Without it, the entailment fails.
4
Determine the consequence
The author says such a syllogism is not called a technical qiyas with respect to that conclusion unless the extra premise is added.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل شرطا أو الفرع مانعا ؟
:قلت هذا لا يضر ؛ إذ المراد باللزوم كون المقدمتين بحيث لو فرضتا صادقتين لزم صدق النتيجة، لا أن المقدمات والنتيجة صوادق في نفس الأمر ، وإلا خرج القياس السوفسطائي. هذا، والله أعلم.
(وَأَخْرَجُوا بِاللُّزُومِ الذَّاتِي)، والمراد به كون المطلوب لازما للقضايا، من غير واسطة في العروض (مَا يَكُونُ) لازمة (لِمُقَدِّمَةٍ أَجْنَبِيَّةِ)، بحيث يكون لولا المقدمة الأجنبية لم يلزم منها شيء، بل يكون المطلوب لازما بالحقيقة للمقدمة الأجنبية وتلك المقدمات معا.
أَمَّا غَيْرُ لاَزِمَةٍ) لأصل المقدمات في الصدق (كَمَا فِي قِيَاسِ الْمُسَاوَاةِ، وَهُوَ الْمُرَكَّبُ مِنْ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ مُتَعَلِّقُ مَحْمُولِ الْأُولَى مَوْضُوعُ الْأُخْرَى، نَحْوُ (أ) مُسَاوٍ لِبَ)، وَ(بَ) مُسَاوِ لِ(جَ) ، يَلْزَمُ مِنْهُ بِوَاسِطَةِ كُلُّ مُسَاوٍ لِمُسَاوِ لِ(جَ) أَنَّ (أ) مُسَاوِ لِـ (ج)) ، فهذا القياس الخالي عن تلك المقدمة لا يسمى قياسا اصطلاحا بالنسبة إلى هذه النتيجة ، (فَحَيْثُ تَصْدُقُ تِلْكَ الْمُقَدِّمَةُ - كَاللُّزُوم)، نحو (أ) لازم لـ(ب)، و(ب) لازم لـ(ج)، (وَالتَّوَقُفِ - ) ، نحو (أ) موقوف على (ب) ، و(ب) موقوف على (ج) (تَصْدُقُ تِلْكَ النَّتِيجَةُ) ؛ لأنها بالحقيقة نتيجة لمجموع تلك المقدمة والمقدمات، (وَفِيمَا لا يصدق تلك المقدمة (فَلَا) يصدق النتيجة لزوما، بل قد تكذب، ( كَالتَنَاصُفِ وَالتَّضَاعُف)، نحو الواحد نصف الاثنين، والاثنان نصف الأربع، وبالعكس، وقد يصدق كما في المباينة .
The original is either a condition or the branch is an obstacle? I said: This does not harm because what is meant by implication is that the two premises are such that if they were assumed true, the truth of the conclusion would follow, not that the premises and conclusion are true in reality; otherwise the sophistical syllogism would be excluded. This, and Allah knows best. And they excluded by essential implication—and what is meant by it is that the required conclusion is necessary for the propositions without an intermediary in attribution—that which is necessary due to an extraneous premise, such that without the extraneous premise nothing would follow from it, but rather the required conclusion is in reality necessary for the extraneous premise and those premises together. As for that which is not necessary for the original premises in truth, such as in the syllogism of equality, which is composed of two propositions where the predicate of the first is the subject of the second, like (A is equal to B) and (B is equal to C); it follows from it by means of (everything equal to an equal of C) that (A is equal to C). So this syllogism empty of that premise is not called a syllogism terminologically with respect to this conclusion. So wherever that premise is true—like implication, e.g., (A is necessary for B) and (B is necessary for C), and like dependence, e.g., (A depends on B) and (B depends on C)—that conclusion is true, because it is in reality the conclusion of the sum of that premise and the premises. And where that premise is not true, the conclusion does not necessarily follow; rather it may be false, like half and double, e.g., one is half of two and two is half of four, and conversely, and it may be true as in disparity.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of essential implication (al-luzum al-dhati) and its role in distinguishing proper syllogisms from incomplete ones. The author first clarifies that the required implication is a logical entailment: if the premises were true, the conclusion must be true, not that they actually are true. This prevents sophistical syllogisms from being counted. Then, he explains that through essential implication, logicians exclude cases where a conclusion depends on an extraneous premise. The example given is the syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawat): from 'A = B' and 'B = C', we cannot directly conclude 'A = C' without the additional premise 'everything equal to an equal of C is equal to C'. Without that extra premise, the syllogism does not produce the desired conclusion terminologically. The author then subdivides cases where the extraneous premise is true, such as implication (luzum) and dependence (tawaqquf) — there the conclusion holds because it is truly the product of all premises. In contrast, when the extraneous premise is false, as in half/double relations, the conclusion may be false. This is illustrated with numerical examples: 'one is half of two' and 'two is half of four' leads to 'one is half of four' only via the premise 'everything half of half of something is half of it', which is false. The passage uses mathematical examples for clarity, but the logical principle applies to any domain, such as fiqh rulings involving equality of causes.

Key Terms
اللزوم الذاتي
قياس المساواة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is qiyas al-musawat not considered a proper syllogism (qiyas) in the technical sense?
A) Because its premises are always false.
B) Because it requires an extraneous premise to yield the conclusion.
C) Because it is not composed of categorical propositions.
D) Because its conclusion is always false.

Key Takeaway
Essential implication demands that the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises alone, without needing an additional unstated premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the argument: 'A is equal to B in intoxication' and 'B is forbidden'. State the conclusion that would be drawn in qiyas al-musawah. Identify the extraneous premise required. Is that premise always true?

8 min
The Truth of the Extraneous Premise in Logical Entailment
Author lists examples of extraneous premises and their truth conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish cases where an extraneous premise is true (as in luzum and tawaqquf) from cases where it is false (as in tanasuf and tada'uf), and explain the effect on the conclusion.

Core Idea
A conclusion depends on the truth of the extraneous premise; if it's false, the conclusion may be false.

The author goes beyond defining essential implication to examine situations where the needed extraneous premise might be true or false. For relationships like implication (luzum) and dependence (tawaqquf), the extra premise holds, so the conclusion follows. But for relations like half and double (tanasuf, tada'uf), the extra premise is false, so the conclusion may be false. This teaches us to check the truth of supporting premises in any chain of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of extraneous premise mentioned
Look for the terms: اللزوم, التوقف, التناصف, التضاعف, المباينة. Understand what each signifies.
2
Classify each as true or false
The author says that for luzum and tawaqquf, the extraneous premise is true, so the conclusion holds. For tanasuf and tada'uf, it is false, so the conclusion does not follow necessarily.
3
Analyze the example of half and double
From 'one is half of two' and 'two is half of four', to conclude 'one is half of four' requires the premise 'whatever is half of half of something is half of that something', which is false; thus the conclusion may be false.
4
Apply the insight
When encountering any chain argument, always check whether the linking principle is true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل شرطا أو الفرع مانعا ؟
:قلت هذا لا يضر ؛ إذ المراد باللزوم كون المقدمتين بحيث لو فرضتا صادقتين لزم صدق النتيجة، لا أن المقدمات والنتيجة صوادق في نفس الأمر ، وإلا خرج القياس السوفسطائي. هذا، والله أعلم.
(وَأَخْرَجُوا بِاللُّزُومِ الذَّاتِي)، والمراد به كون المطلوب لازما للقضايا، من غير واسطة في العروض (مَا يَكُونُ) لازمة (لِمُقَدِّمَةٍ أَجْنَبِيَّةِ)، بحيث يكون لولا المقدمة الأجنبية لم يلزم منها شيء، بل يكون المطلوب لازما بالحقيقة للمقدمة الأجنبية وتلك المقدمات معا.
أَمَّا غَيْرُ لاَزِمَةٍ) لأصل المقدمات في الصدق (كَمَا فِي قِيَاسِ الْمُسَاوَاةِ، وَهُوَ الْمُرَكَّبُ مِنْ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ مُتَعَلِّقُ مَحْمُولِ الْأُولَى مَوْضُوعُ الْأُخْرَى، نَحْوُ (أ) مُسَاوٍ لِبَ)، وَ(بَ) مُسَاوِ لِ(جَ) ، يَلْزَمُ مِنْهُ بِوَاسِطَةِ كُلُّ مُسَاوٍ لِمُسَاوِ لِ(جَ) أَنَّ (أ) مُسَاوِ لِـ (ج)) ، فهذا القياس الخالي عن تلك المقدمة لا يسمى قياسا اصطلاحا بالنسبة إلى هذه النتيجة ، (فَحَيْثُ تَصْدُقُ تِلْكَ الْمُقَدِّمَةُ - كَاللُّزُوم)، نحو (أ) لازم لـ(ب)، و(ب) لازم لـ(ج)، (وَالتَّوَقُفِ - ) ، نحو (أ) موقوف على (ب) ، و(ب) موقوف على (ج) (تَصْدُقُ تِلْكَ النَّتِيجَةُ) ؛ لأنها بالحقيقة نتيجة لمجموع تلك المقدمة والمقدمات، (وَفِيمَا لا يصدق تلك المقدمة (فَلَا) يصدق النتيجة لزوما، بل قد تكذب، ( كَالتَنَاصُفِ وَالتَّضَاعُف)، نحو الواحد نصف الاثنين، والاثنان نصف الأربع، وبالعكس، وقد يصدق كما في المباينة .
The original is either a condition or the branch is an obstacle? I said: This does not harm because what is meant by implication is that the two premises are such that if they were assumed true, the truth of the conclusion would follow, not that the premises and conclusion are true in reality; otherwise the sophistical syllogism would be excluded. This, and Allah knows best. And they excluded by essential implication—and what is meant by it is that the required conclusion is necessary for the propositions without an intermediary in attribution—that which is necessary due to an extraneous premise, such that without the extraneous premise nothing would follow from it, but rather the required conclusion is in reality necessary for the extraneous premise and those premises together. As for that which is not necessary for the original premises in truth, such as in the syllogism of equality, which is composed of two propositions where the predicate of the first is the subject of the second, like (A is equal to B) and (B is equal to C); it follows from it by means of (everything equal to an equal of C) that (A is equal to C). So this syllogism empty of that premise is not called a syllogism terminologically with respect to this conclusion. So wherever that premise is true—like implication, e.g., (A is necessary for B) and (B is necessary for C), and like dependence, e.g., (A depends on B) and (B depends on C)—that conclusion is true, because it is in reality the conclusion of the sum of that premise and the premises. And where that premise is not true, the conclusion does not necessarily follow; rather it may be false, like half and double, e.g., one is half of two and two is half of four, and conversely, and it may be true as in disparity.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of essential implication (al-luzum al-dhati) and its role in distinguishing proper syllogisms from incomplete ones. The author first clarifies that the required implication is a logical entailment: if the premises were true, the conclusion must be true, not that they actually are true. This prevents sophistical syllogisms from being counted. Then, he explains that through essential implication, logicians exclude cases where a conclusion depends on an extraneous premise. The example given is the syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawat): from 'A = B' and 'B = C', we cannot directly conclude 'A = C' without the additional premise 'everything equal to an equal of C is equal to C'. Without that extra premise, the syllogism does not produce the desired conclusion terminologically. The author then subdivides cases where the extraneous premise is true, such as implication (luzum) and dependence (tawaqquf) — there the conclusion holds because it is truly the product of all premises. In contrast, when the extraneous premise is false, as in half/double relations, the conclusion may be false. This is illustrated with numerical examples: 'one is half of two' and 'two is half of four' leads to 'one is half of four' only via the premise 'everything half of half of something is half of it', which is false. The passage uses mathematical examples for clarity, but the logical principle applies to any domain, such as fiqh rulings involving equality of causes.

التوقف
التناصف
التضاعف

Key Takeaway
When an argument requires an extraneous premise, the truth of that premise is crucial; if it is false, the conclusion does not necessarily follow.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Why the Equality Syllogism Does Not Break the Threefold Division
Author refutes an objection to the completeness of the threefold division of proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the completeness of the threefold division of proof (hujja) by explaining why the equality syllogism (qiyas al-musawat) is not essentially conclusive and reduces to ordinary syllogisms.

Core Idea
The equality syllogism is not essentially conclusive and with an added premise reduces to two ordinary syllogisms.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential criticism: that the threefold division of proof into syllogism, induction, and analogy is incomplete because the equality syllogism is excluded. The author responds by clarifying the nature of essential conclusiveness and showing that the equality syllogism, when properly analyzed, is not a separate type but a combination of two ordinary syllogisms. Understanding this defense reinforces the foundational classification of proofs in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the claim that excluding the equality syllogism from qiyas disrupts the threefold division. Note why it is not covered by induction or analogy.
2
Extract the author's first response
The author quotes the original text: the division is not disrupted because proof is defined as essentially conclusive, and the equality syllogism is not essentially conclusive.
Essential conclusiveness (muwassil bi al-dhat) means the argument yields certainty without additional premises.
3
Analyze the reduction argument
Show that the equality syllogism with an extra premise becomes two syllogisms: first from A=B and B=C to A=equal of C, then from that and an added premise to A=C. This shows it is not a separate type.
The extra premise is 'whatever equals an equal of something, equals it'.
4
Address the middle term objection
The objector says the middle term is not repeated. The author responds that the entire term (the equal of C) is repeated, and that suffices for a syllogism.
Repetition of the term in its entirety is sufficient to indicate necessity.
5
Conclude the defense
Summarize that the equality syllogism does not break the threefold division because it is not essentially conclusive and reduces to ordinary qiyas.

وَأَمَّا هُوَ مَعَ تِلْكَ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ فَرَاجِعٌ إِلَى قِيَاسَيْنِ) وداخل فيه؛ فإن (أ) مساو لـ(ب) و(ب) مساو لـ(ج) قياس منتج لقولنا (أ) مساو لمساو لـ(ج)، فإذا ضم إليه تلك المقدمة صار قياسا منتجا لقولنا ((أ) مساو لـ(ج) . Section on the Proof. For if it is said: The enumeration of proof into three has been disrupted by excluding it (the equality syllogism) from qiyas, because it does not enter into induction or analogy, he answers by saying: 'The enumeration is not disrupted by its exclusion, because it (the proof) is [defined as] that which is essentially conclusive, and this (equality syllogism) is not essentially conclusive.' And as for it together with that premise, it reduces to two syllogisms and is included in it; for (A equals B) and (B equals C) is a syllogism producing our saying (A equals an equal of C), and when that premise is added, it becomes a syllogism producing our saying (A equals C). Just as it is a syllogism with respect to (A equals an equal of C). For if it is said: The middle term is not repeated in this syllogism, so how is it a syllogism? He answers: 'The repetition of the term in its entirety indicates its necessity is evidence.' As for a necessary (premise) that is contradictory in boundaries, it is coordinated to his saying 'as for non-necessary', such as you say: 'The part of substance, its removal necessitates the removal of substance' and 'Everything that is not substance, its removal does not necessitate the removal of substance.' It follows from it, by means of the contrapositive of the second premise—which is our saying 'Everything whose removal necessitates the removal of substance is substance'—that (the part of substance is substance). For the first premise, when combined with the contrapositive of the second, forms a syllogism in the form of the first figure producing this conclusion. So this conclusion truly belongs to this syllogism, not to the first. So stand firm on it and do not deviate. And I do not know a strong reason for excluding this division; for the contrapositive is like the simple conversion, and what follows from it by means of simple conversion—like the three figures other than the first—they included in qiyas, except that the contradictoriness of boundaries distances it greatly from the natural disposition, unlike simple conversion. And there is something in it; for he distinguishes between what follows by means of contrapositive and what follows by means of simple conversion; for in...
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This section addresses an objection to the completeness of the threefold division of proof (hujja) into syllogism, induction, and analogy. The objector claims that excluding the equality syllogism (qiyas al-musawat) from qiyas voids the division because it is not included in induction or analogy either. The author responds by quoting the original text: the division remains complete because the equality syllogism is not essentially (bi-al-dhat) conclusive—it requires an extra premise. Then he explains that with that extra premise, the equality syllogism actually becomes two separate syllogisms: first, from 'A = B' and 'B = C' we get 'A = an equal of C', and then adding the extra premise yields 'A = C'. He also addresses a potential objection about the middle term not being repeated, citing the principle that repetition of the term in its entirety is sufficient. Next, the passage discusses a type of necessary premise called 'contradictory in boundaries' (mutanaqida fi al-hudud). The example involves substance: 'The part of substance, its removal necessitates the removal of substance' and 'Everything that is not substance, its removal does not necessitate the removal of substance.' Through contrapositive (aks al-naqid) of the second premise, we get 'Everything whose removal necessitates the removal of substance is substance', which combines with the first premise in a first-figure syllogism to prove that 'the part of substance is substance'. The author then expresses doubt about the justification for excluding this division, arguing that contrapositive is similar to simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi) and logicians include syllogisms that depend on simple conversion, but here they exclude this type due to the unnatural contradictoriness of the boundaries. He hints at a suspect distinction between contrapositive and simple conversion. The passage ends mid-sentence.

Contradictory Boundaries and the Use of Contrapositive
Author discusses a case of contradictory boundaries and uses contrapositive to derive a conclusion, then questions its exclusion from qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive a conclusion using contrapositive (aks al-naqid) from contradictory boundaries (mutanaqida al-hudud) and evaluate the author's argument about its inclusion in qiyas.

Core Idea
Contrapositive is as valid as simple conversion, but its unnaturalness may lead logicians to exclude it.

The passage examines a special case in logic: when premises involve contradictory boundaries, a conclusion can be drawn using contrapositive. The author demonstrates with an example from substance ontology and then questions why logicians exclude such cases. This discussion highlights the criteria for a valid syllogism and the role of conversion operations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the case
Identify the two premises: (P1) 'The part of substance, its removal necessitates the removal of substance' and (P2) 'Everything that is not substance, its removal does not necessitate the removal of substance'.
These premises are about substance (jawhar) and are contradictory in boundaries.
2
Apply contrapositive
Derive the contrapositive of the second premise (P2): from 'If not-substance then not-necessitate-removal' we get 'If necessitate-removal then substance'. This is a valid immediate inference.
Contrapositive (aks al-naqid) transforms 'Every A is B' into 'Every not-B is not-A'.
3
Combine premises in a syllogism
Combine P1 with the contrapositive of P2 in a first-figure categorical syllogism: Major premise: 'Everything whose removal necessitates removal of substance is substance'; Minor premise: 'The part of substance, its removal necessitates removal of substance'; Conclusion: 'The part of substance is substance'.
This conclusion is validly drawn from the new syllogism, not from the original premises alone.
4
Evaluate the author's critique
The author questions why logicians exclude this type of argument from qiyas, noting that contrapositive is like simple conversion, which is accepted. He suggests the only reason is the unnaturalness of contradictory boundaries.
Simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi) is used in the second and third figures of the categorical syllogism.

Commentary
This section addresses an objection to the completeness of the threefold division of proof (hujja) into syllogism, induction, and analogy. The objector claims that excluding the equality syllogism (qiyas al-musawat) from qiyas voids the division because it is not included in induction or analogy either. The author responds by quoting the original text: the division remains complete because the equality syllogism is not essentially (bi-al-dhat) conclusive—it requires an extra premise. Then he explains that with that extra premise, the equality syllogism actually becomes two separate syllogisms: first, from 'A = B' and 'B = C' we get 'A = an equal of C', and then adding the extra premise yields 'A = C'. He also addresses a potential objection about the middle term not being repeated, citing the principle that repetition of the term in its entirety is sufficient. Next, the passage discusses a type of necessary premise called 'contradictory in boundaries' (mutanaqida fi al-hudud). The example involves substance: 'The part of substance, its removal necessitates the removal of substance' and 'Everything that is not substance, its removal does not necessitate the removal of substance.' Through contrapositive (aks al-naqid) of the second premise, we get 'Everything whose removal necessitates the removal of substance is substance', which combines with the first premise in a first-figure syllogism to prove that 'the part of substance is substance'. The author then expresses doubt about the justification for excluding this division, arguing that contrapositive is similar to simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi) and logicians include syllogisms that depend on simple conversion, but here they exclude this type due to the unnatural contradictoriness of the boundaries. He hints at a suspect distinction between contrapositive and simple conversion. The passage ends mid-sentence.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
العكس المستوي
متناقضة الحدود
الجوهر
الشكل الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the exclusion of the case with contradictory boundaries from qiyas?
A) He endorses the exclusion wholeheartedly.
B) He argues it should be excluded because it is invalid.
C) He questions the exclusion and finds no strong reason for it.
D) He says it is a separate type of proof.

Key Takeaway
Contrapositive is a valid logical operation akin to simple conversion, and its exclusion from qiyas due to unnaturalness is questionable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the method from the lesson, apply contrapositive to the second premise 'What is not substance does not necessitate removal of substance' to derive 'What necessitates removal of substance is substance'. Then combine with the first premise 'The part of substance necessitates removal of substance' in a first-figure syllogism to prove that the part of substance is substance. Write out the syllogism in English or Arabic.
?
Consider a similar pair from fiqh: 'Every contract that involves uncertainty is invalid. Every contract that is not invalid does not involve uncertainty.' Derive the contrapositive of the second premise and combine with the first to prove something about a specific contract that involves uncertainty.

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
The Three Arguments Are Exhaustive: Reduction to Syllogism
Author defends exhaustiveness of tripartite classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze an argument and determine whether it reduces to a categorical syllogism, preserving the tripartite classification.

Core Idea
Every valid argument reduces to syllogism, induction, or analogy; exceptions collapse into syllogism when properly analyzed.

The passage defends the standard classification of arguments. An objector claims that a certain argument is neither syllogism, induction, nor analogy, breaking the classification. The author shows that this argument, when properly understood, is actually a syllogism in disguise. In this lesson, we will learn how to identify such reductions, using the example of equality and the key role of an additional premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three argument types
Recall the standard tripartite classification: syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), and analogy (tamthil). In the passage, these are the three types of 'hujja' (argument).
Syllogism is a deductive argument with two premises; induction generalizes from particulars; analogy argues from likeness.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says the classification is disturbed because a certain argument does not fall under induction or analogy, and it is excluded from syllogism. Identify what the objector thinks is missing.
The argument in question is not a direct means to the conclusion by itself—it does not 'convey by essence' (لا يوصل بالذات).
3
Follow the author's resolution
The author argues that when this argument is combined with an additional premise, it resolves into two syllogisms. The example uses equality: A = B, B = C yields 'A = equal of C'; then with the premise 'equal of C = C', we get 'A = C'. This shows it ultimately fits under syllogism.
The key is that the argument alone is incomplete; adding a premise makes the syllogistic structure explicit.
4
Apply the logic to an Islamic example
Consider a legal argument: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Wine is intoxicating. Therefore wine is forbidden.' This is a clear syllogism. But what if someone argues: 'Wine is intoxicating, and intoxicants are generally forbidden, so wine is probably forbidden'? This is not a syllogism but an inductive generalization. The classification helps in assessing certainty.
The tripartite classification remains intact because any valid reasoning can be reconstructed as one of the three types.

فصل في الحجة
فإن قيل: قد اختل حصر الحجة في الثلاثة بإخراجه عن القياس؛ لعدم دخوله في الاستقراء والتمثيل أجاب بقوله: (وَلَا يَخْتَلُّ الْحَصْرُ بِإِخْرَاجِهِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لِلْمُوَصِّلِ بِالذَّاتِ)، وهذا غير موصل بالذات.
وَأَمَّا هُوَ مَعَ تِلْكَ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ فَرَاجِعٌ إِلَى قِيَاسَيْنِ) وداخل فيه؛ فإن (أ) مساو لـ(ب) و(ب) مساو لـ(ج) قياس منتج لقولنا (أ) مساو لمساو لـ(ج)، فإذا ضم إليه تلك المقدمة صار قياسا منتجا لقولنا ((أ) مساو لـ(ج) . (كَمَا أَنَّهُ قِيَاسٌ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى أَنَّ (أ) مُسَاوِ لِمُسَاوِ لِ(ج)) ، فإن قيل: الأوسط غير متكرر هذا القياس، فكيف يكون قياسا ؟ أجاب بقوله: (وَتَكْرَارُ الْحَدَّ بِتَمَامِهِ مَا دَلْ عَلَى وُجُوبِهِ دَلِيلٌ .
في
وَأَمَّا لَازِمَةٌ مُتَنَاقِضَةٌ فِي الْحُدُودِ)، عطف على قوله «أما غير لازمة»، كَمَا تَقُولُ جُزْءُ الْجَوْهَر يُوجِبُ ارْتِفَاعُهُ ارْتِفَاعَ الْجَوْهَرِ، وَكُلُّ مَا لَيْسَ بِجَوْهَرِ لَا يُوجِبُ ارْتِفَاعُهُ ارْتِفَاعَ الْجَوْهَرِ ، يَلْزَمُ مِنْهُ بِوَاسِطَةِ عَكْسِ نَقِيضِ الْمُقَدَّمَةِ الثَّانِيَةِ) ، وهو قولنا كل ما يوجب ارتفاعه ارتفاع الجوهر جوهر» (أَنَّ جُزْء الْجَوْهَرِ جَوْهَرُ ؛ فإن المقدمة الأولى إذا ضمت إلى عكس نقيض الثانية تركب قياس على هيئة الشكل الأول ينتج هذه النتيجة، فهذه النتيجة لهذا القياس حقيقة لا من الأول، فاستقم عليه ولا تزل.
(وَلَا أَدْرِي وَجْهَا قَوِيًّا لِإِخْرَاجِ هَذَا الْقِسْمِ؛ فَإِنَّهُ)، أي عكس النقيض كَالْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي)، وما يلزم منه بواسطة العكس المستوي كالأشكال الثلاثة غير الأول أدخلوه في القياس ، سِوَى أَنَّ مُنَاقَضَةَ الْحُدُودِ أَبْعَدَهُ عَنِ الطبَعِ جِدًا)، بخلاف العكس المستوي ، (وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ)؛ فإنه فرق بين ما يلزم منه بواسطة عكس النقيض، وبين ما يلزم منه بواسطة العكس المستوي؛ إذ في الأول ليست النتيجة لأصل المقدمات، بل لما يحصل من انضمام الصغرى مع عکس نقيض الكبرى، بخلاف ما يلزم منه بواسطة العكس المستوي ؛ فإن النتيجة بالحقيقة لأصل القياس والعكس المستوي واسطة في الإثبات فقط .
،
ونحن سنبين إن شاء الله تعالى إنتاج الأشكال بوجه لمي بحيث لا يمتري واحد من العامة ، فضلا عن الخاصة، في أن النتيجة لازمة للقياس لزوما ذاتيا ، فاستقم عليه ، والعلم التام عند علام الغيوب.
Chapter on the argument. If it is said: The classification of argument into three has been disturbed by excluding it from syllogism because it does not fall under induction and analogy. He answers by his statement: 'And the classification is not disturbed by its exclusion, because it is for that which conveys by essence,' and this does not convey by essence. And as for it together with that premise, it reverts to two syllogisms and is included in it. Indeed, (A) is equal to (B) and (B) is equal to (C) is a syllogism producing our statement: (A) is equal to the equal of (C). Then when that premise is joined to it, it becomes a syllogism producing our statement: (A) is equal to (C). (Just as it is a syllogism with respect to that (A) is equal to the equal of (C)). If it is said: The middle term is not repeated in this syllogism, how can it be a syllogism? He answers: The repetition of the term in its entirety indicates its necessity. And as for a necessary entailing contradictory in definitions, it is an apposition to his statement 'as for non-necessary'. For example, you say: A part of substance, its removal necessitates the removal of the substance; and everything that is not a substance, its removal does not necessitate the removal of the substance. From it follows by means of the contrapositive of the second premise, which is our statement: Everything whose removal necessitates the removal of the substance is a substance, (that the part of substance is a substance). For the first premise when joined to the contrapositive of the second forms a syllogism in the form of the first figure producing this conclusion. So this conclusion belongs truly to this syllogism, not from the first. So be upright upon it and do not deviate. And I do not know a strong reason for excluding this division; for it (i.e., the contrapositive) is like simple conversion, and what follows from it by means of simple conversion, like the three figures other than the first, they included in syllogism, except that the contradictoriness of definitions made it very far from nature, unlike simple conversion. And there is much to be said about it. For there is a difference between what follows from it by means of the contrapositive and what follows by means of simple conversion, because in the first the conclusion does not belong to the original premises, but rather to what results from joining the minor to the contrapositive of the major, unlike what follows by means of simple conversion, for the conclusion truly belongs to the original syllogism and simple conversion is only a means in establishing. And we will soon explain, God willing, the production of the figures in a clear manner such that no one among the common people, let alone the elite, will doubt that the conclusion is necessarily entailed by the syllogism with a self-evident entailment. So be upright upon it. And complete knowledge is with the Knower of the unseen.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage defends the exhaustiveness of the standard classification of arguments into syllogism, induction, and analogy. An objector claims that a certain argument is excluded from syllogism and does not fit induction or analogy, making the classification incomplete. The author responds that the argument in question is not a direct means to the conclusion by itself—it does not convey by essence (لا يوصل بالذات). However, when combined with another premise, it resolves into two syllogisms, thus falling under syllogism. To illustrate, he uses the example of equalities: A equals B and B equals C yields the intermediate conclusion 'A equals the equal of C'; then with the premise that 'the equal of C is C', we get 'A equals C'. This shows it is ultimately a syllogism. A further objection is raised: the middle term (a common term) is not repeated in this syllogism. The author replies that the repetition of the term in its entirety suffices to indicate the necessity of the conclusion. Next, the author discusses a necessary entailment that is contradictory in definitions (lazima mutanaqida fi al-hudud). He gives an example: a part of substance whose removal necessitates the removal of the substance, and whatever is not a substance does not necessitate the removal of the substance upon its removal. By taking the contrapositive of the second premise (i.e., 'everything whose removal necessitates the removal of the substance is a substance'), we combine it with the first premise to form a valid first-figure syllogism concluding that the part of substance is a substance. The author notes that he sees no strong reason to exclude this type of argument from syllogism, since it uses the contrapositive just as other valid arguments use simple conversion. However, he acknowledges that the contradictoriness of definitions makes it less natural. He distinguishes between arguments using contrapositive and those using simple conversion: in the former, the conclusion is not directly from the original premises but from the minor premise joined with the contrapositive of the major; in the latter, the conclusion is from the original syllogism and the conversion is only a means of demonstrating it. The passage ends with a promise to explain the figures of the syllogism in a clear manner that leaves no doubt that the conclusion follows necessarily.

Key Takeaway
The tripartite classification of arguments is exhaustive because any valid reasoning can be shown to reduce to syllogism, induction, or analogy.

The Syllogism
advanced
7 min
The Contrapositive as a Valid Inferential Step: Reducing to First-Figure Syllogism
Author defends inclusion of arguments using contrapositive in syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use the contrapositive (aks al-naqid) to restructure a premise and verify that the resulting argument is a valid first-figure syllogism.

Core Idea
Contrapositive transforms a premise to complete a syllogism; though less natural, it is as valid as simple conversion.

The passage discusses a necessary entailment that is contradictory in definitions. The author gives an example: part of substance, etc., and shows how using the contrapositive of the second premise yields a valid first-figure syllogism. He defends its inclusion in syllogism and contrasts it with simple conversion. In this lesson, we will learn the mechanics of contrapositive and its role in syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the original argument
The author presents two premises: (1) A part of substance, its removal necessitates the removal of the substance. (2) Everything that is not a substance, its removal does not necessitate the removal of the substance. The goal is to prove that the part of substance is itself a substance.
These premises do not directly form a syllogism because the middle term is not clear.
2
Apply the contrapositive to the second premise
The contrapositive of premise (2) is: 'Everything whose removal necessitates the removal of the substance is a substance.' This is derived by taking the original statement 'If not substance, then not necessitate' and converting it to 'If necessitate, then substance.'
Formally: 'Every non-substance does not necessitate removal' becomes 'Every necessitator of removal is a substance' via contrapositive.
3
Form the new syllogism
Now combine the first premise (minor) with the contra positive of the second (major): Minor: Part of substance is such that its removal necessitates removal of substance. Major: Everything whose removal necessitates removal of substance is a substance. Conclusion: Part of substance is a substance. This is a first-figure syllogism (AAA).
The middle term is 'necessitates removal of substance'. The syllogism is valid.
4
Compare with simple conversion
The author notes that the contrapositive is like simple conversion (e.g., 'Every A is B' converts to 'Some B is A'). Both are ways to adjust premises, but contrapositive involves a change in quality and quantification (negation), making it less natural. However, its validity is equally accepted.
In simple conversion, the conclusion is directly from the original syllogism; in contrapositive, the conclusion arises from the new combination, not the original premises alone.

Source Text

Next, the author discusses a necessary entailment that is contradictory in definitions (lazima mutanaqida fi al-hudud). He gives an example: a part of substance whose removal necessitates the removal of the substance, and whatever is not a substance does not necessitate the removal of the substance upon its removal. By taking the contrapositive of the second premise (i.e., 'everything whose removal necessitates the removal of the substance is a substance'), we combine it with the first premise to form a valid first-figure syllogism concluding that the part of substance is a substance. The author notes that he sees no strong reason to exclude this type of argument from syllogism, since it uses the contrapositive just as other valid arguments use simple conversion. However, he acknowledges that the contradictoriness of definitions makes it less natural. He distinguishes between arguments using contrapositive and those using simple conversion: in the former, the conclusion is not directly from the original premises but from the minor premise joined with the contrapositive of the major; in the latter, the conclusion is from the original syllogism and the conversion is only a means of demonstrating it. The passage ends with a promise to explain the figures of the syllogism in a clear manner that leaves no doubt that the conclusion follows necessarily.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
لازمة متناقضة
الشكل الأول
جزيء الجوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Contrapositive transforms a premise to fit a syllogism; though less intuitive, its validity is equivalent to simple conversion.

Division of Qiyas into Iqtirani and Istithna'i
Author divides the syllogism into conjunctive and exceptive, and further into categorical and conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i) syllogisms, and identify categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti) subtypes.

Core Idea
The primary division of qiyas is whether the conclusion is stated in the premises (istithna'i) or not (iqtirani).

In this passage, the author establishes a fundamental classification of syllogisms. The main division is based on whether the conclusion (or its contradictory) appears as a premise. If it does, the syllogism is exceptive (istithna'i); if not, it is conjunctive (iqtirani). The conjunctive is further divided into categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti) based on the type of propositions used. This classification is the gateway to understanding the structure of arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division
Look at the definition: istithna'i is defined by the conclusion being mentioned. Note that iqtirani is the default where conclusion is not mentioned.
2
Understand the condition for istithna'i
The passage says: 'if the conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned in it'. This is the defining feature.
The contradictory is the negation of the conclusion.
3
Recognize iqtirani as the opposite
If the conclusion is not mentioned, it is iqtirani. This includes most standard syllogisms.
4
Subdivide iqtirani
The conjunctive is divided into hamli (categorical) when both premises are subject-predicate, and sharti (conditional) when one or both premises are conditional.
Conditional includes hypothetical and disjunctive.
5
Apply the classification
Practice by taking a given syllogism and determining whether it is istithna'i or iqtirani, and if iqtirani, whether it is hamli or sharti.
Check if the conclusion appears in the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهُوَ، أي القياس، اسْتِثْنَائِيُّ، إِنْ كَانَ النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكورا فيه القياس إلى تقسيم اقتراني واستثنائي الحملي والشرطي
And it—i.e., the syllogism—is exceptive if the conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned in it. The syllogism divides into conjunctive and exceptive, [and the conjunctive into] categorical and conditional.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage is a brief definitional classification of the syllogism (qiyas). The author states that a syllogism is called 'exceptive' (istithna'i) if its conclusion (or the contradictory of the conclusion) is explicitly stated as one of its premises. Otherwise, it is 'conjunctive' (iqtirani). Then he further divides the conjunctive syllogism into 'categorical' (hamli) and 'conditional' (sharti). This is the standard division in Islamic logic: the primary division is by whether the conclusion is stated (istithna'i) or not (iqtirani). The iqtirani is then divided by the type of propositions it contains: if both premises are categorical (subject-predicate), it is hamli; if one or both are conditional, it is sharti. The exceptive syllogism is also divided later, but the author only mentions the iqtirani sub-division here. This classification is foundational for understanding the different forms of arguments in al-mantiq.

Key Terms

القياس الاستثنائي
الحملي
الشرطي

Key Takeaway
A syllogism is either exceptive (conclusion stated) or conjunctive (conclusion not stated); conjunctive is further divided into categorical and conditional.

Division of Qiyas and Its Terminology
Defines the two main types of qiyas and the technical terms for its parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two main types of qiyas (istithna'i and iqtirani) and identify the logical components (minor term, major term, middle term, premises, figure) from a description.

Core Idea
A syllogism is either exceptive (conclusion present) or concatenative; its skeleton requires a middle term.

This passage lays the groundwork for all syllogistic reasoning by defining its fundamental types and parts. Without this vocabulary, you cannot even begin to evaluate an argument's validity. Pay close attention to the distinction between 'form' and 'matter' and how the middle term determines the figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions
Look for the terms istithna'i (exceptive) and iqtirani (concatenative). The division depends on whether the conclusion or its contradictory appears 'in its form' (bi-hay'atihi) within a premise.
The author justifies the condition to avoid circularity (musadara) or falsehood.
2
Understand the condition for istithna'i
The conclusion or its contradictory cannot itself be a premise; it must be only part of a premise. Otherwise, the syllogism would be invalid.
This ensures the conclusion is not assumed in the premises.
3
Locate the component terms
The subject of the sought conclusion is the minor term (asghar), its predicate the major term (akbar). A repeated term between the premises is the middle term (awsat). Label the premises: sughra (minor premise) contains the minor term, kubra (major premise) contains the major term.
The middle term is essential; without it, no syllogism is formed.
4
Trace the figure
The figure (shakl) is determined by the position of the middle term in the two premises. The first figure occurs when the middle term is predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major premise.
The passage only describes the first figure; the other three follow logically from different positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهُوَ أي القياس استثنائي إن كان النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكورا فيه بهيئته، وإنما قال بهيئته لأن عين النقيضة أو نقيضها لا يمكن أن يكون مقدمة، وإلا لصارت مصادرة، أو اشتمل القياس على كاذب في زعم المستدل، بل إنما يكون جزء مقدمة، والقضية لا تكون جزء قضية ما دامت قضية. وإلا يكن مذكورا بهيئته، بل بمادته فقط فاقتراني، فإن تركيب من الحملية الصرفة فحملي، وإلا فشرطي. ولما كان الحملي أكثر استعمالا من الشرطي، وأعظم بحثا من الثلاثة ووجه الاستثنائي قدمه، وقال: وموضوع المطلوب يسمى أصغر، وما هو فيه الصغرى، ومحموله أكبر، وما هو فيه الكبرى، ولا يتم بهما، بل لا بد من أمر متكرر، والمتكرر الأوسط، والقضية التي جعلت جزء قياس بل حجة مطلقا مقدمة، وطرفاها حدا، واقتران الصغرى بالكبرى بل الحاصل بعد الاقتران يسمى قرينة وضربا وهيئة، ونسبة الأوسط إلى طرفي المطلوب أي الأصغر والأكبر يسمى شكلا، فالأوسط إما محمول في الصغرى وموضوع في الكبرى فهو الشكل الأول.
And it—that is, the syllogism—is exceptive if the conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned in it in its form. He only said 'in its form' because the very contradictory or its contrary cannot be a premise, otherwise it would become a petitio principii, or the syllogism would contain a falsehood in the opinion of the arguer; rather it is only part of a premise, and a proposition does not become part of a proposition as long as it is a proposition. And if it is not mentioned in its form, but only in its matter, then it is concatenative. And if it is composed of purely categorical propositions, then it is categorical; otherwise it is conditional. And since the categorical is more frequently used than the conditional, and greater in research than the three figures of the exceptive, he gave precedence to it. And he said: The subject of the sought conclusion is called the minor term; the premise in which it is the subject is the minor premise; its predicate is the major term; the premise in which it is the predicate is the major premise. They are not sufficient without a repeated term. The repeated term is the middle term. The proposition that is made a part of a syllogism, or more generally a proof, is called a premise; its two extremes are called terms. The conjunction of the minor with the major, rather the result after conjunction, is called a qarina, a mood, and a figure. The relation of the middle term to the two extremes of the sought conclusion, i.e., the minor and major, is called the figure. The middle term, if it is predicate in the minor and subject in the major, then it is the first figure.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage is a definitional taxonomy of syllogism (qiyas) and its terminological apparatus. It opens by stating the primary division: a syllogism is either istithna'i (exceptive) if the conclusion or its contradictory appears as a premise in its form (hay'a), or iqtirani (concatenative) if only the matter (madda) is present without the form of the conclusion. The author justifies the condition 'in its form' to avoid circularity (musadara) or false premises. Within iqtirani, if composed entirely of categorical (hamli) propositions, it is called hamli; otherwise sharti (conditional). The author notes that hamli is more common and thus prioritizes it. Next, the components of a categorical syllogism are defined: the subject of the desired conclusion is asghar (minor term); the premise containing it is sughra (minor premise); the predicate is akbar (major term); the premise containing it is kubra (major premise). These two premises alone are insufficient; a recurring term (al-mutakarrir) called awsat (middle term) is necessary. The definitions then extend: the proposition serving as part of a proof is muqaddima (premise); its extremes are hadd (terms); the combination of minor and major after conjunction is called qarina, darb, or hay'a (mood/figure); and the relation of the middle to the extremes is called shakl (figure). The passage concludes by describing the first figure: middle term predicate in minor and subject in major. This passage is foundational for understanding syllogistic structure.

Key Terms
القياس الاستثنائي
القياس الاقتراني
الأوسط
الشكل

The Syllogism
beginner
