The Key Terms of a Categorical Syllogism
Author defines the technical terms for the parts and structure of a syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to label the parts of a categorical syllogism (asghar, akbar, awsat, sughra, kubra, muqaddima, hadd, qarina, darb, shakl) and identify the figure based on the middle term's position.

Core Idea
The structure of a syllogism is defined by the roles of its three terms: minor, major, and middle.

The categorical syllogism is the most common form of argument in Islamic logic. Before you can evaluate whether a syllogism is valid, you must be able to name its parts. This lesson teaches you the precise terminology used by logicians to describe the components of a syllogism, from the terms (hudud) to the premises (muqaddimat) and the overall figure (shakl).

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion (matlub)
Every syllogism aims to prove a conclusion. Find the proposition that is being argued for. Its subject and predicate are the minor and major terms.
2
Label the subject of the conclusion as the 'asghar' (minor term)
The subject of the conclusion is called the minor term. The premise that contains the minor term is the minor premise (sughra).
3
Label the predicate of the conclusion as the 'akbar' (major term)
The predicate of the conclusion is the major term. The premise that contains it is the major premise (kubra).
4
Find the term repeated in both premises: the 'awsat' (middle term)
The middle term does not appear in the conclusion. It links the minor and major terms. It is the key to the syllogism's structure.
In the example 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; this drink is intoxicant; therefore it is forbidden', 'intoxicant' is the middle term.
5
Determine the figure (shakl) from the middle term's position
Look at the minor premise: the middle term can be subject or predicate. Then look at the major premise. If the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major, that is the first figure. This lesson focuses on defining the figure; details of other figures come later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان الحملي أكثر استعمالا من الشرطي، وأعظم بحثا من الثلاثة ووجه الاستثنائي قدمه ، وقال : ( وَمَوْضُوعُ الْمَطْلُوبِ يُسَمَّى أَصْغَرَ ، وَمَا)، أي المقدمة هُوَ فِيهِ الصُّغْرَى ، وَمَحْمُولُهُ أَكْبَرَ ، وَمَا هُوَ فِيهِ الْكُبْرَى)، ولا يتم بهما، بل لا بد من أمر متكرر، (وَالْمُتَكَرِّرُ الْأَوْسَطُ، وَالْقَضِيَّةُ الَّتِي جُعِلَتْ جُزْء قياس)، بل حجة مطلقا ( مُقَدِّمَةً ، وَطَرَفَاهَا حَدًا، وَاقْتِرَانُ الصُّغْرَى وَالضروب يسمى بالكبرى) ، بل الحاصل بعد الاقتران يسمى (قَرِينَةً وَضَرْبًا وَهَيْئَةً وَنِسْبَةُ الْأَوْسَطِ إِلَى طَرَفَيْ الْمَطْلُوبِ)، أي الأصغر والأكبر يسمى (شَكْلًا، فَالْأَوْسَطُ إِمَّا مَحْمُولٌ فِي الصُّغْرَى وَمَوْضُوعٌ فِي الْكُبْرَى ، فَهُوَ) الشكل الأول.
And since the categorical syllogism is more commonly used than the conditional, and is a greater subject of investigation than the other three [types], and the face of the exceptive syllogism, he [the author?] gave precedence. He said: 'The subject of the sought conclusion (matlub) is called the minor term (asghar), and the premise in which it occurs is the minor premise (sughra). Its predicate is called the major term (akbar), and the premise in which it occurs is the major premise (kubra).' And it is not completed by these two alone; rather there must be a repeated thing. (The repeated thing is the middle term (awsat). And the proposition that is made a part of a syllogism—rather, an argument in general—is called a premise (muqaddima), and its two extremes are called terms (hadd). The conjunction of the minor [premise] with the major [premise] is called a conjunction (qarina) and a mood (darb) and a form (hay'a). And the relation of the middle term to the two extremes of the sought conclusion—meaning the minor and major [terms]—is called a figure (shakl). So the middle term is either a predicate in the minor [premise] and a subject in the major [premise]: that is the first figure.'
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage defines the technical terms of a categorical syllogism. The subject of the conclusion is called the minor term (asghar), and the premise containing it is the minor premise (sughra). The predicate of the conclusion is the major term (akbar), appearing in the major premise (kubra). A third term, repeated in both premises, is essential and is called the middle term (awsat). Each premise is called a muqaddima, and its subject and predicate are the limits (haddān). The combination of the two premises is called qarina (conjunction), and its specific structure is the darb (mood) and hay'a (form). The arrangement of the middle term relative to the minor and major terms defines the shakl (figure). For example, if the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major, that is the first figure. In fiqh, this is like arguing: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; this drink is intoxicant; therefore it is forbidden'—here 'intoxicant' is the middle term.

Key Terms
الأصغر
الأكبر
الأوسط
المقدمة
الشكل

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Four Figures of Syllogism and Their Natural Order
Classification of the four syllogistic figures with evaluation of naturalness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the figure of a categorical syllogism by locating the middle term and explain why the first figure is most natural and the fourth is rejected.

Core Idea
The first figure is the gold standard; other figures are ranked by naturalness and reducible to it.

The position of the middle term determines the figure of a categorical syllogism. The classical Islamic logicians ranked the four figures by their naturalness, with the first being self-evident and the fourth so unnatural that Avicenna and al-Farabi discarded it. This lesson walks you through their classification and the conditions for valid syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Locate the middle term in the premises
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. Its position (subject or predicate) in each premise defines the figure.
In a categorical syllogism, there are exactly three terms: subject, predicate, and middle.
2
Apply the figure definitions
First figure: middle is predicate in minor, subject in major. Second: predicate in both. Third: subject in both. Fourth: subject in minor, predicate in major. This is called the 'converse of the first'.
Memorize these patterns; they are the key to identifying any figure.
3
Assess naturalness
The first figure is natural and self-evident; the second is close in apparent production; the third is less; the fourth is very unnatural, so much so that Avicenna and al-Farabi (the two masters) dropped it from consideration.
Naturalness here means how easily the conclusion is recognized without additional mental effort.
4
Apply the rule of two particular premises
No syllogism in any figure can be valid if both premises are particular (e.g., 'Some A is B, some B is C'). Such premises do not guarantee a necessary conclusion.
This rule applies to all figures; it is a universal condition for categorical syllogisms.
5
Understand reduction between figures
Figures can be reduced to one another through conversion (aks) of one premise. This shows the primacy of the first figure, as other figures are considered less natural and need conversion to reveal their validity.
Reduction is a key technique in formal logic to test validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحجة الْأَوَّلُ ، لِأَنَّهُ عَلَى نَظْمِ طَبِيعِيٌّ) ، وبديهي الإنتاج، فهو الأول بالنسبة إلى باقي الأشكال؛ لتوقفها عليه، (أَوْ) الأوسط (مَحْمُولُهُمَا فَالثَّانِي)، أي فالشكل الثاني، وَهُوَ أَقْرَبُ مِنَ الْأَوَّلِ في ظهور الإنتاج، (حَتَّى ادَّعَى بَعْضُهُمْ أَنَّهُ بَيَّنُ) إنتاجا (۱) ، (أَوْ) الأوسط (مَوْضُوعُهُمَا فَالثَّالِثُ ، أي فالشكل الثالث ، (أو عَكْسُ الْأَوَّلِ)، بأن يكون الأوسط موضوعا في الصغرى محمولا في الكبرى (فَالرَّابِعُ) ، أي فالشكل الرابع ، (وَهُوَ أَبْعَدُ جِدًّا عن الطبع، بحيث لا يعلم نتيجته إلا بتكلف شديد ، حَتَّى أَسْقَطَهُ الشَّيْخَانِ عَن الاعْتِبَارِ . وَكُلُّ شَكْلٍ يَرْتَدُّ إِلَى الْآخَرِ بِعَكْسِ مَا تَخَالَفَا فِيهِ وَلَا قِيَاسَ [ مُطْلَقًا ] (٥) أي شكل كان (مِنْ جُزْئِيَّتَيْن ).
Chapter on the syllogism. The first [figure]: because it is in natural order and self-evident in production [of conclusion], so it is first relative to the remaining figures, because they depend on it. Or the middle [term] is predicate in both: the second figure. He [the author] said: 'It is closer than the first in apparent production, until some claimed that it is evident in production.' Or the middle is subject in both: the third figure. Or the converse of the first: such that the middle is subject in the minor and predicate in the major: the fourth figure, which is very far from nature, such that its conclusion is not known except with great strain, so much so that the two masters (al-Shaykhan) dropped it from consideration. And every figure reduces to another by conversion of that in which they differ. And there is no syllogism at all, in any figure, from two particular [premises].
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage classifies the four figures of the categorical syllogism based on the position of the middle term. The first figure (middle term predicate in minor, subject in major) is the most natural and self-evident, like arguing: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' The second figure (middle term predicate in both) is closer in apparent validity; some even claimed it is evident. The third figure (middle term subject in both) is less natural. The fourth figure (middle term subject in minor, predicate in major) is so unnatural that Avicenna and al-Farabi (the 'two masters') rejected it. However, figures can be reduced to one another through conversion (aks), e.g., converting one premise changes the figure. Finally, a valid syllogism cannot have two particular premises (e.g., 'Some A is B, some B is C' yields no necessary conclusion). This condition is similar to the rule in fiqh that a proof cannot be built on purely probable premises.

Key Terms
الشكل الأول
الشكل الثاني
الشكل الثالث
الشكل الرابع
الشيخان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the two masters (al-Shaykhan) drop the fourth figure from consideration?
A) Because it is invalid in all moods.
B) Because its conclusion is not known except with great strain, being far from natural reasoning.
C) Because it has no practical applications.
D) Because it can be reduced to the first figure.

Key Takeaway
The first figure is the most natural and self-evident; other figures are ranked by their distance from it, and the fourth is so unnatural that leading Islamic logicians rejected it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Four Figures of the Syllogism and Their Naturalness
Author explains the order of the four figures and their relative naturalness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four figures of the categorical syllogism based on the position of the middle term and rank them by naturalness.

Core Idea
The first figure is natural and self-evident; the fourth is so unnatural it was abandoned.

The author presents the four figures of the categorical syllogism, each defined by the position of the middle term. He ranks them by how naturally they produce a conclusion, from the intuitive first figure to the forced fourth figure. Understanding this ranking helps you evaluate the strength and validity of syllogistic arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion for figures
The figure of a syllogism is determined by the position of the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) relative to the minor term (subject of conclusion) and major term (predicate of conclusion). In the author's description, note how the middle term's role changes.
2
Distinguish the four figures
First figure: middle term is subject in major, predicate in minor. Second figure: middle term is predicate in both. Third figure: middle term is subject in both. Fourth figure: middle term is subject in minor, predicate in major (reverse of first).
Memorize these positions as they determine the figure.
3
Rank the figures by naturalness
The author ranks them: first is most natural and self-evident; second is close to first; third is next; fourth is very unnatural, requiring great effort, and was dropped by al-Farabi and Ibn Sina.
Naturalness (tab') refers to how intuitively the conclusion follows.
4
Understand reduction between figures
The author states that each figure can be reduced to another by converting the premise that differs. For example, the first figure can be reduced to the second by converting the major premise. This shows the interconnectedness of the figures.
Reduction is a core technique in classical logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحجة: الأول، لأنه على نظم طبيعي، وبديهي الإنتاج، فهو الأول بالنسبة إلى باقي الأشكال؛ لتوقفها عليه. (أو) الأوسط محمولهما فالثاني، أي فالشكل الثاني، وهو أقرب من الأول في ظهور الإنتاج، (حتى ادعى بعضهم أنه بين) إنتاجا. (أو) الأوسط موضوعهما فالثالث، أي فالشكل الثالث. (أو عكس الأول)، بأن يكون الأوسط موضوعا في الصغرى محمولا في الكبرى (فالرابع)، أي فالشكل الرابع، وهو أبعد جدا عن الطبع، بحيث لا يعلم نتيجته إلا بتكلف شديد، حتى أسقطه الشيخان عن الاعتبار. وكل شكل يرتد إلى الآخر بعكس ما تخالفا فيه.
Chapter on the proof. The first, because it is upon a natural arrangement, and self-evident in production, so it is first relative to the remaining figures because they depend on it. (Or) the middle term is predicate of both, then the second, i.e., the second figure, and it is closer than the first in the manifestation of production, so much so that some claimed that it is evident in production. (Or) the middle term is subject of both, then the third, i.e., the third figure. (Or) the reverse of the first, such that the middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major, then the fourth, i.e., the fourth figure, and it is very far from nature, so that its conclusion is not known except with great difficulty, so much so that the two masters dropped it from consideration. And every figure reduces to another by conversion of that in which they differ.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a classification and ranking of the four figures of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by stating that the first figure is natural and self-evident, and the other figures depend on it. Then he describes the second figure, where the middle term is the predicate of both premises, and notes that it is closer to the first in manifesting production. Some even claimed it is self-evident. Then the third figure, where the middle term is the subject of both premises. Finally, the fourth figure, which is the reverse of the first (middle term subject in minor, predicate in major), is very unnatural and difficult, so much so that al-Farabi and Ibn Sina disregarded it. The passage ends with the principle that each figure can be reduced to another by conversion of what differs between them. For example, the first figure can be reduced to the second by converting the major premise.

شكل
إنتاج
طبع

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Three Essential Rules for Valid Syllogisms
Author states three rules for valid syllogisms: no two particulars, no two negatives, conclusion follows the inferior premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify violations of the three fundamental rules of categorical syllogisms: two particulars, two negatives, and the inferior premise rule.

Core Idea
No valid syllogism can have two particular or two negative premises; the conclusion always follows the weaker premise.

The author here condenses three critical conditions that any categorical syllogism must satisfy to be valid. These rules help us quickly reject many invalid arguments without analyzing figures and moods. Understanding them is essential for constructing sound proofs in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rules
Locate the three conditions stated in the passage: no two particulars, no two negatives, and the conclusion follows the inferior premise.
2
Understand why two particulars fail
With two particular premises, the middle term may not be distributed, so no conclusion follows. For example, 'Some animals are horses' and 'Some creatures are animals' yields no necessary link because 'animals' is not distributed.
A particular premise does not distribute its subject or predicate.
3
Understand why two negatives fail
Two negative premises cannot produce a valid conclusion because they exclude rather than connect terms. Example: 'No stone is animal' and 'No animal is immortal' gives no relation between stone and immortal.
Two negatives create no positive link.
4
Apply the inferior premise rule
The conclusion takes the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the weaker premise. If one premise is particular, the conclusion is particular; if one is negative, the conclusion is negative.
The weaker premise is the one that is particular or negative.
5
Test a syllogism against these rules
Given a categorical syllogism, check each rule: are both premises particular? Are both negative? Does the conclusion match the inferior premise? If any rule is violated, the syllogism is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا قِيَاسَ مُطْلَقًا (أي شكل كان) مِنْ جُزْئِيَّتَيْنِ وَلَا سَالِبَتَيْنِ، وَالنَّتِيجَةُ تَتْبَعُ أَخَسَّ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ.
And there is no syllogism absolutely (in whatever figure) from two particular [premises] nor from two negative [premises], and the conclusion follows the inferior of the two premises.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage states three fundamental rules for valid categorical syllogisms. First, a syllogism cannot be composed of two particular premises (whether affirmative or negative). Second, it cannot be composed of two negative premises. Third, the conclusion takes on the quality of the weaker premise: if one premise is particular, the conclusion is particular; if one is negative, the conclusion is negative. These rules stem from the requirement that the middle term must be distributed at least once. For example, from 'Some animals are horses' and 'Some creatures are animals' no conclusion follows because the middle term 'animals' is not distributed. Similarly, from 'No stone is animal' and 'No animal is immortal' no conclusion follows because both are negative. And if one premise is universal and the other particular, the conclusion must be particular.

Key Terms
جزئيتين
سالبين
أخس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following combinations of premises would produce a valid syllogism according to the rules in the passage?
A) Two particular affirmatives
B) Two universal negatives
C) One universal affirmative and one particular affirmative
D) One particular affirmative and one particular negative

Key Takeaway
A valid categorical syllogism must have at least one universal premise, at least one affirmative premise, and the conclusion must match the weaker premise in quantity and quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the premises 'No human is immortal' and 'Some philosophers are not human', check the three rules: are there two negatives? No (only one negative). Are there two particulars? No (one universal negative, one particular negative). Then what conclusion, if any, follows according to the inferior premise rule? Explain your reasoning.
?
Construct a valid categorical syllogism about zakat (obligatory charity) using one universal premise and one particular premise, ensuring the conclusion follows the inferior premise.
